The Red Page
Making an Army
The reader who wants to know all that
happens to a recruit who enlists in Kit-
chener’s Army, from the first “Squad—-
’shun!” to the finished product in the
charge, wall not miss The First Hundred
Thousand by lan Hay (Blackwood, per
Dymock’s Library). The trouble about this
volume is that there must be few who
haven't dipped into the story in Black-
wood’s Magazine, and found themselves
-wishing for more. lan Hay cannot help
being interesting, and he adds to a delight-
ful style an equally delightful humor. The
battalion —a Highland one —was the first
of the Service Battalions that was destined
to take its place alongside the Old Regiment
in the firing-line, and lan Hay takes his
reader, like a recruit, through all the details
of the arduous training, with many a witty
criticism of that dignified entity, the British
Army, and shows us the result being tri-
umphantly' tried out under fire. By the end
of the first chapter the reader is introduced
to a number of officers and men w r ith whom
he soon becomes on the most intimate and
friendly terms. And these friends are well
-worth making. To us, who nobly choose to
stay in mufti, this book brings a sense of
the making of an army out of a mob with-
out the possible discomfort of actually taking
part in that operation. And for those who
mean to enlist there is no other book on
the war, even including that popular,
but solemn, literary classic. Infantry Train-
ing, 1914, which will give them such invalu-
able sidelights on their job. The author of
that latter work. General Staff, whose ad-
dress is “War Office.” ought to prescribe
The First 100.000 as a companion manual
for every member of an officer’s class.
J*
lan Hay remarks that “the Great War
has been terribly hard on the text-books.”
Thus the text-book, written by General
Staff, before that invaluable officer met the
Hun, told you to put your trenches with a
good field of fire, and when not entrenched
advised you to take advantage of natural
cover, like farm buildings, etc., and insisted
on recruits being given practice in firing at
long range. But General Staff came back
from the Front with some revises in his
proofs. The amended version is: Never to
place your trenches where you can see the
enemy a long way off. If you do, he will
see you and shell you out of them. You
need not be afraid of being rushed : a field
of fire of 200 yards will be sufficient to wipe
him off the face of the earth. Never take
cover in farm buildings, etc., or any place
likely to be marked on a large-scale map.
Their position and range are known to a
yard. Your safest place is the middle of an
open plain or ploughed field. In musketry,
concentrate all your energies on taking care
of your rifle and practising “rapid.” You
will seldom have to fire over a greater dis-
tance than 200 yards. “When we go out on
service only three things will count —to
march; to dig ; and to fire, on occasion, 15
rounds a minute.”
It is pleasant to learn that in England
they have their Liverpool camps, too. Mud-
splosh Camp “consists of 300 wooden huts,
in all stages of construction. .. .The huts
are heated with stoves, and will be delight-
fully warm when we get some coal. They
are lit —or rather wired for —electric light.
There are numerous other adjuncts to our
comfort—wash-houses, for instance. These
will be invaluable, when the water is laid
on.... There is a spacious dining-hall; and
as soon as the roof is cm, our successors, or
their successors, will make merry therein.
Meanwhile, there are worse places to eat
one’s dinner than the floor —the mud out-
side, for instance.”
The camp has its steriliser, working over-
time. It does not sterilise anything but
blankets. “Yes, quite so. When blankets
are passed from regiment to regiment, in a
camp where opportunities for ablution are
not lavish, these little things will happen.’
As Private Hogg discovered, the steriliser
does its best, but that is all. Examining his
blankets after the operation he remarked,
on observing the presence of his familiar
friends still in the blankets, “Aye; but they
must have gotten an awfu’ fricht!”
The effect of military training on the
recruit is aptly likened to the experience of
the rivets in Kipling’s story of The Ship
that Found Herself. “He no longer gets
drunk from habit. When he does so now, it
is because there were no potatoes at dinner, or
because there has been an unattended leak
in the roof of his hut for a week, or because
his wife is not receiving her separation allow-
ance. Being an inarticulate person, he finds
getting drunk the simplest and most effective
expedient for acquainting the powers that be
with the fact that he has a grievance. For-
merly, the morning list of ‘drunks’ merely
reflected the nearness or remoteness of pay-
day. Now, it is a most reliable and invalu-
able barometer of the regimental atmos-
phere.”
Jt
So, from “Blank Cartridges”—the long
period of make-believe, the author goes to
“Live Rounds” —the real thing, and reminds
us at the conclusion of the first part that
the forthcoming of the second part will de-
pend upon the author’s eye for cover.
(Luckily it was good.) One of the experi-
ences of the company is the digging of a
redoubt at night. The men are warned,
when getting the earth, not to dig deep. They
soon find the reason. The place is full of
little wooden crosses.
“ ‘Germans,’ said the Engineer.
“‘How long have they been —here?’
“ ‘Since October.’
“ ‘So I should imagine,’ said Blaikie, with
feeling. ,
“ ‘The crosses aren’t much guide, either.’
continued the Engineer. ‘The deceased
are simply all over the place. The best
plan is to dig until you come to a blanke
There are usually two or three to a blanket.’
“To-day, out here, bombs are absolute
dernier cri. We speak about rifles anc
bayonets as if they were so many bows ant
arrows.... In the hand-to-hand butcher
which calls itself war to-day, th
rifle is rapidly becoming demode. For lon
ranges you require machine-guns; for shor
bombs and hand-grenades The bomb offi
cer, hitherto a despised underling, has sue
denly - attained a position of dazzling em
nence....ln fact, he threatens to disput
the. pre-eminence of that other regimenta
parvenu, the Machine-Gun Officer.”
One curious result of this interminabl
trench warfare is the existence, at certai
hours of the night, of an informal truce
founded on the principle of live and let live
“It would be an easy business to wipe ou
that working party over there by the barbed
wire with a machine-gun. It would b
child's, play to shell the road behind th
enemy’s trenches, crowded as it must b
with ration-waggons and water-carts, into
blood-stained wilderness. But so long a
each side confines itself to purely defensiv
and recuperative work, there is little or no
interference... .After ail, if you proven
your enemy from drawing his rations, h
will prevent you from drawing yours. Then
both parties will have to fight on empty
stomachs, and neither of them, tactically
will be a penny the better.”
The attitude of the men at the front to the
cold-feet brigade at home is neatly illustrate!
from three points of view. One is that of
Captain Petitpois (which, of course, isn’t his
real name), a French liaison officer. He is
concerned about the attitude of the British
Government. His opinion is that the Cabinet
—“these assassins—these imbeciles—these
cretins” —should be shot. He is calmed down
only by the British officer’s explanation tha>
the Government and the Opposition are
merely a troop of comedians, playing their
old game of politics to keep the country
amused. The second point of view is thai
of Captain Wagstaffe, just returned from
short-leave in London. “The dear old coun-
try at large is its dear old self, as usual. It
is not worrying one jot about Conscription
or us, or anything like that. The one topic
of conversation at present is—Charlie Chap-
lin.”
“ ‘Who is Charlie Chaplin?’ inquired sev-
eral voices.
“Wagstaffe shook his head. ‘I haven’t the
faintest idea,’ he said. ‘All I know is that
you can’t go anywhere in London without
running up against him. The mention of
his name in a revue is greeted with thunders
of applause’....”
And the third comment is made by Pri-
vate Mucklewame, when he heard of the
Welsh coal-strike. “All the decent lads are
oot here.”
Is not that true of the strikers in Aus-
tralia whose demands hold up the manufac-
ture of munitions? It is not the Labor
party that is striking: all the decent lads of
the Labor party ate doing their duty at the
Front. It is the dregs of the Labor party,
the old,, the possessors of cold feet, who are
pretending to represent the depleted Labor
party here—by striking against the interests
of the decent element.
The book closes with a stirring and graphic
narrative of “The battle of the slag-heaps,”
when, at incredible sacrifices of life, The
Big Push was made at Loos.
It may be unpatriotic for this page to re-
mark that. The Queen’s Gift Book, pub-
lished in aid of Queen Mary’s Convalescent
Auxiliary Hospitals (Hodder and Stough-
ton ; 2s. 6d.), is, in art, literature and ap-
pearance, inferior to Melba’s Gift Book, pub-
lished in Australia; but patriotism hardly
extends to literary criticism. Of the 25 items
no. less than six are things that have been
printed before; and though names like Con-
rad, Barrie and Galsworthy appear, the con-
tributions from these authors are not likely
to appear in any collected edition of their
works. Hall Caine’s verses on “Woman” are
just the sort of verses one would expect Hall
Caine to write. Conan Doyle puts into verse
form, under the title “Ypres,” prosaic state-
ments such as these: “You held council at
your quarters, when the budding Alexanders
and the pickel-haubed Caesars gave their
reasons. Was there one amongst that bristle-
headed circle of commanders ever ventured
the opinion that a little town of Flanders
would hold you pounded here through all the
seasons!” The stories are ink and water,
and the illustrations, with few exceptions,
are of the colored Christmas-card variety of
popular art. But, of course, it’s all for a
good cause.
on‘/?/££l me r ty ” : Red Pa 9 e mentions (B.
20/1/16) that R. C. Mills got his D.Sc. in
London for a thesis on Australian colonisa-
tion. This leads me to say that Australian
Universities might do a lot more than they
are doing to encourage research work on
our history. There is an immense amount
of raw material to be worked up. I
know an underground dungeon in Hobart
choked with old State documents and records
ot every kind. Here there await unearthing
some of the most romantic and thrilling
chapters of our past. The same thing is
more or less true of records preserved in
every Australian capital. The sort of work
that can be done is shown bv Scott’s Terrb
Napoleon or Miss Phillip’s A. Colonial Auto-
nomy. It is about time that our own Uni-
versities offered scholarships and research
degrees for work on our own history. These
would be worth far more to us than prizes
for an accurate knowledge of the orations of
Demosthenes or the peculiarities of some
mediaeval despot.
A New Moon
J. Scouller, who is also his own publisher
(27 Salisbury-road, Stanmore, Sydney),
not only solves the large question, Life;
What and Whence Is It? but provides for
the Hun after the war. He is rather unkind
to the previous philosophers and scientists
who have attempted to define Life, because
none of them has hit upon the fact that
“organic evolution can only be explained by
assuming the existence of persistent psychi-
cal entities, clothed in material forms....
Only by adopting the theory of metem-
psychosis, or the transmigration of souls, is
it possible to explain the facts of organic
evolution.” Life, on this earth, depends
upon the sun ; but it arrived here in a much
more dramatic manner than we have hither-
to foolishly believed. J. Scouller sends us
the news direct from the front. It appears
that many millions of centuries ago war
broke out amongst the inhabitants of the
sun —for J. Scouller believes that beneath the
photosphere, which is all we can see of the
sun, there exists a solid terraqueous globe
inhabited by beings rather like J. Scouller,
and on the authority of Dr. Elliott he attri-
butes to the sun “the most delightful
scenery” and much more regular seasons
than we have here. And in the sun “a great
potentate (Satan), with his vaulting am-
bition, sought to have pre-eminence over the
powers and principalities which represented
true progress.” But he was defeated, and
“the continent which had been defiled by
him and his hosts was separated off from
the matter of the sun by means of volcanic
agency and sent whirling off into space in
the form of the planet which we now in-
habit.” Naturally from such beings those
monsters of the prime, with which Tennyson
has so long worried us, were spawned.
But Life is built up by the germ cell, and
the reproductive cells act in some respect
like intelligent beings. Now how did the re-
productive cell learn to do its work? “The
psychic entity represented by the germ-cell
must have derived its capacity as a result of
experience gained during a long succession
of former lives in connection with organisms
of a human or sub-human character.” Thus
each gamete, or reproductive cell, represents
a separate and distinct psychic entity capa-
ble of building up and controlling an organ-
ism similar to that of the parent from which
it was derived. Hence man is not a simple,
indivisible soul clothed with a material body,
but a composite being—a cellular commu-
nity—in which vast numbers of subsidiary-
lives contribute to the one communal life.
Thus man has no consciousness of any pre-
vious existence, for the consciousness of the
organism is not that of individual psychic
entities, but of a new collective community.
Death merely means the temporary dispersal
of these individual entities, which continue
to exist, because they have always existed,
and will ultimately form part of a new
human life.
However, this speculation is hardly as in-
teresting as the future of the Hun. This war
is the prelude to God's Kingdom on the
earth. But the Hun has shown himself quite
unfitted to inhabit a regenerated earth. “We
may be quite certain that Nature will see to
it that the enemy.kingdoms will have no part
or lot in the coming Kingdom of Heaven on
earth.... We can readily imagine that the
countries of Central Europe, as'well as parts
or Asia Minor, will be separated off the
earth, as the result of seismic activity, and
that these separated portions of the earth
will be cast off into space, where they will
constitute a new satellite which will' circle
round the earth as the moon now does.” J
Scouller argues from the scientific hypo-
thesis that the present moon was
thrown off from the earth, but he
neglects the fact that this happened
by tidal action when the planet was much
hotter. The Pacific Ocean is believed to
represent the large hole made when that con-
tinent swung off. _ Anyhow, when Central
Europe is hoisted into the firmament, it will
have gained a place in the sun, and the
Kaiser ought to be satisfied. But it would
be just like him to set about throwing lunar
Alps upon us every moonlit night.

Irish Love-Poems
What is Love?
A love all-commanding, all withstandinn
Through a year is my love •
A grief darkly hiding, starkly Udina
Without let or remove ■ ®
Of strength a sharp straining, past sustain.
Wheresoever I rove,
A force still extending without ending
Before and around and above.
Jf ea ui €n , ’ tis the brightest amazement
The blackest abasement of Hell ”
A struggle for breath with a spectre
In nectar a choking to death • ’
’ TiS “tkZIT™ Hemn ’ S «
Then Champion Feats under M o vlr< a
water; «
’Tis pursuing the cookoo, the wooing
Of Hclio, the Rock’s airy daughter.
Till my red lips turn ashen,
My light limbs grow leaden,
My heart loses motion,
In Death my eyes deaden,
So is my love and my passion,
So is my ceaseless devotion
To her to whom I gave them,
To her who will not have them.
—From the Early Irish.
Cuttin’ Rushes.
Oh, maybe t it was yesterday, or fifty yean
Meself was visin’ early on a day for cuttin’
rushes,
Walkin’ up the Brabla’ burn, till the sun was
low,
Now I’d hear the burn run an’ then I’d
hear the thrushes.
Young, still young! — an’ drenchin’ wet the
grass,
Wet the golden honeysuckle hanqin’
sweetly down;
Here, lad, here! Will ye follow where I
pass,
An’ find me cuttin ’ rushes on the moun-
tain?
Then was it only yesterday, or fifty years
or so?
Rxppin round the bog pools high among
the heather,
The hook it made me hand sore, I had to
leave it go,
I teas he that cut the rushes then for me
to bind together.
Come, dear, come! — an’ back along the burn
See the darlin’ honeysuckle hangm’ like
a crown.
Quick, one kiss — sure, there’s someone at
the turn!
“Oh, we’re after cuttin’ rushes on the
mountain .”
Yesterday, yesterday, or fifty years ag0....
I waken out o’- dreams when I hear the
summer thrushes.
Oh, that’s the Brabla burn, I can hear it
sing an’ flow,
For all that’s fair, I’d sooner see a bunch
o’ green rushes.
Run, burn, run! can ye mind when we were
young?
The honeysuckle hangs above, the pool is
dark an’ brown;
Sing, burn, sing! can ye mind the song ye
sung
The day we cut the rushes on the moun-
tain?
Moira O’Neill.
To Morfydd.
A voice on the winds,
A voice on the waters,
Wanders and cries:
Oh, what are the winds?
And what are the waters?
Mine arc your eyes.
Western the winds are,
And western the icaters,
Where the light lies:
Oh! what are the winds?
And what are the waters?
Mine are your eyes.
Cold, cold grow the winds,
And dark grow the waters,
Where the sun dies.
Oh! what are the winds?
And what are the waters?
Mine are your eyes.
And down the night icinds,
And down the night icaters
The music flies:
Oh! what are the winds?
And what are the waters?
Cold be the winds,
And wild be the waters,
So mine be your eyes.
Lionel Johnson.
From “The Book of Irish Poetry,” edited
by Alfred Perceval Graves (Every Irishman’s
Library, Unwin.)

Henry Fletcher writes: —Red Page
(10/2/’l6) states: “The author of The
Waybacks books had very little to do with
the dramatisation.” This is a serious error.
I wrote every word of the play, and re-
arranged and rewrote to meet the wishes of
the producer ; for this I had extra pay and
royalty. But, as with a former successful
play of mine, there was no clause in agree-
ment that my name must be announced as
the writer.

still. We call them “Disease Germs,” and they also

intestines, fever with its hallucinations, or biliousness with
its aches and pains, is the result.

Australia Riddled with
Deadly Kidney Disease
ALMOST EVERTfBODY COMPLAINING.
Kidney disease is most certainly on the in-
crease. Thousands and thousands of people
in the towns and villages of this country are
in terrible danger of deadly kidney disease.
This insidious disease creeps on, gradu-
ally getting a firmer and firmer grip, until
when it is too late the poor sufferer discovers
he has Bright’s Disease. Have you kidney
trouble? Do you know how to recognise
whether you are suffering from this or not?
Here are some of the symptoms of kidney
disease : Aches and pains in various parts of
the body, nasty taste in the mouth first thing
in the morning, constipation, cloudy and bad-
colored urine, general feeling of weakness
and irritability, puffiness under the eyes—
these are all symptoms of kidney trouble.
And then people who suffer from backache,
rheumatism, gout, sciatica, gravel, stone or
pains in the bladder, muscles and joints are
all suffering these pains through weak or
diseased kidneys, for these are the sure and
certain symptoms of kidney trouble.
All these things mean that the kidneys are
weak and diseased. They need strengthening
and making healthy. *

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Moonlight March.
Far in the vale of the hollow moon,
Where the laughter lives upon leaf and tree,
Where the stars drop kisses and soft clouds
croon
A low wind-song to the purple sea,
It was there they came in their thousands
strong,
An army moving with measured tread,
The men who followed the drums of song
Through the conquering march of the ages
dead.
Breastplate of priest and sceptre of king,
Sword and gauntlet and trumpet blare,
Prow of ship to the east a-wing,
Lure of love and a woman’s hair,
Chatter of slaves in the market place,
Merchant clamor of credits gone—
All are a broken sandal lace -
To the breath of the singer that marches on.
Forty deep through the woods they came;
Forty sweet was their front of sound—
Orpheus wrapped in a robe of flame,
Sappho with crimson girdle bound,
Tall Musseus and Homer blind—
Bravely they marched in the moon-vale
white;
The stars blew kisses and night was kind
And the dream-sea sighed with its old
delight.
“Halt! Break off!” ’Twas a voice I
knew —•
Voice of a king by a right divine.
Laughter out of the branches blew;
White nymphs ran with their cakes and
wine.
Picnic under the hollow moon —-
Lone I stood in the shadowed throng;
And the soft clouds passed with their ancient
croon
Over the kings and queens of song.
Lone I stood ; and the gross life mine
Beat to flame in ray pulses red ;
But my lips of flesh could drink their wine;
I knew their speech and the words they
said.
For they were as we. and the world went by;
We are as they, and the lots are drawn ;
Schoolman and shopman haste to die,
And the man is dust, but the dream goes on.
Forty deep through the woods they go ;
Forty sweet is their front of sounds
New recruits in the hindmost row
Join in the march the white world round.
Chatter of slaves in the market place,
Merchant clamor of credits gone—•
All are a broken sandal lace
To the joy of the singer that marches on.
N.s.w.
D. M. W.
The Big Grey Ship.
Grey of the dawn and seagulls screaming,
Southern breezes tossing the foam,
Rising sun on the grey gums gleaming,
House on the hillside, someone’s home!
Soldier! Lord of the Merry Laughter!
Heart-courageous, steadfast, true,
Here’s “To-morrow and what comes after !”
Good-bye to the Big Grey Ship and You !
Grey, grey mist o’er the harbor falling,
Soft, mysterious, strangely sad,
Wheeling gulls from the gardens calling.
Where is my merry, brown soldier lad?
Dreams of kisses and last embraces,
All must go, there is work to do.
Here’s to the Girls with Smiling Faces
Who wave to the Big Grey Ship and You !
Grey of the evening. Night descending,
Stars obscured by a gentle rain,
Golden curls over knitting bending.
Weaving thoughts into purl and plain !
“Slip one, knit one, decrease to seven’’ —
God of the English, kind and true,
All that is good on Earth, in Heaven,
Watch over the Big Grey Ship and You !
n'.s.w.
ISOBEL H. RENTOUL.
Dreams.
There is no sadness in the dreams
I keep of you, >
But warm, sweet bushlands stage then-
themes,
And skies of blue ;
For yours, of all the hearts I know,
I knew was true!
Love of my love’s first spring; How bare
Earth sometimes seems !
Where is the joy we used to share
In hills and streams?
The sweet, fresh wonder that survives
But in my dreams!
Within a world that leaves its trace
On all who stay—
Fades freshness from both heart and face —■
I still delay ;
But Death forever shrines in you
Your girlhood’s day!
Though all are dead that shared the years
We knew of old,
Dead with the change that fair youth sears,
Turns warm hearts cold,
My heart still has its dreams of you—•
Dreams of pure gold !
You lie not where they laid you. I,
Closing my eyes.
Walk in the lands of memory,
’Neath wonder skies,
With you whose eyes are clear with that soft
speech
That knows no lies ! -
While my heart lives you live; and deep,
When death’s last due
Is paid, together we shall sleep.
The future’s view
Death bounds; yet all the dark is Heaven
Because of you!
N.S.W.
HAMER.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 6s. Cd. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas Number, post paid.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to the

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
Tiie Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. Tiie Bulletin desires, further, those

humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,

but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 24, 1916.
THE GREASE TRAP AT THE WRONG
END.
In the midst of strikes, patriotic speeches,
rigmaroles about the bountiful harvest and
the vast amount of money in the country,
and Bill Higgs sitting down on gold ex-
ports, in case all the cash escapes to achieve
cigars and asparagus, Victoria, and inci-
dentally Australia, has been hearing some-
thing lately about the elusive job, The hole in
the cashbox and the p.n.; also, as’a side-line,
about the job that vanishes down the drain,
and the export of flirt and presenting the
foreigner with a greasy substance to anoint
himself and convert into dollars. The'frith
is beginning to disappear from the beer, and
because, among other things, Coiien is not
at the old address, and the war is mopping
up cash like a thirsty camel accumulating
water at the back of Bourke, and because
the expedient of drugging the community
wdth calamitous palliatives has been an
easy method of escape from work and worry
to a horde of successive politicians, Peacock
is short of doles and a lot of people are
going short of toil. And this in a State
which has expended over £70,000,000 on
“developing” itself, in which there was last
year a State and Federal expenditure of
£23,000,000, and where a million and a half
of people are inhabiting 87,000 square miles
of land (half of them jammed into Mel-
bourne), and have been alternately babbling
about prosperity and howling for a cheap
loaf. All of which are points, parts and im-
portant pieces of the discussion about how it
happens that with thousands of men going
off to the war, with a strike or something
like it breaking out every five minutes, and
the world and Australia yelling for the pro-
duce of land and factory and mine, for
brains and for skill and for brawn, the Vic-
torian should be forming frequent deputa-
tions to a Minister urging him, for Heaven’s
sake, to smoke Cohen out of his entrench-
ments, or raid the banks, or do any old thing
he could think of to get into debt so that the
cashbox might be distended and the public
paysheet remain the same copious document
as before. Incidental to all this is that
other Victorians formed up into a deputation
the other day and asked Tudor what he
thought of Australia sending away, over a
quarter of a million tons of ivool per annum,
of which half was not wool but of wdiich a
fifth of the residue was a very valuable com-
mercial substance, eagerly competed for by
manufacturers in Europe and America, put
to a score of uses, and some of it, either
neat or travelling under various disguises,
was sold back to Australian citizens. This stuff
is called lanolin. Practically the whole
world’s supply of it is captured from the
sheep’s overcoat in England and Europe, and
the capturing is done by work which Aus-
tralians didn’t do. That the value of the
grease is about £7,000,000 a year is only a
detail; so is the quite obvious fact that Aus-
tralians engaged in capturing lanolin instead
of standing aside to let the foreigner head it
off would not, amongst other things, have to
depend on Cohen. Nor would they be helping
swell the disastrous interest bill or load up
the swag for the Australian kid. They
would be doing something that counted in the
way of making the bill and the swag
lighter. They would be producing some-
thing better than debt. The demonstrations
outside Peacock’s office to send for Cohen
and the inquiry addressed to Tudor con-
cerning lanolin represent wholly irreconcil-
able theories about the job and the. day’s
wage and the path Australia is going to
follow. They are in conflict all over the
Commonwealth, and the kernel of it is in the
question whether Australians shall clean the
jumbuck’s covering and card it and mill it,
and annex that lanolin, or wait miserably
at the steps of the pawnshop.
* * * *
The Bulletin is firmly of opinion that
if it were not for the limitations of chemi-
cal knowledge, lack of enterprise among ship-
builders and foreigners and the conservative
instincts of Cocky, the dairy industry in
Australia would still be where the wool and
wheat-growing industries are. Instead of
separating the fat from the fluid delivered in
the bucket by Strawberry, putting the pro-
ceeds up into pats and rolls of butter, mak-
ing cheese, converting the by-products into
sucking-pig and bacon, Australia would
have risen nobly to the occasion and
delivered floods of milk into vast marine
tanks for transport to London and Berlin,
where the contents would have been reduced
to their constituent parts, and some of them
carried back as ballast for feeding Melbourne
and Sydney. A splendid opportunity has
been lost to the foreign-trade brigade, through
absence of imagination —just as jobs are
being lost to Australians and effort wasted
and cash squandered through hanging on to
the weary old gags about development and
p.n.-promoted prosperity and bloat, instead
of realising that wealth has been slipping
through their fingers every year in the ship-
ments of wheat and ore, and wool and dirt
laboriously dragged alongside the steamer
and the windjammer. It is not wheat the
Englishman wants, but flour; not ore, but
refined metal; not bales of grease, but wool
or what is made from wool, and the stuff
that can be skimmed off the mess Australians
didn’t clean up. It is in working up the ore
he has, grinding the wffieat he grows and
utilising the by-products, handling the wool
clip with discernment of its use and possi-
bilities here on the spot instead of over
yonder, that Australians will find the way to
a self-reliance that shall be real and profit-
able.
A country which exports 531.436,8781 b.
of greasy wool (Knibbs’s figures for 1913)
and only 64,450,0861 b. of cleaned wool in a
year has not commenced to be industrially
sane. When it allows a valuable by-product
of the limited volume it docs treat to vanish
down the drainpipe it seems to have
decided on a policy of unexampled stu-
pidity. The waste on freight alone is
stupendous; the loss of employment enor-
mous. And the losses thereabouts only need
to be multiplied by the waste and losses that
are represented by thousands of miles of
land adjacent to railways yet only half
utilised or even actually idle; by the losses
caused through the steady destruction of
forests and the importation of immense
quantities of, foreign timber; by the waste
on minerals; by the pulley-hauling about of
great masses of vegetation and animal pro-
ducts ; by the relatively complete absence
of chemical and technical science from agri-
cultural and manufacturing processes—all
this can be reduced to a simple calculation
that reveals the why and the wherefore of
the beggarly device for staving off the con-
sequences of political failure by pawning
the future. The more money that is bor-
rowed and scattered about building elephant-
houses and flea-breeding emporiums and
superfluous railroads (and neglecting a rail-
road vital to Australia) the more men have
to be continually employed on this class of
work, living at the best from hand to
mouth and more or less nomadic. And
while that is so the opened lands of Australia
are to a great extent vacant places, and
industries which would absorb labor, to the
great advantage of individuals and the
economic strengthening of the nation,
remain—well, that brings wool and dirt and
lanolin into the argument again for pur-
poses of illustration. The foreigner reaps
the benefit of the madness. He gets what
Australia fools away at great expense to
herself; he turns it into sovereigns that are
usually minted in Australian gold—and then
he invests in an Australian debenture, and
Australians go on digging and “developing
their resources” and forgetting all about
lanolin and other things, and the interest
leaks steadily away to be invested in more
debenture and make the leak bigger than
ever before. Of course it is lunacy, and
eoually of course when the consequences
of the national folly to the men who
have earned their meagre living from the
proceeds of pawn being thrown out of a job
it is mighty hard on them. They are the
victims of a gigantic political fraud, prac-
tised by demoralised parties for the imme-
diate advantages of a passing moment. And
it has been a mighty treason to Young Aus-
tralia this demented evasion of economic
truth in an orgy of extravagance and
waste. But the job subsidised by Cohen
has got to stop some time, and so far as
The Bulletin is able to see the sooner it
ends the less emphatically will Young Aus-
tralia have to curse when he takes the lid
off. And as under the combined influence
of Kultur and the rush of the Allies for cash
it looks like reaching an abrupt stop in a
day or two, this paper suggests that to
start with the man in Victoria and else-
where whose job is beginning to wear a
preeari 3 aspect should make lanolin
the t« , of a loud sermon to be
delivers .1 to loan-mongering politicians
who have been aiding and abetting
lanolin and multitudinous other sub-
stances to leave the premises without a pass-
port. Sending out the “S.O.S. signal to
Cohen is likely to be a pathetic circum-
stance. Cohen has been complaining lately
of hardness of hearing, and only the other
day Treasurer Higgs received confidential
advice from London that all Israel might
become stone deaf if the explosion kept up
much longer.
THE SCAPEGOAT.
The greatest modern political convenience is
beer. Wherever it flows there is a moder-
ately decent chance of men becoming unruly
and walking through some of the outer
trimmings of the Ten Commandments, but
the worst rows ever caused by the consump-
tion of the stuff itself are a small circum-
stance to the steady and prolonged uproar
among the alleged moralists who see in it
the root of all evil. That uproar, which
can be calculated upon with something like
mathematical precision, is used by political
parties both for defence and attack. A
party desiring to shield its actions from
observation has merely to set the anti-liquor
agitation going with a full head of steam
and its noise will drown all minor voices and
concentrate the whole public attention on a
single point which in the sum of things
political and social is of altogether minor
importance. On the other hand, a party
desiring to hit its political opponents in a
vital spot sools the anti-liquor agitation on
to them with whoops and curses, and watches
to see them torn to pieces by the infuriated
person with the umbrella. Neither party
believes that the booze question is of
primary importance, or that the enthusiast
for thirst has a bite which is really venom-
ous; but all parties believe that Bung and
his assailant are valuable pawrns in the poli-
tical game, and as such to be used to secure
temporary victory. Amid all the wrangle
and noise and purposely-created confusion
Bung is apt to have a bad deal and the
public a still worse one; and at the present
moment, when the raging discord is reaching
its climax, it is up to that public to step in
and make it clear to the contestants that
their attempts to raise cyclones in wash-
hand basins have no chance of being treated
seriously.
* * * . *
The recent refusal of the Holman Gov-
ernment to make an earlier closing hour for
hotels than 10 o’clock may have been right
or wrong; but the hysterical persons who
see any link between this and the recent
riot in Liverpool and Sydney have a peculiar
sense of logic. The trouble began in the
morning and practically the whole of the
recruits in tw T o large camps had broken from
control while perfectly sober and before
liquor had taken any hand at all in the
game. W. A. Holman is, on the evidence,
not only right in saying that the nine o’clock
closing, which he refused to accept, would
have done nothing to remedy matters, but
might with perfect honesty have gone farther
and declared that if every pub in the County
of Cumberland had been closed, locked and
surrounded by a double guard of the driest
and holiest umbrellas, the outbreak would
have occurred just the same, and might even
have been aggravated by the fact that the
absence of beer was regarded as a just griev-
ance. The fact that the pubs were closed at
six o’clock on the night of the strike is proof
enough that the biggest reduction of boozing
hours suggested by the extreme party in Par-
liament would not have made the smallest
difference to the rioters. The trouble began
in cold blood, in bitter sobriety, and pub
hours have no more right to be blamed for it
than the railway time-table or the width of
Redfern tunnel. If all the beer in
Sydney had been buried in stone vaults
at the moment when the human tornado
struck the city it would have stood a big
chance of being torn from its place of seclu-
sion.
No kind of excuse can be offered for
mutiny; but some attempt to get at the
cause of it may reasonably be made by
persons who refuse to think through their
boots. To start with, the concentration of
enormous bodies of men within a few miles
of a city that looks at life easily and has
religious objections to anything in the way
of serious restraint is a policy which deserves
all the bricks that can be thrown at it. A
new mode of life is never so difficult among
new surroundings as it is when attempted
near the old familiar beat. It is tolerably
safe to say that if the big camps from which
practically all the trouble has come were
50 miles from Sydney there would have been
little enough to curse over; and it is still
more certain that if there had been a dozen
camps instead of two, the pride of each
bunch of trainees in its own share of the
war preparation would have overcome any
desire to make a loud noise or wag its
undisciplined tail in the face of authority.
But to make a camp so large that it has no
individual character of its own, and to
place it at a point where it is practically
a suburb of the city, is to cry aloud
for just the sort of trouble that "arrived
last week. This statement is not new,
nor is its discovery recent. To any-
body except those who had the power to
alter it and didn't it has been quite evident
for months that the military authorities
would never get the best out of the men
offering for service while they herded them
in huge mobs within a shilling train ride of
the place where they were in the life-long
habit of doing any blessed thing they
pleased.
* * * *
As far as the liquor question touches the
troubles of the big camp at all, its connec-
tion is to be found in the absence of the
wet canteen. It is plain horse-sense that
men accustomed to drink beer in their ordi-
nary citizen life will want a mug now and
again while undergoing a strenuous training
Rightly or wrongly, the pub is the sociai
centre of Billjim’s normal existence; and,
in the process of turning him into a soldier,
a soldier pub, which is a wet canteen, is
almost a necessity. With such a canteen
his life will centre in the camp ; without it
ho will spread himself over the whole civi-
lian beerscape and, even if he does not be-
come a nuisance to himself and others, will
be something less of a soldier when he
boards the transport than he easily might
have been made. The wet canteen is a
logical necessity in a State where liquor is
commonly used. If the State were under
a prohibitory law a dry canteen might be
in order; but there can be no sort of advan-
tage in denying to the soldier his own camp
pub when the ordinary citizen still has his
bar open at the street corner. The real
solution of the whole liquor problem lies in
the establishment of a system of public
ownership and control of booze. All the
outstanding evils connected with alcohol can
be traced to the fact that it is in the hands
of Private Enterprise. Yet the Common
wealth military authorities with the oppor-
tunity of nationalising the soldier’s drink
right to their hand fail to take advantage
of it; and a section of the fool public joins
in the cry to shut the pubs because the sol-
diers, having been driven into them by the
folly of those in charge of the military sys-
tem, occasionally get out of hand.
FIVE-BOB-A-DAY SOLDIERING AS
IT APPEALS TO NEBUCHAD-
NEZZAR, PIZARRO AND W
SMITH.
When Hezekiah, King of Judah, showed
his Babylonian visitors the treasures of the
Temple and let them peep for a wistful
moment into his own strong-room he was
sowing the seeds of trouble. The Babylonians
went home with tales that opened the eyes
of the Euphrates natives; and a generation
or two later the legend of Jerusalem’s gold
had grown so big by frequent telling that an
expedition was fitted out to make a raid on
the city and carry off the plunder. When
Pizarro saw a specimen of Inca gold, which
some wandering Indian had contrived to filch
away and carry to Panama, the glint of the
precious thing laid such a hold on the reli-
gious soul of the Spanish adventurer that he
felt bis sword itching in its scabbard to con-
vert the Empire of the Sun to a right regard
for holy things. Jerusalem found its safe
burst open and the Inca was toppled off his
throne because in each case unguarded
wealth had been advertised to a treasure-
hungry world. The moral is that it is a good
thing to hide your cash when in rough com-
pany and say as little as possible about the
wife’s habit of leaving her diamonds lying
around when you happen to be dining with
burglars. All. the same, it is difficult
to hide the marks of prosperity and to keep
up a pretence of poverty on a large scale.
Australia, among the world burglars who
use the 17-inch howitzer instead of the
centrebit, does not make a pretence of feeling
poor. Our five-bob-a-day soldiers walk openly
among the three-and-ninepence-a-week armies
of the world, and in a single day the city of
Sydney raises by subscription a sum equal to
three or four times the annual revenue of
one of the kingdoms engaged in. the big
European scuffle. All the world is taking
note ; and those portions of the world that
are hungriest and most reckless begin to feel
a religious call, like that which came to
Pizarro, to go and have a look at the place
where so much wealth is bred. The lure of
Australia becomes stronger. Vague fancies
about gold stacked under the trees or lying
loose about the doorsteps crystallise into vivid
dreams among the men who are wont to cut
a tough throat for a few coppers or sack a
maddened and brutally-resisting village for a
few* bushels of corn. Five-bob-a-day walks
before them in war-soiled khaki; and as its
thousands go by the enthusiast for plunder
makes his calculations about Australia and
resolves to get his hands into her shining
resources. From an advertising point of
view the whole thing is a perfect success.
* * » *
There can be no sort of doubt that the man
who has been properly impressed by the
vision of five-bob-a-day w T ill get to Australia
in some quantity. What it is important for
us to consider is whether he will come as
Nebuchadnezzar came to Jerusalem, as
Pizarro came to Peru, or as Bill Smith
originally came to the Illawarra. Nebuchad-
nezzar came along with an outsize in armies,
which paid no heed to Judah’s paltry
defences, and without troubling about Hague
conventions or international law helped him-
self to every saleable thing Jerusalem con-
tained. Pizarro arrived in Peru with some
crosses, a small band of devoted brigands
and a huge pretence of friendliness and mis-
sionary zeal; and succeeded in murdering,
robbing and converting to Christianity on a
magnificent scale. Bill Smith came to
Illawarra, bought land, milked a cow and.
despite the pestiferous foreign atmosphere o
the dairy-yard, learned to think Australian.
The arrival of a sufficient number of hi
Smiths may be a protection against the m-
vasion of a Nebuchadnezzar; P ro . t^ ctl y}
against Pizarro must be of a c *’ ,® re
nature. Unless the present war should iA
amazingly wrong and suddenly wheel so"
ward from the sack of Europe, it is to
ably certain that Nebuchadnezzar s at
will not occur immediately; though it
equally certain that the eye of the in
national plunderer must burn upon (® r |
food and mineral resources with a ba
light. Possessing such a country as -
tralia as a base, all the armies y. w
destruction could be fed and equipped hi• P
petuity; and these are the things *
ciiadnezzar now considers as thought .
as he once pondered over the gold and s
vessels in the vaults of Jerusalem,
danger of the larger invasion must 1
when the battle clouds break; but wit
there may yet be time to meet it *
however, is an altogether different P
position. In his modern guise
longer girds on armor and swears j
lago. but decorates himself with ti , a
of a Beef Trust or the barbed tail «
Brimstone Combine. The old I IZA perll-
fair words lured 10.000 unarrne .
vians into the great square o
marca and slaughtered them 0 f
glory of God and the renown
Spain. # • *
The modern adventurer is an( }
simple person, staying at a good n
smoking big cigars, but rounding l


nnhlic for slaughter in the same old way.
His combination of stealth and kindness
fipceives even the expert m commercial rob-
hprv and when Courts and Commissions sit
upon him his lawyers and agents can gener-
ally contrive to prove that he does not exist.
Already Australia has some experience of
hinu but the huge advertisement of our
wealth and resources in the eyes of the
world which the war has made possible, and
The flaunting of a five-bob-a-day military sys-
tem in the faces of the sevenpence-halfpenny
patriots of other lands, will convince a still
more numerous Pizarro that there are Inca
shrines here to roh and Inca gold-mines to
exploit Our advertisement in the world
war thus works in two directions. It
whistles Bill Smith away from his slum or
fishing smack or barren acres to come and
make his home in a country that lies wide and
rich to the sun. and it pom-poms on a trom-
bone to the person who robbed Bill Smith
in other and narrower lands to extend his
field of operations to a realm where bigger
burglary is possible. It is one of our most
pressing jobs to see to it that such burglary
is made impossible. If Pizarro at his first
landing, when his tired, disreputable and pro-
perly-scared followers crawled ashore on the
unknown coast of Peru, had been met by a
regiment or two of the Inca’s best troops
there would have bee.n no slaughter at Cajam-
arca and no overthrowing of that strange,
mild, socialistic despotism by Spanish bar-
barians. It is right at the landing that
Pizarro must be met every time. In one
American city there is no Beef Trust because
the butchers of that place held a meeting,
and by resolution decided to shoot at sight
any person remotely suspected of having any-
thing to do with the big concern. That is
the one effective means of dealing with
Pizarro. Pie must be shot at sight—with a
legislative twelve-inch gun if possible; but
shot, in any case, even if there is nothing
better to fire at him than the garden roller. In
the coming of Bill Smith in quantity to
Australia lies our great hope of defeating the
open attack of Nebuchadnezzar; but we
shall never succeed in that larger task if we
let Pizarro make good his landing and drain
the resources of the country white for his
own gain. About our defence against Nebu-
chadnezzar we can do little till the present
war is over; but if we wait until the guns
cool to stop Pizarro, his grip will be on our
throat and his hand on our cash pocket at
the very moment we are cheering for the vic-
tory his coming has made barren.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Representing Australia.
li’ is a feature of our alleged modern demo-
cracy that everybody who makes sufficient
noise “represents” somebody or something.
Oeorge Reid, presented with a Tory
seat in the House of Commons, talks about
representing Australian interests; yet his
constituents would rise as one infuriated
coat-of-arms and tear him out of his eye-
glass if he even suggested to them one notion
that is honestly and distinctively Austra-
lian. But Reid is in no danger of doing
anything of the kind; although he never
discovered Australia, Australia long ago
round him out. Andrew Fisher is perhaps
a shade more in touch with our ideas ; but
to say that Fisher, oozing porridge and
1 resbyterianism in every syllable, can repre-
sent Australia is to utterly mistake the
position. Others whose claims are even
more mysterious occasionally misuse lan-
guage to declare that they are in some sense
Australia’s representatives. The so-called
* ustrahan colony in London —men who ex-
i 11 t .this continent with sheep or took
oil ot its mineral wealth, but regarded it as
oo poor a place for permanent residence —
ccasmnally step into the limelight as those
? se >deas are expressive of the strong life
rider our own broad sun; and even the
- luoded Carson was proposed recently by
u° n ne wspaper as the head of a party
tch among other minor jobs was to
repre Sen t the Australians.” All such talk
mia ■ u.tha n the remarks of the honorary
f. Ir .*. niS , cat on the house roof. In plain
a democracy w r orthy of the name can
never be represented by a man or a party.
It can be represented only by itself.
Once in the eyes of the world has Aus-
tralia been represented fully and honestly.
It was on the morning at Gaba Tepe when,
set to the task that war authorities deemed
impossible, the men of the Commonwealth
made their historic onslaught with a spirit
that will ring down the endless road of His-
tory. The strenuous, casual, lighthearted,
irreverent voice of this continent was heard
there above the roar of all the guns, and
the world sat up and observed that this was
not. at all the kind of thing that it had been
led to expect from what it had previously
seen of the country’s alleged representatives.
The severe months that followed bore out
all the promise of the landing. War writers
have showered their praises upon the men
who held _ the trenches and almost made suc-
cess possible in spite of ill-devised strategy.
Any number of people are found to-day who
acclaim the fact that the Australian has
proved himself one of the best and most
resourceful fighters on this planet; yet all
the alleged representation of this continent
in the past gave no inkling of the fact; and
only when a section of the nation itself
stepped forward to attend to the job did the
world’s imagination lay hold of the idea that
the Australian people were something quite
out of the ordinary. But if representa-
tion of Australia has been a poor
business abroad it has been equally
farcical at home. From the Federal
Parliament to the municipal council in the
suburbs, the men elected to the jobs of legis-
lation and government are never representa-
tive of the people who give them place and
power. A few dozen conspirators in one
shady corner select a candidate, and a score
of other intriguers in a murky retreat select
somebody else, and the choice at the ballot-
box is between these two. The bulk of the
electors may have no use for either of them;
but have to swallow one or the other. As a
result of this maddening system, men go
into Parliament or one of the lesser cata-
combs representing nobody but the handful
of people who formed the majority upon their
selection committee. They are not repre-
sentative in any fruitful sense, and strangers
may be pardoned if they misunderstand a
nation which persistently wears its hat on its
feet. In actual fact a democracy cannot be
represented except by itself. Australia, as
the mistress of a continent, can employ
certain of her citizens to write her laws,
keep her accounts and carry on the regular
routine of business; but the big things that
really count must come from the people
themselves. The machinery to do this is
neither complicated nor expensive. On the
platform of the Labor party, or hidden
away somewhere in the cracks of its exposed
timbers, is a proposal to establish the Refer-
endum with Initiative. That proposal, if
carried into law, would place the direct
power of doing things in the hands of all
the people. There would be no waiting until
the time was ripe or the potato cooked, but
when Australia desired to make a law that
law would be made swiftly. We need
a Gaba Tepe on Australian soil, and we
can’t get it until the Referendum with
Initiative gives us the word to rush the
trenches and drive out those who block
national progress.
The Army that Came to Town.
The sequence of events at the big Liverpool
(Sydney) training camp had gone this way:
Muddle.
Chaos.
Royal Commission.
More Chaos.
Change of Command.
Men placed on their honor to behave.
Fight, bash, booze.
General Me Cay arrives and makes remarks.
Men assured they must work or be fired.
And it stood at that until the begin-
ning of last week, when the contents
of the new camp at Casula emptied
themselves' into .Liverpool and announced
that they were going on strike against 40
hours of drill per week. Whereupon the
available Liverpoolites said “Good enough”
and likewise went on strike and, after
tearing the innards out of the local
pubs, abandoned the remains and, com-
mandeering the railway service, advanced
on Sydney in thousands. What super-
vened in the city was _ just plain
horseplay, highway robbery, riot, larrup and
annexation of beer, followed by desperate
collisions with multitudinous John Hops,
charges by mounted troopers, and smashed
heads. By night-time the gleam of bayonets
was in the streets, ball cartridge had been
served out to a regiment camped on the Show
Ground, shots had been fired into a mob of
rioters at the Central Railway Station, the
casualties being one killed and eight
wounded. Two thousand men on police and
picket duty were at that stage struggling
with the situation. It was one of the most
amazing outbreaks Australia has experi-
enced. The disgrace—a poor, futile word
in the circumstances —goes a long way beyond
the men directly concerned ip the disturb-
ance. The system under which troops are
raised for service contributed to it. The
throttling of the newspapers and Parliament
had led to damming up conditions which had
to burst. From the start the state of affairs
at Liverpool has been scandalous. Evidence
of this has been daily before the eyes of
Sydney in what was lurching about the
streets and was officially corroborated first
by the findings of Justice Rich and more
recently by the reported utterances of
General McCay. The development has been
steadily in one direction —from bad to worse.
Neither the public nor the recruits were able
at any time .to detect any sign of
official recognition that men were con-
centrated at Liverpool for the stern
and arduous business of preparing for
participation in a desperate war, in
which personal and collective fitness and dis-
cipline are essential to effective service. The
bell wethers of the big rumpus will
probably be rooted out and punished. They
ought to be. But unless Minister for De-
fence Pearce can get himself out of his
habitual frame of mind of being “very
pleased,” knock off carrying on a long-range
squabble with Holman, and apply himself
to the job of tidying up the mess at the
’•ieht nlace with as much thoroughness as he
has displayed in making the censorship an
effective instrument, the better for all hands
except the Hun. An army going on strike is
a fairly bizarre circumstance, but it isn’t
explained by remarking that some anonymous
ruffian suggested raising Cain on the
premises as a patriotic method of helping the
enemy. It more forcibly suggests that some-
one higher up ought to be seriously consid-
ered.
The Somewhat Numerous Watt.
The standards of personal and national
usefulness are getting more mixed than
ever. Watt, M.P., formerly Victorian
Premier, is a case in point. Watt’s posi-
tion has been a • subject of . discussion by
others and earnest consideration by himself
for quite a long time. At the beginning of
things there was a yarn about him having
consulted another politician and been
assured that he was more useful as Watt,
M.P., than as Private Watt or Colonel
Watt or any other Watt behind a gun.
He accepted the verdict, and the matter
stood at that until the other day in Mel-
bourne when at a recruiting rally someone
put it up to him once more. “I have on my
card,” explained Watt, “intimated my
readiness to enlist when I see that by doing
so I will be giving more useful service than
I am doing now.” Watt has an exagge-
rated idea of a talker’s importance. What
he is doing now is to speak with great fervor
to the multitude, frequently telling it . to
wake up, get a move on, realise the situation
and otherwise do the right thing. He is
doing this very well; but there is no pos-
sible argument to justify a man in pre-
ferring to be a seven-horsepower recruiting
agent instead of a one-horsepower soldier.
There are hundreds of elderly men in
Watt’s electorate who can say all he is
saying. There are perhaps thousands of
women who can say it even more effectively,
and unless there happens to be some sound
military reason for Watt not letting them
have the platform while he is measured for
a soldier’s suit he ought to be where he is
telling other men they should be found.
Less than to any other man in a democratic
community is it open to a member of Par-
liament to say, when the life of a nation is
threatened, that his place is on the. stump
rather than in the trench. On him the
obligation of being first in the performance
of civic duty rests heavily, and unless he
discharges it he is merely helping to accu-
mulate evidence of the politician’s social
inferiority. Winning an election entitles
him to a salary, a free pass on the railways
and a seat in Parliament; but there is noth-
ing in the contract, explicit or implied, that
he can thenceforward regard himself . as
exempt from the first and most vital
national duty. There is quite a consider-
able number of Watts in Australia’s mul-
titudinous Parliaments, and the time is
about ripe for them to consider themselves
in relation to their job, their country and
their fellows. From bush, station, mine,
factory, school, office and shop Australians
have gone to fight for freedom. They are
men who are helping to do the work of
humanity. And the humblest of them is a
better Australian, a more useful citizen and
the player of a finer part in the nation’s
business than the frequent Watt of
verbal fluency. The man who thinks that
addressing meetings is as useful as address-
ing a few pointed bayonets to the Hun and
the Turk has got things upside down.

The Hob-nailed Patriot.
In the midst of frenzied appeals to chuck
Germans out of billets and consider methods
to forever smash enemy trade it would not
be amiss to turn slight attention to the fact
that Australia is a rich country that, like
all other countries, isn’t going to get any
benefit out of its riches if its people don’t
work. It may not be altogether unreason-
able to suggest that a man, even if of enemy
extraction, who quietly and conscientiously
keeps on doing the toil he is paid to do, is
not at the present moment such an enemy
to this country as a native-born patriot who
howls for the other man’s removal from his
billet and won’t keep working in his own. In
point of fact, insomuch as the person of
enemy extraction who keeps steadily at his
job is assisting in the creation of British
wealth, he is considerably more of an enemy
to Germany than the native-born person
who frivolously and persistently keeps on
spragging the wheels of production. The
eventual issue of the war; the progress and
prosperity of this country and the Empire;
the chance of Australia rising from the posi-
tion of a huge, helpless, sparsely-settled con-
tinent, only saved from immediate invasion
by the shadow of the British flag, into a
mighty, homogeneous white nation, capable
of dictating within its dominions any policy
ir desires, and of initiating any conditions
(industrial or otherwise) it cares for, and
of upholding and enforcing by its own might
such conditions regardless of conflicting
ideas in the outside world —all these mat-
ters and many more rest ultimately on the
same sound and vigorous development of the
resources of Australia, coupled with those
of the rest of the Empire. Many of them
rest absolutely on Australia’s shoulders, and
if the nation does not cast adrift trivialities
and settle down to urgent business the time
will assuredly come when all the rest of the
Empire will not be able to save it from the
result of its own asininity. Most wage-
earners clearly recognise that a day’s wage
lost is a day’s wage gone, and snort vigor-
ously about broken time; and what must
the verdict be upon a country which has
enormous indebtedness already incurred, and
perhaps incalculable burdens ahead, and
yet, while fortunate enough to escape within
its own boundaries the horrors and devas-
tation which equally innocent nations have
groaned under, fritters away its time in
strikes and industrial deadlocks and poli-
tical pettifogging? No one would deny that
now is a time when every brain should be
active in devising better systems and methods
of production, when every available bit of
brawn and muscle should be put to best use
in turning raw material to valuable pro-
ducts for local or oversea needs : yet what
unprejudiced observer could honestly say
that at present the state of domestic affairs
in Australia showed unmistakable signs of
such a single-minded and patriotic purpose?
Now, if ever, is the time for every citizen,
legislator or commercial magnate or miner
or navvy to recognise and translate into
action the fact that the future of this
country rests primarily on good, honest,
hard graft—not the graft of American
slang, but the decent, self-respecting toil
that the word signifies in the land of the
Southern Cross ; and if even the immediate
personal or financial advantage seems some-
what inadequate, national and individual
needs alike are in the long run going to be
best conserved by mingling a little patience
and common-sense with desire, and trusting
to time and a bit of battling to bring about
a remedy. The principle of attempting to
get a large share of the output by stopping
as much of it as possible is infinitely
inferior to that of increasing it and annex-
ing the surplus.

The Religious Editor’s afternoon sermon :
When I came to the gates of the ill-kept city I
found the walls pasted over with proclamations or
appeals. The first one I saw read “Pay Your
Taxes. Your King and Country Need the Money.”
Close by it was another in different colors: “This
Man Has Paid His Taxes! Have You Paid Yours?”
Further on was a third: “Will You Wear Your
Country’s Receipt?” While I was examining these
curious things an ill-kept, hungry-looking fellow
thrust a bill into my hands. It was apparently
a playhouse advertisement, for it had scrawled across
it, “Theatre Royal To-night; ‘O’Taxpayer, V.C.’
A Thrilling Story of Duty Nobly Done. Performed
by Royal Command before His Majesty. Note:
During the performance the company will sing the
song of the moment —‘Will You Lay Down Your
Cash for the Flag?’ ” Accosting one of the inhabi-
tants I asked: “What means this pasting up of
paper?” After eyeing me narrowly, he answered:
“Stranger, in this free city we are volunteers in
everything. No man, woman or child does or pays
anything by compulsion. We prefer to trust to the
people’s better feelings and to quicken same when
necessary by making a public appeal. All this
paper that you see pasted up is a sign that we are
a little short of money and want the people to know
it. Every taxpayer who nobly comes forward and
pays what he owes gets a medal and his portrait in
the papers, and everybody looks up to him —or down
at him, if the looker happens to be in an upstair
window when the national hero goes by with his
cheering crowd of admirers. There is a play on
now about a man who not only paid his own taxes
in full, but paid them so publicly that all the people
in his street were almost moved to pay theirs too.
You may think perhaps that this is a wasteful and
roundabout method of getting people to do their
duty, but we prefer to have if so. Of course these
frantic appeals cause a good deal of irritation to
the people who don’t pay, %nd I must admit that a
proportion of our national heroes get swelled head
after being thoroughly lionised. A visitor of many
years ago made himself very unpopular by pointing
out that to us a man who merely paid his dues
to the State was made a demi-god, instead of merely
getting a receipt with a cancelled duty-stamp on
it. Then he said something unnecessary about the
demi-god being made to keep .the sponging loafers
who cheered him. I believe he came fremi a wealth-
conscripted country where it was not considered
sensible to have fireworks, bands and cheering round
the Treasury pay-desk. A horribly unemotional hole
I should say. Of course we have our ideals here ;
but we don’t get them shop-worn by forcing them
into everyday use. We believe that every citizen
owes something to the State, and that a lot of the
inhabitants are too dull to realise their duty; but
we will not have them bullied and coerced in the
courts. Our method causes the city to look very
dilapidated at times; but we have the glorious feeling
that Freedom —or something like it —is being fur-
thered and all that sort of thing. If we ever have
a war we intend to proceed on the same glorious
plan of allowing the willing citizen to go out and
get beaten flat while we stay behind and appeal for
more citizens to go out and stop it.” “That seems
very rough on the willing citizen,” said I. “Not at
all, sir!” said my informant; “the stricken hero
will get a medal and a great deal of adulation
and everybody will tell him he is much superior
to all other men.”

A deputation of telegraphists who waited
on Postmaster-General Webster with a
kitful of grievances packed up the Wheat-
stone automatic with the rest and explained
to WebsTer what they thought about it.
The grumble was not that the contrivance
was inefficient nor that it had a habit of
spilling lumps out of a telegram on the
journey, but that it tended to do away with
labor —“threatened to close the avenues of
promotion.” Therefore, said the lightning
jerkers, out with it and get back to the old
hand-worked system. If these telegraphists
had read of a deputation of farm-workers
asking for a return to the scythe-and-sickle
method of acquiring the Australian harvest
they would perhaps have been staggered ; yet
their own petition is parallel. Belonging to
a class of toilers who by the slow grind of
seniority have far to go to rise to managing
a branch office, they naturally enough look
on the new idea with the sour visage, and
this system operating through the service it
is not very surprising that Australia’s
methods are mostly those of last century.
With all its faults the auto, is the only pos-
sible means of coping with telegraph traffic.
More than five years ago it was clear that
hand propulsion was hopelessly inadequate
to grapple with the accumulating business.
Necessity compelled the introduction of the
Wheatstone, and talk of returning to orig-
inal methods is about as sensible as a pro-
posal for ripping up the car lines in Sydney
and Melbourne and goinsr back to the lum-
bering omnibuses.

The Consul-General for Italy in Australia
has notified the Defencq Department that
local unnaturalised Italians are eligible for
enlistment in the Expeditionary forces if
they are of military age and fitness. They
are eligible for service with the army or
navy of Italy, but ' Italy is not taking
measures to get them to the war; so they
are practically in the same position as the
“na uralised” Germans in - our midst who
stay in prosperity and peace in Australia,
whilst men of British and French, descent
are going out to help their compatriots in
the field and save civilisation. Unnatural-
ised neutrals of pro-German sympathies are
also eligible for service on behalf of their
respective Fatherlands, and are therefore
disqualified for enlistment here. So the
longer the war lasts the greater becomes the
proportion of alien residents of military age.
The anomaly needs attention. No able-
The Cruisers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The clear night-skies were splendid,
Their purple lit by stars,
And Ocean’s distance ended
It seemed, on cloudy bars,
Where eastern skies were laden
With radiance rich, that told
That soon the red moon-maiden
Would flood the seas with gold.
Out of the distance dimly
The battle-cruisers grew-—•
Swiftly they came, and grimly,
Black hulls against the blue.
Out of the night emerging.
It seemed, if such might be,
That some sea-god was urging
Their hulls across his sea.
Huge shapes they were, all lightless,
And breathing smoke that trailed
And made the white stars sightless—
Far stars that shrank and paled,
To hear the muffled thunder
Of driven hulls that came —
The great screws churning under.
Flogged like a wind-choked flame.
From dim-lit decks there sounded,
Anon, clear bugle-calls
That thrilled with thoughts unbounded,
By roofs and ringing walls ;
High into Night’s far spaces
Their lonely echoes went
To touch the stars’ white faces
And stir the firmament —
To tell the Night’s dark daughters,
Speaking with quiet lips,
That Britain ruled the w’aters
And these were British shins —
To bid the sea-winds carry
This message far and wide —-
That none shall bite or harry
Where British cruisers ride.
n.s.w.
WILL LAWSON.

The Sudden Arrival of a Bear.
While Everyone was explaining to Every-
body what was going to happen next and
where it was to take place, and who was
going to do it, one of the few big things that
have been pleasant for the Allies occurred
out of earshot. When the echo arrived
Everybody had to turn round in a great
hurry and rush off for an atlas. That it is
a big thing looks pretty certain even if the
usual discount be allowed. In the news
from Flanders at the time there was nothing
to call for applause. The American note
issue had become a whirling tornado of words.
The campaign in Mesopotamia seemed no
better, and Billjim was variously reported
to be pursuing something with a strange
name over the skyline in Tunis, and the rest
of him was allegedly rushing off to Salonica.
In the midst of a tangled nightmare of
dispatches about nothing the Bear was sud-
denly heard bounding off the Caucasus and
springing violently on top of the Turk at
Erzerum, w*hich is in Armenia, and over
beyond the snowy mountains into which the
Unspeakable had dug himself in. Apparently
Abdul was expecting Ivan to laboriously
arrive with siege artillery by the side gate,
but it appears that the Russ silently sneaked
up over the local avalanche and was stream-
ing inside with frozen whiskers and a
bayonet before the son of the Prophet knew
what was happening. The rest of the story
is one of bolting Turk and pursuing Russian
and loud hurrahs from everywhere except
Potsdam. A side-line is what appears to be
a vastly improved state of affairs in the
direction of Bagdad and Suez. It was nearly
time. If the Russian can keep the pressure
going on the Turk and the Anglo-Indian
army gets into effective operation south-
wards the Unspeakable should soon be hav-
ing something of first importance to think
about, and be fairly thankful that Gallipoli
is off his hands. Meanwhile there has been
some mild discussion about the army Aus-
tralia is leisurely raising, and the general
tone of the talk has been that the casual
level is being reached. * Unless such a step is
contrary to the War Precautions Act there
seems fairly conclusive evidence that the
time is ripe for getting more than a casual
move on. The published figures—and they
are only part of the story—are not encourag-
ing.
“Pick”: # When people at Bungendore
(N.S.W.) three years ago formed them-
selves into a Closer Settlement League with
the object of securing a slice of the encircling
big squattages, the members of the Closer
Settlement Board, who visited the locality,
declared it “unsuitable for mixed farming.”
Very well. Last week one farmer there-
abouts harvested 85 bushels of oats per
acre; another stacked 60 tons of hay from
three acres; was secured for greasy
merino; 10 trucks of fat bullocks were re-
cently acquired at £l9 a head; also there are
splendid crops of potatoes, tomatoes and
fruit, and dairying is profitably engaged in
where the big runs haven’t pinched the room.
Now what is necessary for mixed farming?
Tassy has just heard the painful news
from the Public Works Department that a
railway survey has cost over £2OO per mile;
that four miles of railway have been built
at an expenditure of £ll,OOO in money and
six months in time for each mile; that the
Department has spent five years and over
£2OOO in trying to remove a rock at King
Island, and is now further off the end of the
job than when it started; that the whole
Department is in a most frightful muddle;
and that the salaries of most of the officers
of this Department are paid out of loams!
In handling stores the Department is a
world’s champion. No records have been
kept of the goods issued on some works; on
the Don-Melrose tram 3150 rails were re-
ceived from store, but only 37 can be traced
as having been handed out; 18,275 sleepers
came in, and 25 were accounted for in the
books. Of 278 bags of oats 227 were not
accounted for, and of 343 bags of chaff 99
were found in the store. No one possessed
a clue to the whereabouts of the odd 244.
Generally speaking, the habit of this awful
Department had been to send an officer round
to buy timber, horses, bullocks, chaff and
sundries, and then get a list of the prices to
be paid, but latterly even the list was not
bothered about. As the official report ob-
serves : “All the stores may be legitimately
consumed and everything may be absolutely
all right, but there is nothing to show it.”
Which is vague and unsatisfactory.

Auditorium Ashmead Bartlett Lectures
Ashmead Bartlett lost all his final “g’s”
and the attention of the audience in the
back seats, who could hear him only with
considerable strain, at his last lecture at
Sydney Town Hall on Saturday evening. Bart-
lett is a smart journalist who has been well
paid to pose as a lecturer. Again and
again, after a stirring statement, he crushed
the tentative applause by tagging on to his
period a weak and unnecessary* clause. And
he told nothing new to those of the audience
who have read his, and other, despatches.
His lantern pictures of Anzac, however, gave
a better idea of that Australian out-back
selection than his own descriptions. But,
nowadays, the lantern slide is painfully
stationary compared with the cinematograph.

The stage realism of “Under Fire,” com-
bined with the excellent cast and careful
production, looks like assuring this company
a long run at Sydney Criterion. Still, this
realism is a long way behind the realism of
the movies.

At Sydney Adelphi on Saturday night
Gerald E. Griffin will make an offering of
the second of his series of Irish plays, “Con,
the Shaughraun.” Con has not been seen
in Sydney since Dion Boucicault produced
him a dozen years or so ago.
Those who would like to renew their
acquaintance with Benno, Fuzzy Ellis, Billy,
Sarah, and the other “Fact’ry ’Ands” of
Edward Dyson’s, will meet these interesting
individuals at the Palace Theatre on Satur-
day next. The production will be under the
direction of Charles Stanford.

still lead the comedy with their gaily insane

tear Dreadnoughts to pieces with his naked
hands if left alone with a fleet for a day or
two. Otherwise the show is made up of
pleasant average turns.

For those Melbourne people who are at
all worried by the question of Hamlet’s state
of mind before and after his conversation
with his father’s spirit the present perform
a nee at the Princess’s should be a first-class
attraction. The melancholy Dane is a very
real person this time, brooding suspiciously
over the circumstances of his parent’s de-
cease until the ghost comes to state the
facts of the case, which puts him somewhat
off his “block.” A haunted, irresolute young
man, who is neither too mad nor too thea-
trical, considering that he was always in-
terested in plays and players. If Shak-
speare had any very definite intention m
his creation of his most famous character
this is probably the most reasonable inter-
pretation of it. To a considerable propor-
tion of Saturday night’s audience “Ham-
let,” alas! was only an improved Marlow-
drama. Although they didn’t know that
Germany long ago claimed Immortal Wil-
helm for its own, these unsophisticated
critics seemed chiefly struck by the Teutonic
thoroughness of the author’s methods. Shak-
speare was the Kaiser of tragic drama. His
kultur was merciless. Ilis motto was
slaughter. He drew Ills quill from behind
his ear (as the Kaiser drew his precious
sword from Its scabbard) and dipped the nib
in blud. “Hamlet” is an ancient domestic
family tragedy. There are two families, and
little else, in it —the Fontinbraves and the
Poloniouses. And where are they when the
curtain falls for the last time? Out of action.
Every member of them has been murdered,
manslaughtered or extinguished. The head
of the Fontinbrav family died before
the play began, and sent his ghost to make
trouble for his son, widow and brother. Old
man Polonious, with Ms son Laertes and
his daughter, Miss Ophelia Polonious, get
drawn into the trouble and Hamlet kills the
eaves-dropping Polonious. Afterwards he
stabs his uncle with a poisoned rapier and
forces him to swallow the dregs of the cup
of poison which his mother has just drunk
from. Meanwhile Hamlet and Polonious,
jun., have wounded one another fatally, and
Miss Ophelia, after lunatically falling from
a weeping willow branch into 10ft. of fish-

only, instead of, as of old, a semi-circle of
