The six o'clock closing of hotels in Syd-
ney is responsible for expressions like these
among some theatre-goers. Thirsty souls
are doing their best nightly to forget the
interval habit.
Rosencrantz and Guildenstern are a crude
pair of courtiers not nearly as well spoken
as the two casual soldiers on the ramparts.
And Osric is neither youthful nor graceful.
Miss Harvey does the little part of the
Player queen very nicely, and Miss Hunter
Watts, as a brunette Ophelia, is charming
in her sanity and impressive in her madness.
No Ophelia could be more gently or pathetic-
ally realistic.
* * * *
Apart from other things, “The Waybacks,”
at Melbourne King’s, is disagreeably remind-
ful of “On Our Selection” in its thin, but
lurid flavoring of bellowdrama. Just as the
Steele Rudd play found room for a working
viP in, so the present outbreak includes a
young rotter who endeavors to provide re-
lief from the roars of the Waybacks by
frightening his dear old uncle to death. But
beyond the puerile wickedness of nightly
patrolling uncle’s residence in a suit of
phosphorus, the young man isn’t especially
offensive. He embezzles not, neither does he
insult with base proposals the lonely school-
marm whom a syrupy bush Adonis is fated
to wed. All the characters are dramatically
negligible. The only one with any claims
to vitality is Dad, and he merely reflects the
crude and primitive nature of Dad Rudd.
All Rudd’s fondness for sudden violence and
whiskers is carefully repeated in old-man
Waybaek. As for the Wayback family, they
are at least original in being too numerous
to mention.
Of the cast, William Stewart as Dad
follows the histrionic trail which comedian
Bert Bailey played before him. He is an
ancient with a large heart, an ingratiating
energy, and a bellow equal to all emergen-
cies. Miss Gladys Lee as Ma is stricken
with lethargy, while Miss Blair and Miss
Rooney, daughters Tilly and Euphemia. are
not unusual enough to leave even an abiding
impression of caricature. Cleave McGrath
gives emphasis to the loutish imbecility of
Jabez, but a perpetually gaping mouth is
the beginning, middle and full-stop of Leslie
Mansfield’s representation of Rube. Frank
Hatherly as the bad boy is doubtfully accept-
able, and Herbert Stallard is warned against
uttering the hero's sentiments in a high-
pitched voice that with half a note rise would
become a whistle.
Rather suddenly the panto at Melbourne
Her Majesty’s has announced its intention
of quitting the premises. It will run up to
the last day of the month only, so anyone
desirous of seeing it again had better tie a
piece of cotton round his nose as a reminder.
On March 4 the Comic Ops. return with
“Gipsy Love” and Florence Young.

The Follies at the Tivoli (Melbourne)
continue to be “supplemented by the Belgian
Concert Party,” as the advt. puts it. From
an impartially artistic point of view the
Follies are subjecting the Concert Party to
interruptions, and there are times when one
wishes they wouldn’t. The three instru-
mentalists are a musical apology for all the
ragtime items in the entertainment, and
when baritone Goossens warbles “O Solo
Mio” to their joint accompaniment, the
apology seems more than ample.

This week Ashmead Bartlett is dealing
more comprehensively with the Dardanelles
subject at the Auditorium. Everybody knows
that Bartlett knows his subject, and
he needs no further assurance of the
public’s desire to make him warm up to it.
All the applause necessary to urge a lecturer
on his long, lonely way is always in readi-
ness to burst forth.

A film version of “The Man Who Stayed
at Home,” followed by a striking Zeppelin
item, gave a warlike complexion to Hoyt’s
Melbourne programmes on Saturday. This
week the house is screening the Australian
production of Nurse Cavell’s murder, with
Vera Pearce sweetly suffering Hun ven-
geance in the name-part.
The most striking thing in sight in Adel-
aide shows is the Nurse Cavell film-story at
West’s; with Vera Pearce and Harrington
Reynolds in the star parts. It is a fine
example of the value of the new method of
teaching history as you may wish it taught.
It is unlikely, for example, that even a Hun
court-martial had a horrid grin on its face
when pronouncing sentence of death ; and it
is certain that the. victim did not, as depicted,
proudly wave aside the proffered eye-bandage
and stand boldly up to face the volley. Won-
dergraph is showing Tittell Brune’s “Golden
West” girl with the aces in her stocking,
and one or other of the houses has unreeled

to visit the city) and Gaby Deslys (who is
not likely to). At the King’s Paul Stanhope
and co. take up half the programme with a
revue, “This Is the Life.” At the Royal
Anderson’s Co. is doing good business, slid-
ing rapidly from the breezy “Sailor’s Wed-
ding” to the preachy “Girl’s Cross Roads.”
“Jwosp”: Genevieve Ward, who has gone
back to the stage for George Alexander in
"The Basker” at the age of 67, was origin-
ally in grand opera, and had established her-
self in Italy, Britain and America, when
diphtheria knocked her singing voice out of
gear. Eleven years of teaching in New
rork and then she bloomed at the Theatre
Loyal, Manchester, as a powerful Lady Mac-
beth. There are two biographies of her:
one deals with the career of Genevieve
'»ard, the other is “A Memoir of Ginevra
Luerrabella.”
From a cold corner in N’York :
There are 950 retail flicker palaces in this city,
and in a recent month over 5000 players were
Represented on the screens. More than 2000 flickors
and flickoresses own motor-cars and dwell in Easy-
stieet, and how long it is going to last isn’t
worrying them. Flick has given the legitimate
idage a staggering blow in the meantime, and while
picture humpies are springing up wherever hope of
a dividend leaps in the human breast, real theatres
are finding it more and more difficult to drag anyone
inside.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Avon”: Hamlet glooming on the Mel-
bourne stage again, in the person of Allan
Wilkie, sets my memory machine working.
The workmanlike Sullivan was the best up
to his date, with a performance about
equally welcome in London, Sydney and
Melbourne. Brooke was too heavy for Ham-
let, and seldom acted it. But he had the
matchless voice and profusion of statuesque
attitudes. Charles Kean’s Hamlet would
be only a sort of photograph when he
reached Australia, though he had been bet-
ter in the part than his renowned father.
Montgomery was a Melbourne craze. He
had not been so in London, nor did he be-
come so in Sydney. Titheradge, a far better
actor, was something like him. Montgomery
introduced here the blonde Fechter wig—
apparently the right thing. Creswick’s Ham-
let was astonishingly good, considering his
great age. Adams was the ideal of many.
And honorable mention in varying degrees
to Fairclough, llowe, 11. B. Irving, and the
always intelligent but nasal Dampier, who
overcame many disqualifications by force of
mind.
“Jean S.-” : For the purpose of educating
young and uninformed soldiers anent the
dangers of syphilis Gregan McMahon is
arranging to present Brieux’s “Damaged
Goods” at one of the military camps at an
early date. There is a notable moral in
Brieux’s plain, unvarnished tale of the nice
young man who would not take the doctor’s
advice, and with McMahon as the medico
and poet McCrae as his appalling patient
the “lesson” should soak in with dramatic
emphasis.
“Arradni”: Now that we are about to
get another J.C.W. war production, let’s
hope more attention will be paid to detail
than in “The Man Who Stayed at Home.”
TI wireless nart of “The Man Who Stayed”
w; : farcical to anyone with technical know-
ledge. Frank Harvey didn’t make one
letter correctly during his frantic signalling.
11 is Morseing resembled the attempts of the
beginner, who is fingering a key for the first
time. Then, again, a wireless equipment,
capable of talking to German coast stations
and ships at sea, couldn’t be worked by
a couple of accumulators. A power of at
least two kilowatts would be required, and
such an apparatus couldn’t be enclosed in a
wooden fireplace without consuming the
whole show. Finally the key used was a
toy affair which would melt in actual w r ork.
When so much advt. is being made out of
the “production,” it seems a fair thing that
the “production” shouldn’t be a howling
farce.

“E.T.”: When Barrie’s “What Every
Woman Knows” had been running a week
or two, it struck George Musgrove that cer-
tain passages of dialogue between two of
the middle-aged characters were not
thoroughly understood by the audience. The
male character in question when a young
man had met the lady in Constantinople,
and had almost, but not quite, proposed
marriage to her in those days. lie recalls
the tender memories when they meet again,
after many years, in the second act. “Ah,
those glorious nights on the Bosphorus!”
he exclaims reminiscently. One evening
before the curtain rose Musgrove suggested

that the locale should be altered so that it
might more easily be grasped by those in
front. The actor begged to differ; he
thought everybody knew where the Bos-
phorus was and didn’t like to interfere with
Barrie. “G.M.” was not to be baulked.
Calling a scene-shifter he interrogated him.
“Bill, d’ye know what the Bosphorus is?”
“No, Mr. Musgrove,” said Bill. “Well, d’ye
know what the Mediterranean is?” “Yes,
it’s a sea, ain’t it?” “Correct,” said “G.M.”
Then turning triumphantly to the mummer:
“There you are, my boy; better make it
‘Mediterranean.’ ” A.nd “Mediterranean” it
was henceforth.
“Harrison O.”: That Poverty Point yarn
(B. 10/2/T6) of the naughty girl who found
the peace that passeth all misunderstanding
after witnessing the miracle in “A Village
Priest” may be a mendacity, but it contains
the germ of a great truth. If there is one
person above all others likely to be im-
pressed by a stagey trick of the sort it is the
common or Collins-street variety of Juliet.
For a year or so, as a City court reporter,
I formed platonic friendships with a num-
ber of ladies of ridiculously easy virtue,
who used to attend to receive the usual
“fiver” or a month, and often talked with
them about dramatic art; for Flossie
delights to visit the theatre. To a
couple of likely females I loaned
copies of Ibsen and Strindberg; but
my well-meant efforts to spread culture
among the demi-monde of Melbourne met
with scant success. With a few variations
all of my friends favored the same plays—
“ Camille,” “Everywoman,” “The Second
Mrs. Tanqueray,” “The Sign of the Cross,”
“A Village Priest,” “Parsifal” and “The
Christian.” Apparently these nymphs like
best a play which idealises a mem-
ber of their own class, or else is varnished
with a pale cast of sanctity. But Flo
insists on lily-white morality in her heroes.
There is an interesting psychological study
in this sharing of affection between John
Storm and Camille, Parsifal and Paula
Tanqueray. It is certainly strange that
Flossie, despite the cynicism which she in-
evitably acquires, nearly always has tears
to shed when a dramatist holds the mirror
upside down to her own nature or provides
the comfort of religion without the discom-
fort of a pew.
“C. Ockney”: Rider Haggard, novelist
and farming expert, who is re-visiting
Australia, once aspired to be a
dramatist. Several stage versions of his
“best sellers” have seen the footlights. “She”
was perhaps the most favorably received.
It was adapted for the boards by Clotilde
Graves (better known as “Richard Dehan”
nowadays), and was produced at the old
Gaiety in the Strand. Haggard was in a
private box, and when the curtain fell and
the usual cries of “Author” were raised, he
advanced to the front and bowed. Someone
in the audience yelled “Speech,” and the
biushing novelist (he was young then)
obliged. “La dies and gentlemen,” he said,
“1 thank you for the kind way in which you
have received the play. I won’t go so far
as to say that it is a great play, but I think
you will agree with me that it has great
possibilities.”
“Stargazer”: The influx of American
mummers is having one very marked evil
influence on the Australian stage, it has
served to afflict scores of bright young pros
of both sexes, born and bred in Australia,
with the loathly accents of the cute fellows
from Nevada, lowa, Oklahoma, New York
and thereaway. Apparently it is accepted
as absolutely essential that when a per-
former in Melbourne or Sydney has some-
thing smart to say he must say it out
of the hollows of his neck, via the nose, with
the fetching Yankee timbre that is so popu-
lar. This is an abominable affectation. It
is high time Australia began to fasten on to
her own national peculiarities, good, bad
and indifferent, and if there’s popular fun
to be got out of awful talk let us make it a
local industry.
An “Author’s Theatre” is the latest idea
under discussion in London theatrical
circles. A place where new plays can be
tried and tested for the benefit of the aspir-
ing voung dramatists, and where theatrical
managers can rjrop in and sample the goods
thus submitted,* is declared to be a “loug-
felt want.” Sounds very plausible in theory,
but who is going to fork out the rent, and
pay for the electric light, not to mention
wages of players and staff? If managers
have neither the will nor the time to read
the numerous plays sent to them by un-
known dramatists, they are not likely to
spend their afternoons sampling experi-
mental performances at the Author’s. Marie
Lohr is one of the leading actresses who
have expressed cordial approval of the pro-
ject. Marie says she is “intensely inter-
ested” in the idea, and it seems to her to
“present some fascinating possibilities.” It
will save her the reading of so many plays.
Dick Boucicault, partner with Robert
Brough in a well-remembered high-class
Australian theatrical enterprise, was about
to start management on his own at the New
Theatre, London, on Boxing Day, when last
mail was leaving. lie was to begin with
a revival of “Peter Pan.” which he pro-
duced for several successive Christmas sea-
sons in an adjacent theatre as chief lieu-
tenant of the late Charles Frolimann, mur-
dered by Huns in the Lusitania. After
Peter Pan Dick will present his wife, Irene
Vanbrugh, in a new play specially written
for her by Somerset Maughan. He is also
to have a character in it himself. Irene
has for a long time been wishful to play a
couple of Shakspearian roles, Portia and
Beatrice, and Richard hopes to be able to
gratify her desires in that respect.
The Duke of York’s, London, a theatre
that surely ought to be renamed in a more
sensible fashion, was the headquarters of
the late Charles Frohmann, and it has now
been taken over by Miss Horniman, who
has acquired the remainder of Frohmann’s
lease. Miss Horniman has made a big suc-
cess during recent years as manager of a
repertory theatre at Manchester. It was
she who discovered Stanley Houghton and
n rod need most of his plays long before the
world woke up to their merits. Miss
Hornimau says she reads on the average
40 plays a week, and she reckons that
she has waded through a total of 10,000
in her time. Her verdict is that not two
per cent, were worth a second thought. In
starting her new London enterprise she
lias isssued a proclamation that she. has
“no use for a war play, being convinced
that theatre-goers have at present no relish
for that sort of thing.”
“Private Boxer”: It’s always a puzzle to
me where the picture-show spruikers come
from. Are they, like poets, “born, not
made” ; or do they, like Topsy, “just grow” ?
Some I have listened to can talk like M’s.l*,
at a picnic; others like imported mummers
speaking their well-thought-out impromptus
before the curtain on first nights. All are
worth hearing. They know their job and
are as “continuous” as the shows they ad-
vertise. Many a trained mummer might
well marvel at and envy their quick
“study.” Also their quick wit, for their
replies to interruptions and interrogations
from the crowd at times will take a lot of
beating, even by mimes who patter ever so
eloquently behind the footlights. Give me
the good spruiker in preference to the
mediocre mummer. Whence comes the
spruiker and his tKibe?
Oswald Stoll aspires to be the Napoleon
of British variety. From his headquarters
at London Coliseum he directs a score of
palatial music-halls, and he has just made
a notable addition to the list by acquiring
the Alhambra. His only rival, Alfred Butt,
who runs the Palace and the Empire, was
also after the Alhambra, and thought he
had secured it when at the eleventh hour
Stoll chipped in with a better and more
attractive offer. The Alhambra is one of
the oldest and largest of the London variety
houses, and for a long time it specialised in
gorgeous ballet productions with plenty of
pretty girls, magnificent dresses and expen-
sive staging. But that form of entertain-
ment has now gone completely out of
fashion, and, in consequence, the Alhambra
has not been a paying property of late.
Stoll is going to reconstruct the Vast ram-
shackle structure and run it on the lines
lie has so successfully adopted at the Col-
iseum close by. He will be the second Mel-
bourne man to take the Alhambra in hand.
Alfred Moul, once musical critic of Mel-
bourne Age, was its managing director for
some years.
“Dumper”: The Australian film manu-
facturer has other troubles besides the
absurdly low tariff. He is bound by certain
censor regulations that are never applied
to the imported article. For instance, no
local picture must make fun of the police, yet
nine-tenths of the imported comic films are
police skits. Then in the dramatic sphere
we are never allowed to depict the villainous
Tammany policeman, though that person is
largely responsible for the success of many
imported photoplays. Again, local bush-
ranging pictures are absolutely forbidden;
yet nothing is said against an imported film
like “Stingaree,” the scenes of which are
laid in Australia, the hero being a bush-
ranger and glorified martyr, who in the very
first series sticks up and robs a mail coach.
I: is another bad instance of Australia's
pernicious habit of handing the foreigner the
oyster and hitting our own people in the
eye with the shell.
“Moana”: There seems to be something
approaching an exchange of mummers going
on between Australia and Wilson’s country.
The number of players with a twang now
getting a hearing on this continent is easily
balanced by the army of Australians across
the Pacific. Here are a few of them and
their present jobs : Ivy Scott, well known in
musical comedy with Williamson’s, is now
wife to a big theatrical manager, and is
going on tour as Maid Marion in the comic
opera, “Robin Ilood.” Charles Coleman is
playing in a sex drama, “The Natural
Law.” Dion Titheradge goes into a new pro-
duction by William Brady. Leonard Willey
is appearing in a dramatic version of Steven-
son’s “Treasure Island.” Claude Flemming,
years ago here with Nellie Stewart, is the
baritone lead in a musical show, “Two’s
Company.” Elsie Mackay, Fan Dango’s
stepdaughter, is to appear with Cyril Maude
in “Grumpy”; and Leila Carmichael, once
decorating the Williamson chorus, has mar-
ried one of the Lubin Co.’s directors, and is
helping to make flicker film for hubby.
“Jwosp” : Tom Graves, the little comedian
with a tricky memory, who was with “The
Gay Gordons” five or six years ago, died in
his greasepaint at Chicago on December 22.
lie was playing the leading comedy role in
“Nobody Home,” and just before the close
of the piece dropped dead in the wings. He
was a brother of George Graves, whose style
he largely followed. When Gay Gordoning
here he often got the verses of his songs
mixed and had to gag until he could take
mental soundings.

PENEOLD'S
WINES
That outbreak of last week was not the
first of its kind in Sydney. The 99th Regi-
ment, Colonel Henry Despard, was the
last of the British troops to occupy the
old barracks in George-street. Despard, a
martinet, was known as “Keep off the
grass.” He had a family cow, and the lawn
in Barrack-square was preserved for the
animal’s use. Late in 1845 the Colonel dis-
continued the grog ration. Not only’ did the
men lose the liquor, but they discovered
that the money allowance in lieu thereof
was just one-half what it should be. There
was a wet canteen in the barracks, run by
Thomas Kellett, who also kept the Crispin
Arms, in Clarence-street, and later on built
the Sir Joseph Banks Hotel at Botany. The
99th men refused duty, and General Sir
Maurice O’Connell —whose wife, daughter of
Governor Bligh, had witnessed another
mutiny—sent to Van Diemen’s Land for the
Xlth regiment, Colonel Bloomfield, to hurry
over and quell the disturbance. The regi-
ment landed where Circular Quay now is
and marched up George-street with fixed
bayonets to the parade ground, where they
were received with loud cheers by the 99th,
who were confined to barracks. But no
blood was shed. Bloomfield, a different type
of man to Despard, understood the soldier,
and soon had the irritated regiment under
control. The men got their grog money in
full and the mutiny fizzled into history.
« * * *
“Melb”: Military-surgeon Crossley, from
Egypt, lectured here this week on the Great
Pyramid, of interest to many thousands of
Australian soldiers, besides tourists. The
other Pyramids are empty imitations of the
great one. Piazzi Smyth gives its age as
4087 years. The American International
Bible Students’ Association, largely repre-
sented in Australasia, in a book on the Pyra-
mid claiming a million readers, produces an
idea that it was constructed under the
guidance of Melchizedek. It is said
to-be a Bible, with endless significance.
Therefore, the orthodox hold that it is from
Divine inspiration, infinitely beyond the wis-
dom of the Egyptians.

“General Sorrowful” writes :
Marching in fours one of the columns passed
down the city street bronzed and hard-looking ; also
half-full of beer. Men rushed from shops and offices
and waved the gallant lads good-bye. Girls leaned
from upstairs windows, passing down glad-looks and
scraps of ribbon. After a while the column didn’t
march in fours, and the waving and the glad-looks
were cut off. The men slouched along any old
way. Some of them were cheerfully kicking a kero-
sene-tin backwards and forwards across the road.
Some were ringing a bell. Most were climb-
ing into passing tramcars and telling the guards to
settle up with Kitchener. A Chow drifted into the
firing-line loaded with a couple of baskets full of
lettuce and spring onions. The vegetables dis-
appeared into space inside lOsee. A cart stacked
with lemonade and cordials happened along. It lost
its freight in one lift. A cake shop was stripped
as bare as an imported tree in winter. Then the
column surged on avd acquired beer and cigars at
the corner, and gradually broke up into roving
bands. At the pub some of the joyous spirits jambed
hard to the bar, and after consuming most of the
contents raged for the Johns to come on. The Johns
accepted the invitation, and unlimbering their batons
made the conflict as willing as the alcoholic cir-
cumstances allowed. For five minutes the air was
full of smash, crash and skin, and then the chal-
lengers broke and fled to re-form further on and
smash some more unarmed windows. “Look out!”
bellowed someone, and the ring of horses hoofs sug-
gested that it was time to retreat. The stampeders
made a wild rush for safety as the troopers rode
into the hail of blue metal. Thenceforward there
were wild alarms and scattering to and fro. In the
circus were teams of agitated figures in khaki pur-
sued by hard-faced, purposeful individuals with
lumps of timber. “Get it on to ’em,” screamed
someone as a fire-hose was brought to play upon a
picket. “Firel” said someone else. The inflamed
rabble poured away leaving a corpse and about a
dozen stricken bodies and Australian blood dripped
into Australian soil. A file of soldiers with bayonets
moved quickly across a street. Bedraggled men,
exhausted with shouting and fighting, boarded the
midnight trains and crawled away back to camp.
They had worked harder during that day and night
of sickening guzzling and scrapping than if they had
drilled from daylight to dark without a spell or a
feed. Many of those who were left behind were in
hospitals or prison cells.
* * * *
“Hamer”: A curious concern, started in
London by a clergyman, and known as “The
League for the Marrying of Broken Heroes,”
vanished like a scalded cat when the origi-
nator’s bishop threw his gaiters at it. But
it endured long enough to show that the
man who returns from the war, leaving a
portion of himself behind, need not be lonely
in his helplessness. The League’s object
was, frankly, to secure for incapacitated sol-
diers wives of a strong, energetic type who
were willing to support the family. Hus-
bands will be a scarce commodity when the
war is over; and the League’s advertise-
ments until it was scared off brought in a
rush of applications from women who were
anxious to get mates while the early doors
were open. Some day soon a herd of
disappointed women will start out after that
bishop and chase him on to the roof of St.
Paul’s.
South Australia has spent nearly £50,000
on a block of Old Folks’ Homes, to take the
foimer occupants of what was curtly called
the Destitute Asylum. The old place was
cramped in the city, on North Terrace,"
close to Government House, and both Uni-
versity and Public Library would probably
like its ground. The Homes’ site is high,
broad, and breezy from Magill, five miles out
on the first slopes of the hills. The place is
far better suited for its purpose than the
rejected Asylum site, and the new buildings
are supposed to be the very latest and best
design.

AUSTRALIA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(God give vs a good conceit of ourselves.)
Australia, my native land,
A stirring whisper in your ear —
’Tis time for you to understand
Your rating now is Al, dear.
You’ve done some rousing things of late,
That lift you from the simple state
In which you chose to vegetate.
The persons so saperi-or,
Whose patronage no more endures,
Now have to fire a salvo for
The glory that is fairly yours.
At length you need no sort of crutch,
You stand alone, you’re voted “much ’—
Get busy and behave as such.
No man from Oskosh, or from Hull,
Or any other chosen place
Can chance in with distended skull,
And cast aspersions in your face.
You’ve given all the world to know
Your proper standing as a foe,
And hats are off, and rightly so.
You’ve furnished heroes for the fray,
Your sterling merit’s widely blown
To all men’s satisfaction; say,
Now have you proved it to your own ?
Now t have you strength to stand and shim
In your own light and say, “Divine
The thing is that I do. It’s mine ! ”
The cannon’s stroke throws customs down
The black and bottomless abyss,
And quaking are the gilded crown
And palsied feet of prejudice.
The guns have killed, but it is true
They bring to life things good and new - .
•God grant they have awakened you !
My ears are greedy for the toast
Of confidence before our guest,
The loyal song, the manly boast
Your splendid faith to manifest.
In works of art and livelihood
AS ONE WHO KNEW.
SUNDAY SCHOOL TEACHER : “And now, my dear children, where do all the good girls
go?”
CHOSUS: “To Heaven, teacher.”
TEACHER: “'That’s right. Note, where do the bad girls go?”
SOPHISTICATED PUPIL: “To the soldiers’ camp.”
Shirk not the creed, “What’s ours is good,”
Dread not to have it understood.
Australia, lift your royal brow,
And have the courage of your pride.
Audacity becomes you now.
Be splendidly self-satisfied.
No land from lowliness and dearth
lias won to eminence on earth
That was not conscious of its worth.
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.

“Masarcus”: According to a Yankee call-
ing himself Captain James Brown there is
an island off the western ledge of Australia
upon which £12,000,000 in English sov-
ereigns was buried by pirates or land agents
ia 1850. Brown has got to the ear, or leg,
of sundry influential anu trusting business
men of Kansas City, who have financed an
expedition, which is now at San Francisco
fitting out a ship to bring home the sugar.
Brown says he is the only survivor of the
pirates who buried the treasure, and this is
alleged to be his second attempt to uncover
it (Evidently he was the pirate with the
worst memory.) The backers have been
told to read “Treasure Island” till next
November, when Brown expects the spades
to grate on the lid of the chest or the skull
and cross-bones or something.
* * * *
“Billy Blue”: N. S. Wales Liquor
Act has been responsible for the closing
lately of one of the oldest pubs in the south-
ern part of the State. This was Mandell-
son’s Hotel at Goulburn. It was not the
first hotel in that part, a drinkery having
been opened in the neighborhood by one Mat
Healy in 1832. The present town of Goul-
bnrn was laid out in ’33, and one Jack Cole,
a sawyer, realising the prospects of the place,
bought a corner section and opened a public-
house thereon. He remained till ’43, when
Joseph Mandellson came along and bought
him out, pulled down the old place and built
the house just closed by local-option vote.
At the time of closing it contained over 50
rooms, and in its time sheltered all sorts
of travellers, including sundowners, Gover-
nors and Judges when on the wal-
laby. It was there that the first
Masonic lodge meeting in the township
was held. In the diggings days at Araluen
and other fields Mandellson’s was the popu-
lar resort. There is a legend that Mandell-
son was once offered £30,000 to get out and
refused.

Paris Nesbit, of Adelaide, in a moment
of characteristic excitement, laid an infor-
mation for “fraudulent conversion” of £l4
against his own sou and partner. It is
safe to say that nobody ever supposed that
there was anything in such a charge against
the highly-esteemed Nesbit junior; and
when the case came on his lawyer read a
letter from the senior admitting that the
charge was made “with undue haste and
without full consideration,” and expressing
“sincere regret.” Counsel added hand-
somely that “only a man who was truly
great could make such a frank and generous
admission of error.”

comfortable, elderly gentleman in an arm-
chair by the fire to send patronising mes-
sages to soldiers on active service in the
middle of winter. They can’t shoot back.”

One Darcy Broughall had his ardor for
collecting wives discouraged at Melbourne
last week when Edith Connor sued him for
breaching his promise to marry her. It was
explained that Darcy, after being engaged to
plaintiff for a year, casually informed her
that he had lately met the wife he divorced
years previously, and that she urgently
desired to marry him some more. Also he
thought the ex-wife had a sort of first call
on him, and he trusted Miss C. would realise
the reasonableness of the argument. She
didn’t. She went after him for £249 dam-
ages. When the jury learned that Darcy
had not only defrauded Miss Connor but
also his ex-wife, and had married a third
lady, it said something firm about £l7O dam-
ages and costs. Some men have very expen-
sive hobbies.

“Billy Blue” : The last of the great Berry
Estate at Coolaugatta, in Shoalhaven, has
been subdivided and sold. Originally the
estate consisted of a free grant of 14,000
acres to Alexander Berry and Edward Woll-
stonecraft, brotliers-in-law and merchants in
Sydney, in May, 1822, the only condition
being that they were to keep and work 100
convicts on the estate. At that time each
convict was costing the Government £l6
a year to feed and guard. Berry and his
partner gradually increased the acreage by
purchase till the whole estate consisted of
80 square miles of country. The convict
servants at Berry were treated well, and
when their sentences expired were encouraged
to settle down with a supply of axes and
pay the rent in felled trees. At one time
Berry had as many as 368 tenants clearing
the iand and their characters at the same
time.
* * * *
The allowance given to soldiers’ wives in
England is promoting a new industry, which
has grown to substantial dimensions. Many
married and other soldiers, oppressed by the
monotony of the same old missus, marry
another woman on the eve of departure for
the Front. The consequence is that the War
Office is later besieged by a rightful wife and
a “creature” who thinks she holds the first
mortgage. Then the harassed W.O. has to
shove the Kaiser problem to one side while
it sorts the ladies out. Some enterprising
warriors will find something worse than war
awaiting them in England —unless _ poly-
gamy is made compulsory during their ab-
sence. Failing that their refuge is the list
of missing.

wrecks on King Island, “Briner” (B.
10/2/TO) seems to have lost his own bear-
ings. The records show that the Neva
(credited with 500 women convicts by
“Briner”) had 340 individuals on board all
told, viz., 150 female prisoners with 33
children, nine free women with 22 children,
and 26 of a crew. Though all hands were
not lost as stated, it
was a sufficiently
wholesale drowning,
only eight of the crew,
the skipper and 15 of
the women managing
to reach the shore
alive.. These women
were all in a more or
less nude condition
(the ship ran ashore
in the middle of the
night), and seven of
their number perished
before morning. The
survivors, being mor-
tally afraid to venture
inland on account of
the ferocious aborigi-
nals they believed they
would meet, managed
to keep alive for three
weeks with shellfish
gathered on the beach,
when they were dis-
covered by the leading
resident of the soli-
tude, a sealer named
Scott, who was accom-
panied by the inmates
of his harem, three
black gins and seven
piebald children. Scott
and his team did their
best for the ship-
wrecked wretches for
a month, when they
were conveyed to
Launceston by Cap-
tain Charlie Friend, a
whaler who chanced
to visit the locality
of the wreck in his
cutter. And I must
insist? on having the
eight recorded survi-
vors of the British Admiral of 1874. There
were 79 souls lost —quite enough corpses for
aii old-time wreck. I want, if possible, to
bieak “Briner” of the “all-hands-lost” habit.
Brother Chidley sends a cry from Mace-
donia :—•
Reception House,
15th February, 1916.
Editor, Bulletin. Sir, —Will you let the public
know that I have never had a fair hearing? I
don’t mean at the Court, but by the public and by
learned societies. I have repeatedly applied to the
University, Royal Society and B.M.A. to be allowed
to give a lecture, illustrated, and to answer all
questions. These bodies have always refused. I have
also had all halls and lecture-rooms in Sydney closed
against me by the police. Is that fair?
I earnestly beseech the public and the Government
in the interests of truth and humanity to see to it
that I be allowed to address the public before bury-
ing me for life with lunatics. I ask this not so
much in fairness to myself, as in the interests of
science and humanity.

“Saint Agent” discusses business :
Is Australia looking for a patron saint ? If so,
St. Mark is an applicant for the billet through some
Melbourne Anglicans who are tendering on his behalf.
His “day” in the Church calendar falls on April 25,
the date of the famous Landing; and he happens to
be out of a job as a national patron since the re-
public of Venice, which formerly employed him in
that capacity, went out of business. Mark, by the
way, is his surname, much as if on<s should
speak of “St. Smith”; his first name is
John. It is urged in his favor that he knows
something about the locality of Anzac, since the
Venetians carried his badge on their banners when
they made their conquests in that region seme cen-
turies ago. Moreover, he is alleged to have founded
the first Christian church ,in Alexandria, from which
port the Australasian troops, set out to battle. But
there is one. thing against him, and it is serious. He
started with Paul and Barnabas, the famous
revivalists of his time, on a missionary tour which
included Salonika, and passed within sight of Anzac ;
but in the first few weeks of the tour, after the party
had carried the Great Tidings to the principal towns
of Cyprus, Mark became alarmed at the prospect of
a rough handling on the way across Asia Minor, and
left the others to go on to Gallipoli and Macedonia
alone. A saint who suffered from that trouble is no
fit patron for the men who fought in Gallipoli, even
if their landing did happen to fall on his “day.”
“Try Fluke”: Maori medicine-man Rua
Canaan, who has bolted for his mountain
retreat in the Urewera bush, either to dodge
arrest or to make a dramatic submission
to the John Hops, is a weird sort of poseur
—a Dowie and Brigham Young and Mad
Mullah rolled into one long-haired brown
brother. Rua is the son of a tierce old,
bloke who was christened Canaan by some-
misguided missionary. The old man was;
killed in a light with the pakeha forces ini
’69 —he was one of Te Kooti’s tattooed!
Danites —but Rua is;
not of the warrior-
stamp ; he is all for the
good things of life. His
thousand-odd worship-
pers keep him in luxury
—plenty of wahine,
plenty of tucker and
plenty of cash to take
his seven wives and 12
hairy apostles down to
the coast townships for
a spree. When Rua
and his wahines and his :
bodyguard go on tour-
Julius Knight isn’t in,
it. I collared Rua once>
in a Maoriland town
and hauled him off to*
a photographer’s; the
curio was too good to
miss. The holy man
took up a saintly pose,
after the official hair-
dresser had combed out
his long, wavy tresses
and arranged them pro-
perly on his coat collar,
and one of the 12
apostles stage-whispered
in the brown tongue,
“Isn’t he just like
Jesus!” I’ll give the
Mullah this much credit,
however; he modestly
assured me that he
wasn’t exactly like Ihu
Karaiti; but he thought!
he resembled John the
Baptist, because John
wore his hair long and went about
making prophetic tangis in the wil-
derness. As for his seven wives—well, says
Rua, what about Solomon, and what about
the fourth chapter of Isaiah, which says
that “On that day seven women shall take
hold of one man”? Rua won’t touch tobacco
or allow any of his acolytes to touch it,
but he has no objection to booze. Kapai te
waipiro.
THE EMPTY PACK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Here comes my birthday singing up the track
The past year’s days have hewn. Upon his back
The little stranger swings a goodly pack
Of gifts for me—all gay and nothing black.
First, lavender for friendship; and I smile,
Feeling your grey eyes bent on me the while,
joy to my friends; peace to my enemies ;
And for the past year’s hurts—why, here’s heart’s-
ease!
Here glad health smiles, and many a tripping rhyme
To suit the measure of the mood and time.
Your count is ended? Oh, a moment, hold—
Is not his love tucked in a hidden fold?
N.S.W.
NORA McAULIFFE.
THE REASON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
And why did staid men turn and look
At her with shining eyes ?
Was she some beauty from a book ?
Some woman ultra-wise ?
Was she a gifted authoress
Whose books were widely read ?
Will someone dare to chance a guess
Why each man turned his head?
Was she some mighty queen of song
Whose notes were liquid gold:
The queen for whom we’d waited long—
And were we now consoled ?
Was she a painter genius-thrilled
Whose pictures made the earth
Look commonplace—an artist skilled
Beyond all money’s-worth?
Was she some champion of the Right
Whose words were blazing gems ?
Whose speeches glistened, day and night,
Like priceless diadems?
Was she another Helen come
From out a modern Troy,
That men were almost stricken dumb
With unexampled joy?
Was she a modern Joan of Arc
Come back to tell her tale ?
Or else a suffragette of mark?
A Florence Nightingale?
No ; none of these —but something more:
A lady, eyes-begirt,
Whose limbs were shapely, and who wore
A most transparent skirt!
N.S.W.

“The Bulletin’s” Melbourne artist
visited Sydney incognito last week.
Several publicans kindly placed their
pubs at the disposal of the company.
At length the artist felt the time was
ripe for his oration in reply, but at the
very moment of his “thirdly,” opposi-
tion appeared on the offing. (We men-
tion no names, but have reason to
believe the trouble was due to the
underhand work of certain opposition
artists.)
As the guest of the city the artist felt
it his duty to lead his supporters to vic-
tory.
But the enemy proved too strong.
And he was forced to retreat, aban-
doning much booty.

Judge Boucaut daily travelled to and fro
from Glenelg to Adelaide in a second-class
railway carriage. He affirmed, with sar-
donic truth, that second-class was just as
uncomfortable as first-class.
Matt Baird, who obliged now-Senator
McKissock by beating him for the Ballarat
West seat in the Vic. Assembly, went to the
Front last year as a captain in the A.I.F.
Now, as Major Baird, he is coming back to
Australia from Egypt, invalided after a
long stay in hospital.
Cecil Ilartt, The Bulletin’s corporal-
cartoonist, wounded at Gallipoli, was brack-
eted with Andrew Fisher on the latter’s
recent visit to Ilaresfield hospital, London.
A crayon caricature by Ilartt of Geo. Reid
was pointed out as being a “treasure” of the
institution, and the breathless cablegram
broke the news that Hartt’s black-and-white
work was placing Australian sentiment pro-
minently into London publications. How
Greid and Australian sentiment came to be
bracketed isn't clear. The young artist was
wounded in two places by Abdul; the thigh
wound healed quickly, but the ankle-hurt
has caused prolonged trouble.

Exit Francis Edward Bigge, 96. at Tor-
quay. He entered the navy in 1835. left
it in 1839. and joined a brother in N. S.
Wales. The pair moved to Queensland and
squatted near Ipswich. In August, 1842,
Francis was bailed up by bushrangers and
severely wounded. leader of the gang,
“Long Tom,” and another ex-convict named
Wilson, were captured, convicted and exe-
cuted. The story of this execution was
incorporated by Rolf Boldrewood in “Rob-
bery Under Arms.”
“0.K.” tells how Peacock might have had
his neck broken: —-
The shipboard scene in this year’s Williamson
panto, always sets me thinking of Premier Peacock.
Not Peacock in his well-advertised laughing mood,;
but Peacock in a moment of pale terror. Th%
suggestion comes this way. The- scene, which repre-
sents a stateroom of a passenger ship, is practically
a big box that rolls and rolls until it finally makes
complete revolutions, ■whilst the furniture is flung all
over the place. It is worked on the principle of
the “Haunted Swing,’’ a popular side-show of old,
the swing itself being a fixture in a' wooden apart-
ment that revolved around it when the machinery
was started. You sat on the swing and saw the
walls of the room rise and fall, and you got all the
inglorious effects of seasickness. Then you closed
your eyes (if you had been prompted) and knew that
you were perched on a motionless seat. The Mac-
mahons ran the “Swing.” It was the simplest of
illusions, but the mystery was too profound for
Peacock when he dropped in to have a shilling’s-
worth. He and his friends were warned, as all cus-
tomers were, that they must keep their seats or harm
might come to them. Peacock, however, wanted to
discover what difference it could make to an emi-
nent State politician if he stood on the floor instead
of keeping his seat when the room began to move.
So he quietly slipped off the swing and found himself
in trouble. The floor receded from under him ; he
slithered violently to the wall in front, and slith-
ered more violently to the wall behind, before the
astonished Macmalion in charge of the show could
blow his signal for the machinery to be stopped.
Mac stood always on a step at the side, conductor-
like, ready to cut off the proceedings if perchance
one of the swingsters went giddy or fell dead or
apoplectic to the moving floor. When the “Mother
Goose” panto, scene reminds me of Premier
Peacock’s rash adventure, I think of what must have
happened had Mac’s whistle been out of order. Then
would the calm, unconscious operators down below
have kept the room in action for the usual time,
and Peacock would have emerged from the entertain-
ment with a broken neck, and his funeral would
have been long, long ago.
Another yarn about the late Judge Bou-
caut, of South Australia. Two counsel, call
them Sharp and Nipper, were before him
on a summons job in chambers sharp,
whose summons it was, took a highly tecn-
nical objection to an affidavit used by -Nip-
per whereon the latter, by way of satire,
countered with an objection to the summons
itself, on the ground that it was not printed
with a one-quarter blank margin as required
by Ord. LXCCCC., Rule 9876. To counsels
amazement, Boucaut at once sent for a meas-
uring rule, solemnly scaled off the P a P e L
and, finding the objection well founded,
promptly dismissed the summons.
Joseph Hains, dead at Port Adelaide at
69, managed to combine prosperous hotel-
keeping with strong Labor principles. He
was treasurer of the original local Coastal
Seamen’s Union, and also of the old Port
Adelaide Working Men’s Association in its
toddling days, over 40 years back. During
the waterside strike of 1890 he did particu-
larly good work, and also earned a reputa-
tion for generosity which stuck to him to the
end.
“The Oldest Inhabitant” recalls a few
items: —■
The old Victorian Yeomanry Corps is recalled to
memory by the death of James Callender Campbell,
ex-M.L.C., Vic., at the agie of 77 years. James
arrived by the Royal Charter in ’5B, and entered
into partnership with Campbell Brothers, merchants
of Market-street, went to Maoriland in ’6l, and re-
turning three years later purchased the auction-
eering business now carried on as Campbell & Sons.
It is claimed for him that he was the last survivor
but one of the old corps, the survivor being Ran-
dall Carey, chairman of the Sydney Daily Tele-
graph. The V.Y.C. jvas established in 1855. The
men found their own horses and uniform, and were
commanded by Colonel J. Hunter Ross, a solicitor.
After a time a split occurred, and an important
section seceded and registered as the Prince of Wales
Light Horse, with Mathew Ilervey, an M.L.C. and a
(then) wealthy squatter as commanding officer. All
the leading squatters and merchants who could ride
a horse joined, amongst them the late J. S. Butters.
A drill-sergeant was required, and thereon hangs a
tale. Old Melbournites will remember, in the ’so's,
that there stood in Lonsdale-street on the south side,
between William and King streets, a little public
house known as The Red Lion Inn. It stood back
some distance from the pathway, with an old-time
horse-trough—the scooped-out trunk of a big free-
standing in the -space between the inn and the road-
way. The house was much frequented by soldiers,
the military barracks being close handy. Towards
the end of the ’so’s it was kept by an ex-sergeant-
major of the 40th Regiment. He was a very
large man; his wife was a mite.* They lived
very unhappily. McLean, after leaving The Red
Lion, became landlord of The Bull and Mouth in
Bourke-street. In a drunken fury he attacked the
woman with a carving-knife. For this he got two
years’ imprisonment. To secure McLean as drill-
instructor was the ambition of the new regiment,
the Governor was petitioned for his release, and it
was granted on condition that the regiment became
responsible for the man’s good conduct. He behaved
well until the troop was disbanded. McLean
and his wife then went to Maoriland, where their
quarrels were renewed, ending up by his being
hanged for her murder.

“The Pariah”: I knew Chaplain J> H;
Stewart (mentioned in B. 10/2/T6), and
also met his sister Kitty in Hong Kong. As
a girl just entering her teens, she hid J. K-
and another brother and piloted them about
600 miles to safety after seeing her father
and mother killed by Boxers. She is a
missionary now, somewhere in inland China.
A graceful woman and beautiful, she could
have married well, but declared that it was
her duty to Christianise the Chinese. How
this enormous and impossible job attracts the
religious idealists!

THE UNKNOWN PATIENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
[Henry Lawson was recently an inmate of the
Walker Hospital for Convalescents —“Founded by
the late Thomas Walker, of Yaralla, in the Hope
that Within Its Walls Many Sufferers should be
Restored to Health.” This is by way of a song of
gratitude.]
The moonlight breathes on Walker Home and softens
scrub and hill;
The native trees are strangely stirred, the pines are
very still;
The nurse's lantern flits and flits, and pain and
sorrow :ease,
For all the patients are asleep, and all is Rest and
reace.
Nor class nor creed nor race debars, and even
Wealth is free —
The suffering miser shares alike the Home with
Poverty ;
The felon's past is never known when kindness
“sends him through”—
The stone says “many sufferers”; but it means
“sinners,” too.
Within a corner of the grounds, where patients
seldom go,
Well screened by firs and shrubbery a sandstone ledge
runs low;
And, pencilled by an unknown hand upon the yellow
stone,
Is “God Bless Thomas Walker”—four simple words
alone.
I know not who the writer was, and I may never
know:
It may have been but yesterday, maybe was long
ago.
’Tis near the pathway that divides the women from
the men —
It may have been a tortured Christ or a suffering
Magdalen.
perhaps some shy and shrinking soul, relieved
awhile from care,
Crept out of sight of “sterner stuff” to pay a tribute
there.
Or maybe an Impenitent, and many such there be,
For hard men often drop a tear where none but
God may see.
But, good or bad, or high or low—or were he
anything
(Or even traitor to his creed, and rebel to his
king)—
1 trust the unknown patient went with softened
care and pain,
With health and honesty restored, to fight the world
again.
There is a stately home of Rest where all the scene
is fair,
And in the sun the ripples run along the river there;
’Twas builded in the noonday dream of one of kindly
wealth,
“In the Hope that Many Sufferers Should be Restored
to Health.”
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
THE GASKET.
THE UNKNOWN PATIENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The funeral of the late Mrs. Walter Russell Hail
took place yesterday at Melbourne General Cemetery.
The casket was brought to Melbourne by the second
division of the express.— The Age.
In coffin grim, in days of old,
The fussy undertaker
Did bury Midas ’neath the mould
Of God’s own special acre;
But nowadays the prison cell
He lays a wealthy toff in,
For evermore in peace to dwell,
Is not described as “coffin.’’
A coffin plain of ash or oak
(A cheaper wood makes scandal)
Suffices for a common bloke,
A vulgar Goth or Vandal.
lie sleeps, protected from the air,
In lidded box (not basket),
Whereas a multi-millionaire, 4
A human jewel rich and rare,
Is locked within a casket.
VICTORIA.
O.E.

This co. has altered its book-keeping methods
three or four times ; therefore it doesn’t fol-
low that a profit of £16,354 to-day is the
same thing as one of the same amount three
or seven or any other number of years ago.
At present the plan is to show only the net
profit “after making provision for deprecia-
tion, etc.” ; and the extent of that provision
isn’t disclosed. This is the handy little
device that shipping cos. have for—-well, for
making their published accounts of less
value than they might be. You know that
something ought to be chipped off profits to
pay for repairs and renewals. But the
management may chip off too little, and thus
make the profits out to be greater than they
actually are ; or they may for one reason or
another want to make the profits look
smaller than they really are, and in that
case they chip off too much. If they weren’t
building or reconstructing vessels, etc., you
might get some idea from the movement of
liquid assets. But sometimes they use up
the money as fast as they put it by. In
this case watch two or three items since the
last watering :—•
June,’l4. Dec.,’l4. Dec.,’ls.
Inabilities. . ..£42,055 £9,376 £10,982
Ships, lands, etc.. . 442,465 457,204 459,150
Other assets .. 56,468 37,834 38,868
On those figures ships, .lands, wharves and
waiting-rooms are worth now about £17,000
more than they were 18 months ago; but
the surplus of other assets over liabilities
has shrunk about £16,000; so on that show-
ing the co. has done very little more than
pay its dividend. The point to then look
into is as to whether the co. has stuffed
profits away in property which doesn’t
appear on the sheet. No trace can be found
of any. Take ship property, for instance :
it stands now at £298,548 as against
£291,019 eighteen months ago. So far as
can be traced no considerable additions have
been made to the fleet. One or two of the
steamers have been overhauled, one of the
smaller ferries was reconstructed, and a
punt or two have been added for the Parra-
matta cargo-carrying ; but (setting aside the
increase in value owing to the war) it is very
doubtful indeed whether the co.’s fleet, tak-
ing wear and tear of all the vessels into
account, has increased in value more than
the £7500 which has been charged to capital
account during the year and a half. And that
is another way of saying that during this
period no profits appear to have been stuffed
away. Before capital was so liberally
watered, and when working exes, were much
smaller, no doubt profits were surreptiti-
ously dropped into a stocking; and there is
still a vague idea that the same thing is going
on now. It may be. This paper con-
fesses that it can find no evidence at all of
it.

The published accounts put it differently.
They say that the' capital is £250,000 and
the dividend therefore a trivial 35 p.c. The
apparent discrepancy is bridged over in this
way:—-
1886. —Co. registered; paid capital £46,800, plus
£16,000 in promoters’ shares.
1888. —First stock watering: £41,860 capitalised
(two shares for every three).
1889. —Second stock watering: £17,433 (one in six).
Third stock watering, £15,273 (one in
eight).
1890. —Fourth stock watering, £27,473 (one in five).
1891. —Fifth stock watering, £32,967 (one in five).
1910. Sixth stock watering, £52,194 (one in four).
Bringing the little lots together this is the
result:—
Capital actually subscribed.. £46,800
Promoters’ little lot . . 16,000
Profits capitalised . . . . 187,200
Total alleged capital . . £250,000
Certainly it was a gamble when the line was
laid. Originally Silverton was expected to
supply the bulk of the traffic; and Silverton
has been stone dead these many years. It
couldn’t keep a goat-team going, let alone a
railway. But the extension to Broken Hill
turned out a very different proposition, and
it: lias given the old shareholders their mone'y
back 50 times over.
It' is a good deal like a baker’s business.
Suppose the dough industry isn’t as profit-
able as the association of dough-makers
would like it to be. Up goes the price ;|d.
a loaf. There’s a half-hesitating kick.
Whereupon the highly moral and dreadfully
injured master bakers exclaim: “What! Are
you making all this fuss about a ha’penny a
loaf — a week on the average bread
bill?”- Then it doesn’t seem worth while,
and the threatening protest presently drops
dead. Something like that has happened
dozens of times in connection with this co.
It is the big traffic that does it. There are
nearly three-quarters of a million tons of
minerals to be carried every year; and it is
all through-traffic—there is no dropping of
a dozen of eggs here and the picking up of
a box of butter 10 miles further on. No
doubt the line built in the country where
eggs and butter grow by the wayside will
last longer. This railway will be valueless
when the Broken Hill mines peter out. No-
body can say precisely when that will be.
You can see daylight ahead in some of them
for 12 years, with all the ore below the
bottom level to the good. Yet there is an
end; and there is a much nearer end so far
as the zincy tailings are concerned —the vast
accumulations of tailings which have been
shifted at a great rate during the past seven
or eight years.
So there is going to be a tail to this co.’s
history—if the whole hindquarters are not
bitten off suddenly. That isn’t impossible.
The N.S.W. Government has started the
railway from Condobolin “towards” Broken
Hill; and as the Ministry has given its
friencf the Norton Griffiths syndicate an
undertaking to spend a certain amo.unt per
annum, and as this work is on the Norton
Griffiths schedule, the chances are the un-
profitable job Will be gone right on with.
Broken Hill is about 33 miles from the
border of S. Aus., and the connection with
S.A. rail is supplied by this Silverton Tram-
way Co.’s line. Now a dertain amount of
traffic is bound to go towards Sydney and
Lithgow and Newcastle, for special mineral
freights will be arranged ; and that won’t do
this co. any good. But that isn’t the only
centingency. The line forms part of the
great East and West track which runs from
Port Augusta to Perth ; and the question is
whether the 33 miles between the two State
Government systems will be left in the co.’s
hands. Or whether a competing line will
he built. If the latter isn’t done the
chances are that no action will be taken for
a few years after the State line from Broken
Ilill eastward is completed, as if the co. is
disturbed it must under its Act get 21 times
the average profit for the preceding seven
years. And the Government will probably
wait till the competition has pulled the
profits down a bit.

The sooner the practical S. Australian Gov-
ernment sets an independent accountant of
high standing to tell it what he thinks of
the S. Australian co-operative societies’
manner of bookkeeping and finance the
better. This paper is nearly tired of saying
that, in its opinion, the system is a danger,
and the high moral qualifications of the
directors and managements, as a rule, don’t
atlect the position at all. In this case, as
in that of the old Adelaide Co-operative
Society, shareholders are apparently entitled
to withdraw their capital and interest; and
where that capital and interest are invested
in buildings and plant and stock the rule
is liable to wreck the whole concern. Apart
from that the manner in which the accounts
of this co. are presented must make the
statements quite unintelligible to the aver-
age shareholder. According to the (un-
dated) directors’ report which accompanied
the as at May, 1915, the bal-
ance forward Nvas £274 after various appro-
priations. had been made. < This amount is
swollen in the accounts now presented to
£451, and nobody in the wide world can tell
from the' present accounts where the extra
money came from. Apparently a certain
amount of dividend was not collected (and
apparently also that amount reverted to the
co.), and apparently the amount of the
bonus on trade (5 p.c. on members’ sales of
fruit and 5 p.c. on members’ purchases of
goods) was over-stated in the proposed ‘
Appropriation. The whole thing is untidy
-and unsatisfactory, and no credit at all to
the seven directors who sign it.
This criticism, however, goes only to the
of finance and bookkeeping. The
idea back of the co. is most admir-
able. The shareholders are the fruitgrowers
on the one side and the retailers of jam and
preserved fruits on the other. Tired of
supplying unnecessary middlemen with
profits and unnecessary middlemen’s unneces-
sary agents with wages and salaries, these
two parties combined to form this co., and
its success has been so pronounced' that
every season some extension of premises and
plant bas been called for. Meantime the
utmost economy appears to be exercised in
the management. Thus the directors’ and
auditors fees for the whole 12 months
amounted to only £l3O.

The last three lines show. a pretty serious
slump in profits ; and it will be astonishing
if there isn’t more to come. So far the
recent inflation of property values in Sydney
has been maintained by wholesale borrow-
ing ; partly for military purposes, partly for
the State Labor crowd’s own ends. Every-
body knows, of course, that this borrowing
must stop presently, and instead of the lav-
ish outlay of loans there will be heavy taxa-
tion to meet the interest bill. So the tide
will not only cease to come in ; it will rush
out furiously. In that period the real estate
market is likely to suffer more keenly than
most, and R. and W.’s commission earnings
are likely to wear a much more pallid look.
Still this co. now has the whole of its capital
invested; and if it can’t earn at least 6 p.c.
nowadays there is something very wrong.
The investments ought to bring in 4J or
5 p.c., which wouldn’t leave much for the
business to earn. The real nature of the
bulk of the investments is, however, rather
doubtful.

The statements sent out by this co. are
almost valueless, as they are doubtless in-
tended to be. The co., not being under the
Gas Act except in a general way (though
the concern acrpss the street is), is under
no obligation to present accounts in the
form prescribed by that Act; and the same
old stuffing away which went on in other
cos. possibly still goes on here. On the
other hand, an insufficient amount may be
put aside to cover depreciation. There is
no telling what is done. The only things

The most conspicuous thing there perhaps
is the fine increase in the revenue; and
there is plenty of room for further move-
ment, since the limit of settlement hasn’t
yet been reached at Manly by a long way.
Though sales have increased so much the
directors say that the dividend was reduced
because “during the first half of the year
there was an appreciable falling-off in con-
sumption,” and that is given as the reason
for reducing the dividend. But if former
accounts meant what they said, the 10 p.c.
shouldn’t have been paid, because of the
wretchedly small margin left to come and go
on. However, from the fact that on one
occasion assets were written up and a bonus
was then distributed, the published figures
as before remarked, are probably not to be
taken seriously. Under a decent companies
law, let alone a comprehensive Gas Act
such documents would be impossible. They
offer far too handy a means of letting the
man inside take the outsider down if he feels
inclined to.

ALUMINOFERRTC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as .clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.

According to a statement by Treasurer
I yons the Tasmanian State Government has
decided to get into the oil business by buy-
ing the shale-oil areas of the Railton-Latrobe
Co about 1488 acres in all, estimated
hi ’contain about 6,000,000 tons of shale
and yield about 250,000,000 gallons of
oil* The Commonwealth has offered to take
5000 tons of fuel oil annually for the navy
for 10 years, though its encouragement to
the Australian industry is confined to offer-
ing a price considerably less than it now has
to” pay for foreign oil. However, the Tas.
Government reckons there will be other cus-
tomers for all the extra oil it can produce,
and at better prices than the Commonwealth
is prepared to give.
The successful treatment of the complex
zinc-lead sulphides of the Ilercules-Rose-
bery group of mines (Tas.) has been solved
once more. This time it has been done by
the Gillies electrolytic process. The trouble
has always been the zinc. When the Her-
cules ore was treated at the Zeehan smelters
the zinc was lost, and the Tas. Metals Ex-
traction Co., which spent about £120,000 in
experiments and in erecting works at Rose-
bery to treat this ore, failed. The claim
for the Gillies process, a unit of which has
been running in Melbourne under the super-
vision of the Federal Government’s expert,
is that it produces practically pure zinc
(99.91 p.c.) and leaves the lead, copper,
silver and gold in the residue in a form in
which they can be easily recovered. The
Hydro-Electric Power and Metallurgical
Co., which has the rights of the Gillies pro-
cess, is pushing ahead with the erection of
works at North-West Bay, near Hobart,
and has a contract with the Tasmanian
Government by which the latter is to supply
power for the co.’s works, giving them up
to 10,000 h.p. at about l-10d. per unit. The
supposed fly in the ointment, if the process
proves on a commercial scale what is claimed
for it, is that tfle Mount Lyell Co. has prac-
tically got hold of the three chief Rosebery
mines, the Hercules, Tasmanian Copper and
Primrose, and it is doubtful whether it will
have anything to do with the Gillies process.
It certainly won’t until Gillies can
demonstrate that his costs are all
right. Nearly 20 years ago the Ger-
man professor Schnabel, imported to make
experiments, told the Broken Hill cos. about
25 ways of recovering all their metals. There
never has been any trouble about the same
thing so far as the West Coast complex ores
are concerned; but commercial results are
quite another matter. And unfortunately
it has been on commercial results that the
delightfully optimistic Gillies has been weak-
est ever since he started in on this zinc
business at B. 11. Block 10.
* * * *
Whole-hearted efforts have lately been
made in the lvalgoorlie-Boulder mines to
minimise the dust evil. Before long every
working face in the Great Boulder will be
equipped with sprays; Boulder Perseverance
is similarly provided in various parts of the
present workings, and the Kalgurli has also
made great strides in the same direction.
Improvements have been effected at six
levels in the Ivanhoe, and at the South Kal-
gurli dust-laying appliances are in use at
eight levels. The Golden Horseshoe, Lake
View and Star and Associated have also
greatly improved underground conditions.
Attention is now being given to dust in the
mills and surface workings. A number of
remedies have been tested and some have
already given good results.
* * * *
Getting little else but gibes about being
“a pub- and st*>re-keeper’s push,” the Lease-
holders and Prospectors’ Association (W.A.)
lias given the Labor Government up as hope-
loss, and has induced the Kalgoorlie muni-
cipal council to convene a conference to con-
sider the best means and ways of giving the
mining industry a leg-up. It is proposed
to invite goldfields’ delegates from all parts
of the State and to renew the agitation for
the establishment of mining boards, reorga-
nisation of the State battery system and a
more sympathetic administration of both the
Mines and Water Supply Departments in
the direction of giving greater assistance to
the struggling claim-holder.
* * * #
“Oof Bird” on tour :
Recently the Edna May Central located a 3ft.
parallel reef 200 ft. distant from the main workings
at the No. 1 level. The reef widened in driving and
turned south towards the greenstone country Those
who were blessed with an Edna May education reck-
oned that an enrichment was likely should the reef
come into contact with that class of rock, and they
made a bulldog rush to square their bear accounts.
So far, it looks as though they were right, as the
new reef is now about 12ft. wide, worth lOdwt., and
looks promising. A crosscut is to be extended south
from the No. 2 level to pick up the continuation of
the shoot, and if it succeeds in doing so the Central
will look better than it has »ever done—
which is not necessarily saying much. The Central
has always been successful in striking new ore bodies
at their richest point, and the rich values subse-
quently reported have always grown painfully less
as time rolled on. Still, in this case, the co. seems
to have located something very promising. It is
good that something worth mentioning has been
located, as the property would have been starving for
the want of ore very soon.
Barrackers of Edna May stock were delighted with
the success of the new electric pumping installa-
tion, but they are intoxicated over the appearance
of the No. 4 level. Here a reef 21ft. in width and
worth 30dwt. has been exposed with no sign of a
footwall. The ore body is looking bigger and better
than at any other level, and those above were far
from being wild-cats. The stone at No. 4 level is
free milling, and gold can easily be seen in the face.
Undoubtedly the development is something out of
the ordinary. However, to those who think the stock
good bearing, a word of advice is given: Inspect, or
consult someone who knows a mine, before operat-
ing. If then unable to shake off the bearish feeling
see a doctor.
Edna May Consolidated recently reported: “Struck
ore carrying values,’’ and shares jumped from Is. 9d.
to 3s. 9d. Those who sold at 3s. 9d. are not worry-
ing, as a report on the following day said: “Ore only
7in. thick.” That is mining!
Edna May Deeps announced the other day that the
last section of shaft cost £25 per foot. Just so;
but will the Deeps truthfully say what it cost to sink
the shaft from 270 ft. to 330 ft. ; and also, in view
of the fact that a treatment plant is being erected,
what ore reserves are in sight? The Maori Lass
holds the record for Yilgarn with Q tons of ore
reserves on completion of a mill. Has the Deeps as
much as that?
* » * *
Fremantle Trading (Smelting) Co.
(W.A.) has made a start at both its
Northampton lead mines, and expects to
turn out pig-lead next month. The first
year’s estimated output of 5000 tons has
been sold to the Imperial Government, and
for the first time in its history the co. looks
like entering upon a profitable career.
Efforts are being made in Melbourne and
Adelaide to raise money to equip the old
Mount Rankin mine, eight miles south-west
of Southern Cross (W.A.). The show is
faced with the same difficulty as the Broncho
as regards water; but from all accounts it
is worth a trial, though not justifying e±pen-
diture on a mill.
» * * *
After about 18 months of hard work, the
No. 4 level of the Commodore, Meekatharra
(W.A.), disclosed nothing more than a few
veinlets of ore, and these have been of a
quality guaranteed to make a manager’s hair
curl with vexation at a time when jobs aren’t
too plentiful. The co.’s finances have been
handled with a lot of care, so the show has
a cash credit of about £3OOO, besides 17,000
tons of tailings averaging about 16s. per
ton. There must be a few shillings per ton
profit to be made in that quarter.
Most of the Kalgoorlie outputs for
January are considerably reduced as a result
of the recent woodline strike. The Boulder
is down to less than one-half the usual
monthly average, the Associated about the
same, the Kalgurli just a shade better, while
the Perseverance has shrunk to little more
than one-fourth its average.
* * * *
Bodangora, Mitchell’s Creek (N.S.W.),
has declared its first dividend of 6d. per
share. This will be a pleasant surprise to
many of the shareholders, who had assumed
that the co.’s obligations would be fully
met before any distribution was made.
The record is creditable to Victorian
management. Three years ago the mine
was an abandoned derelict. A Sydney
company spent several thousand pounds
on it without being able to show a profit.
The Victorian’s success is all the more satis-
factory because the revenue has been largely
earned out of exploratory work. System-
atic stoping work has hardly been started
yet.
Six months’ milling work shows 2985
tons handled, with £12,130 won from the
rock at a cost of 455. 7d. per ton. At No.
9 level the reef has lately been cut worth
82s. Values grade off at No. 8 away from
the shaft; starting at 765. for the first
400 ft., they ease to 58s. in the next 100 ft.,
and then fall to 295. for 160 ft. As W. J.
Nicliol will draw the bulk of his stone from
this level in the current term, his average
ought to be on the oz. line. He hasn’t a big
reef to play with, so he will have to hustle
on with No. 9to keep ore reserves up. Still
the mine is much better opened than ever
before, and battery work will be on a bigger
scale. The co.’s only debt is £5250 to the
old Sydney owners, payable at the rate of
50 p.c. of the net profits.
Vegetable Creek Tin (N.S.W.) notifies its
shareholders that the debt, originally
£25,000, to the ground-landlord has now been
wiped off. Originally this concern handed
over 30 p.c. of its tin oxide, but that was
subsequently reduced to 25 p.c.; and then an
arrangement was made to pay the vendors
an amount equal to the dividends paid to
shareholders. The 6d. just declared absorbs
£3250, and this year’s distributions must
depend on* the length of the present block-
ing-out campaign. If Vegetable Creek
had gone ahead with development work and
had pushed its leading drives into unexplored
payable areas there would have been con-
tinuous blocking out, but unfortunately that
has not been done.
* * * *
Goobaragandra gold mine, in the mountain
'fastnesses south of Tumut (N.S.W.), has
sent three bulk samples to the Kembla works
for these results: No. 1 reef, 2oz. 16dwt.;
No. 2 reef. soz. 19dwt.; No. 3 reef, 3oz.
15dwt: The co. is prospecting on three
parallel reefs with “satisfactory results”
from all. The battery which is on its way
to the mine is to be ready by May 1, and
then the tale will be told. But can’t the
name be altered? On ’Change it’s already
Gooseandgander. Perhaps the local name
would do. It is Stinking Creek.
Victoria Consols was one of the few Ben-
digonians to earn more than a crust last
half-year. This was achieved by jumping
off dirt at the first sign of impoverishment,
and then giving the tributor a chance to
make wages. The co. requires up-to-date
plant to do better, but it regards such things
as luxuries, even though they would speed
up matters at 512 ft. There is plenty of
stone overhead, but the risk of its being
low grade seems to make the management
hang off rising or driving intermediate levels.
The other string on which the tributor plays
his tune is at 640 ft. Here he digs into a
saddle 10ft. wide and Oft. thick, making
south. But he won’t be the man in posses-
sion long should the reef start to rise, for
that’s when the co. looks for another run of
payable stuff.
* * * *
The Victoria United, Ballarat East, re-
float hangs fire because the average miner
knows the game just as well as any pro-
moter. For years the mine hovered so near
to the border-fine of ldiss that it eventually
became the home of tributors and low-wage
men. To bump up wages meant insolvency
or calls, and the English scrip-holders
weren’t going on with a mine that couldn’t
retuxm interest. The attempt to raise fresh
capital to bump Bull out is reminiscent of
the Long Tunnel experience, which ended in
the group of shareholders who thought they
knew more than the old hands having to part
up solidly for their excursioning. Unless
the Victoria United is to be run as a charity
concern, the best thing to do is to bring it
to the hammer.
* * * *
Great Fitzro.v, the Queensland mine
which has been consistent in a long series of
failures, despite the tons of money which
have been poured into it, the nmst skilled
engineering and the most perfect mechanical
appliances, is likely to have another lease
of life, thanks to the war-inflated value of
copper and to assistance from the Govern-
ment. Debenture-holders had taken posses-
sion of the mine and had seemingly decided
that it was a hopeless proposition, as all the
extensive plant was advertised for sale by
auction. Following the precedent of
Premier Holman in the Great Cobar case,
the Queensland Premier is now negotiating
with the Fitzroy debenture-holders with a
view to making financial arrangements fok
the resumption of operations. When copper
was near the £6O mark it was reported that
the mine was within an ace of being pay-
able. Now that the metal is over £lOO a
handsome profit should be possible. As it is
almost a moral certainty that high values
will continue for a long time the marvel
is that the debenture-holders should have
thought of abandoning the mine or of asking
the Government or anyone else to join them
in sharing the prospective success.
* * * *
Papuan Brilliant and Victoria (Charters
Towers) has declared a 3d. dividend. This
co. paid six dividends last year, aggregating
£7200 (Is. 6d. per share). Sinking has now
been resumed on the cross reef, but the pre-
sent bottom is in low-grade stone. The
deepest level (No. 4) has been driven 170 ft,
from the underlay shaft, at which point
there were a few inches of payable stone in
the face. It is hoped that a new shoot will
be developed, but the Moonstone and its
cross reef are notoriously patchy. The
latest return was £1934 from 273 tons of
stone.
* * * *
The New Minnie Moxham, Northcote
(N.Q.), has an overflowing dam and a reef
that promises some good returns. A good
shoot of stone has been developed above No.
1 level, which has been driven 130 ft. A
winze is down 60ft. below No. 1 level, and
driving is now in progress from the bottom.
The battery crushed 136 tons of stone for
2470 z. 7dwt., valued at £989.
* * * *
Of the 1,181.006 shares in Broken Hill
Proprietary 782,454 are on the London reg-
ister ; Adelaide holds 177,149; Melbourne.
171,374; and Sydney 40,007. Since 1914
London holding has decreased by 44,000, and
Adelaide by 9137, while Melbourne has in-
creased by 55,150. It can hardly be con-
sidered satisfactory that nearly 70 p.c. of
the shares in Australia’s big mine should be
held by outsiders.

plaintiff’s costs added to its worries.
