Sydney, February 21, 1916.
My Dear Arini. —
A son of the late Marquis of Linlithgow
—so popular as G.-G. Hopetoun—who now
reigns in father’s stead, has given the big
house near Edinburgh as a soldiers’ hos-
pital.
The Queen 'of Sweden, whose pic-
ture appeared in recent illustrated papers,
is in appearance very like Mrs. Ivil-
iani. The latter high-browed lady was
a Swede and wife of the Consul-General
for Germany here when his royal master
began to smash up Europe. As you know,
the Queen of Sweden is the German lady
who sent her blessing, by wire, to the
“Fatherland ” when the guns began to
boom.
Colonel Alexander, of the Punjaub regi-
ment, was amongst the injured in the Tas-
manian railway accident. He was taking
a hurried look at the Jam Island when the
derailed train tipped him out. A broken arm
is his share of the trouble.
Justice Heydon had the luck to leave
Tasmania before the train smash which
shook up so many trippers. He and his
verse-writing lady are back in Sydney.
The Women's National Council —annual
corroboree —meets this year in Hobart. The
committee thoughtfully picked the early days
of March for the fixture. Such questions
as “What are you prepared to do in war-
time?” will be asked at the point of the
knitting-needle.
Trooper W. S. Blue, who died some weeks
ago at Alexandria, was a stepson of the late
Chief Justice Way, of South Australia. In
the pre-war days he was a member of the
Adelaide Hunt Club and a horseman of
note.
Perth's Anglican Archbishop Riley, who
came this way a while ago, has a son in one
of the German prison camps. He had com-
parative luck in his captivity, and was better
treated than most prisoners by the gentle
enemy. He is at Crefeld —or was some weeks
ago.
An Australian woman just out from Zep-
seeing in England remarks: "“Round Win-
chester there are camps containing in all
not less than 20,000 men in training. Yet
the rare sight of a drunken man in uniform
is considered a local scandal. Good feed-
ing. strict discipline and a wet canteen,
backed up by tea and coffee tents, are the
main causes of this happy state of affaii’s.”
The organising of voluntary workers to
run up huts and clear the land for wounded
soldiers is progressing at French’s Forest.
The idea is better than the soil, which is
medium to bad. Difficulty of access and
lack of water are the main drawbacks to
success on the hills beyond the Spit. On
Saturday afternoon about 340 stalwart men
e\acuated their coats to smite the uninspir-
ing earth with spade and mattock. Dr.
Mary Booth has selected two plots for sol-
diers’ widows, and has under her unroving
eye a party of Harbor Trust men who are
putting their muscle into their preparation.
North Sydney gas employees, who are also
on the French’s Forest reclamation, go two
hours earlier to their ordinary work on
Saturday, so as to make an early dash for
the site of their special labors. These Tro-
jans also give up their Sundays to the spade
work. Miss Tindall has rounded up the
Women’s Blue Gum Association, whose hap-
piness it is to succor the patriotic laborer’s
on the job with nourishment.
Miss Brace, the horticultural enthusiast,
just back from Europe and America, went
over the French’s Forest scrub land on
Saturday. She has offered to take over a
five-acre plot, and lay it out (just for love)
for a soldier’s widow. Miss Brace claims
that three acres are quite enough for a
woman and her children to deal with. In-
tensive culture on the French system is her
idea. Meanwhile jour prayers for an im-
proved rainfall at Fi'ench’s Forest are
urgently required.
The “Under Fire” matinge for returned
soldiers had a sweltering afternoon to see
it through. Hugh Ward decorated a box
with Colonel Bob Owen. The Wollongong
soldier is still lame after his damages in
Gallipoli. A slender Mrs. Bob and small
daughter helped to swell the applause when
the last act brought the retreat (off stage)
of the German army. A J.C.W. chorus and
the principals from Her Majesty’s tossed a
few ditties off their chests before the new
curtain rolled up. It’s a handsome bit of
crushed rose-tango drapery, in place of the
shabby old red velvet veteran. Frank
Harvey, who is taking; Julius Knight’s old
place as matin6e idolj looked “positively
beautiful” (I quote something tender with
its hair in a plait) as Larry Redmond.
Are the pork-pie hats really coming in
again? This headgear, like a small pan-
cake laid on an inch or two of foundation,
made a furtive bid for favor in 1915. Un-
fortunately, ladies with faces like' the desert
moon, were the chief votaries, so the pie
was cooked. Easter may revive the pill-box
size in millinery. But if it does, Heaven
help the heavyweight dames who perch these
trifles on deck.
Swimmer W. Longworth has lately been
thrilling the girls by showing his manly
form in khaki. Several recent tea-parties
in a fashionable hostel were quite excited
by the vision of “Billy” in uniform, reduc-
ing a pyramid of buns in a handy corner.
Recently-deceased W. L. Hackett, of
W.A., was 64 when he married (10 years
ago) Deborah, a daughter of surveyor Fred
Slade Brockman. Bride was 19 at the time,
and has done her duty very well in the
matter of the birthrate. Four youthful
Hacketts carry on the enterprising old man’s
name.
An Ashfield girl, Pauline Walker, will
allow H. Waller Muddle (now at the Front)
to regard her as his very own girl when he
comes marching home. Grandpa Walker,
now passed over the shadowy border, was
one time Chief of the musty old Public
Library. Another relative, also well
remembered here, was the late Critchett
Walker, principal Under-Sec. of the Ma
State.
That fine old sport. Surgeon-Colonel
Fiasehi, invalided from Lemnos, has put up
a good recovery in a London hospital. Just
before the drums began to beat, the Sydney
surgeon had mastered the tango and mar-
ried his second wife. Then the tango gave
place to a warlike bound, and the tall, sol-
dierly figure of Fiasehi did a lightning
change into khaki.
Governor Stanley’s Private Sec.. Victor
Hood, spent his short N.S.W. holiday car-
eering off to Canberra. After a hurried look
at Duntroon’s soldier boys, he motored to
Jervis Bay, where in future Jellicoes are to
be turned out. Government official Percy
Owen was his chaperon.
A former Neutral Bay doctor, W. Mac-
donald. was packing up his stethoscope for
the Front when the last mail left London.
Another Mac, Captain G. McDonald, of
the R.A.A., in training at the British end,
had a few days’ leave in London before
going to meet the shrapnel. He had a long
look at the Welsh hills first, having been on
garrison duty in Flewelhvyn land.
The late Mrs. Walter Hall was passion-
ately fond of children. With never a
chick of their own, the millionaire
and his wife rarely missed a chance
of making some youngster happier for their
wealth. There was »a certain frugality
in their personal expenditure. At a
fashionable concert, with the guinea seats
and the “paper” in the front rows, the Hall
couple generally got 10s. worth of music
apiece behind the first barrier. She, kind
soul, was the despair of smart dressmakers,
and her gown, in the days when frocks were
fastened finally over one shoulder, was often
a frank witness of an unsuccessful struggle
to mate the hooks and eyelets.
The trustees who administer the Walter
Hall bequest cared nothing for these matters
of unnecessary detail. They had to deal with
the Woman. A mixture of shrewdness and sim-
plicity, Mrs. Hall was keen as a needle in
the management of her affairs. “Her quick
intuition picked up a point,” says one trus-
tee, “while we, who considered ourselves
smart men of business, were fumbling for
facts.” In all her personal charities she
got a fast sprint on when there were women
and kiddies to be cared for.
Solicitor W. F. Brennan’s tall son Billy
is now a joyous lieutenant at one of the
various Fronts. Egypt was the lad’s jump-
ing-off place. Billy Brennan, who used to
be on a station beyond Moree. is a handy
man with a buckjumper He has a chance
to keep his hand in with his present regi-
ment —the Light Horse.
The late Justice R. E. O’Connor’s son,
Alan, has had a long whack of training
with the Leinsters in Ireland. He is likely
to be in the firing-line any day this month,
but he might have got there sooner if he
had stuck to his Australian mates.
The Cecil Horderns, who have two sons in
the firing-line, chose the better part. They
hurled ostentation out of the window for
their daughter Vera’s wedding. Only rela-
tives and old friends were asked to see the
cake cut at Petty’s. Vera went to her happi-
ness in a tailor-built outfit of cream poplin.
Lavender millinery with a white-lace veil
topped the bridal plumage. Sister Enid was
the only bridesmaid. The ’groom is Warren
Roberts, of Booligal station, N.S.W.
Manager Freddie Shipman and his auburn-
haired lass (Mrs. Freddie) are hereabouts.
They have lassoed Paul Dufault as a 1916
attraction.
Printer W. Brooks’s daughter went off
to England in a very full ship—the Medina.
The Sydney girl expects to find her young
man waiting on the wharf when the boat
ties up at London’s murky docks. He is a
Dr. Roberts, who’s been working with the
11.A.M.C. on the Western Front.
Cabled that F.R.A.S. is the tail that will
henceforth accompany Mary Adelaide Proc-
tor, the star-gazing daughter of the late
astronomer Richard Proctor. One of her
earliest remembrances is of being snatched
from her cot on a winter’s night, wrapped
in a blanket and introduced to a careering
comet that had escaped from the celestial
pound. -Since then rounding up the stars
has been her life’s work. When she lectured
in Australia a while ago the public didn’t
rush the discourses, but the astronomer
made a host of friends. She was in Eng-
land when the last news came this way.
The patriotic outing at Rose Bay on
March 18, in the water-lapped Motor-Boat
Club’s grounds, will tempt the generous
visitor with everything from fatted cream
puffs to guessing competitions. If that range
is not sufficiently extensive additions will be
made to suit customers at the usual rates.
The miserable claw of destiny struck a
party of Y.W.C.A. dames in Tasmania the
other day when a railw.ay smash occurred,
Delegates from the Association had gathered
at a summer corroboree at Brown's River,
and some of them were travelling on the
derailed trajn. The list of killed included
Mrs. Howell, president of the Geelong
Y.W.C.A.
Olive, the pretty daughter of Kelso King,
has been getting more than a fair share of
excitement while doing ambulance work in
France and in the Balkans. It is fretful
to spirited girls to stay at home and merely
construct socks or compose jam, instead of
being an angel aboard a motor ambu-
lance transporting wounded heroes, but we
all can’t go to the Front. If we did our
ears would blush at what the trench-boys
say about the socks and the jam from the
girls who stay at home.
Director Verbrugghen, with Judge Back-
house and Under-Sec. Board, shook hands
with the imported Conservatorium quartette
the other afternoon. Much interest is growing
in the French concert to be held in the Gov-
ernment House ex-stables, where the im-
portations will probably make their appear-
ance.
Mrs. Slap, has followed the steps of other
well-known vocalists and is warbling
across the music-hall footlights. The Tivoli
is her present address.
Greatly daring, the redoubtable “Snowy”
Baker is organising a beauty competition.
His last effort to collect Yenuses at a charity
show caused him a stack of trouble. Ladies
who considered themselves much handsomer
than the hussies who got the prizes wrnited
on the physical culture man to tell him
what they thought about it. Yet he wasn’t
even referee for the event. Undaunted,
Snowy is at it again. This time the fair
and unfair will show their points in
“Canadians” at the big patriotic carnival
on March 10 and 11. Proceeds go to the
Light Horse Comforts Fund.
The “Women’s Work in War Time”
exhibition is postponed till September. The
domestic side of the feminine creature will
then show its full capabilities.
Easter is mercifully late this year.
Woman will not therefore have to swelter
unduly in her best autumn frock, which
she insists on wearing in Show week. The
G.-G.’s ukase re races will not move our
Best People from pursuing the race-card at
Randwick. Since the Denmans’ departure
Sydney has been minus sporting Governors,
anyhow. So Potts Point is now accustomed
to back the losers without the assistance of
Gentleman Helps.
Next Saturday, February 2G. the police
and fire-brigade staffs will co-operate in rais-
ing money “to help the hospitals.” As usual
at these shows, the Roman chariot race is
likely to be the most hair-raising event.
Horatius Bluegum, in a short, red petti-
coat, bustling Marcus Superbus Suburbs, in
white ditto, round the ring ought not to
be missed. The Agricultural Ground is the
arena.
The Tivoli matinee for the benefit of the
widow of comedian Ranier was well managed.
Short, crisp items and bright artists put
the show much above the usual “compli-
mentary” performance. Dorothy Brunton,
in a frock of azure blue, encouraged C. H.
Workman to deliver a specially-good helping
of “So Long, Letty.” Peter Dawson and his
plump charmer, Annette George (who is Mrs.
Peter on the domestic hearth) sang a duet
from the Barcarolle, and for once the hack-
neyed lilt had the genuine operatic swing.
Field Fisher, Miss Nadje (the lady athlete
who ties her limbs in knots) and Connie
Ediss also improved the afternoon. Connie,
gowned in diaphanous draperv. floated from
the wing like an amiable balloon to do her
turn. The Hugh Mclntosh lady, with Mrs.
Corbett and Mrs. Wright as runners-up,
arranged the show.
A proud aunt remarks that her nephew,
Jack Dyer, is now one of the voungest
majors in the King’s uniform. The Si dney
boy. who is only 22, left here as 2nd Lieut,
in the Engineers, landed at Gallipoli on his-
toric April 20. Promoted to a captaincy
and mentioned in despatches, young Dyer
was spared to come through the Great Flit
in December. He got his majority on his
22nd birthday.
Dr. Roger Cope, of Sydney, has been
making a name for himself in the north of
France, where his surgical skill has
brought him into much prominence. A Bel-
gian soldier, who begged to have his shat-
tered arm saved at any cost, now wears a
sheep-bone in his wing, and when he speaks
of the Australian surgeon who did the job
he stands uncovered.
At a matinee the other day Marie Eaßn,
of the J.C.W. team, was braced up to the
chin in shot peacock silk plumage. The
sleeves were in quite another color and
seemed to have no communication with the
greenish-blue cuirass bodice. Marie man-
aged to sing over this lingerie fence, but it
didn’t appear to be easy.

Melba steps off a Vancouver boat about
March 7. The Skylark has had a few thrills
in Canada, having been more or less ad-
jacent when Ottawa's Parliament House went
up in smoke. Our Chief Warbler has been
staying with the vice-regal Connaughts.
Mrs. O’Connor, widow of Morgan O’Con-
nor, M.D., an old identity of Wagga, passed
out a week or so ago at her home at Dar-
linghurst.
Two Sydney boys, Stan and Rupert King
are getting into khaki on the other side.’
Stanley is a medical student. But he hopes
to be a full private in the R.A.M.C.
Mrs. M. C. King Waldron, of Sydney
who died in South Africa recently while on
a visit to her daughter, was the widow of
a former City Solicitor and one of the many
daughters of the late George Dibbs. Mrs.
Waldron’s daughter married Sergeant
Reichenbach, who is helping Botha and
Smuts wipe out the Germans.
After much advancing and retreating and
ccunter-marcliing through a maze of corres-
pondence, a settlement regarding the sol-
diers’ tea garden is in sight. Minister
Crahame has given the dames of the com-
mittee a lease of a plot of ground in
the Domain. The Government has also
shelled out £l4OO for the buildings. As
Anzac Buffets are springing up all over the
city (not to mention a suburban crop), per-
haps “Tea Garden” will serve as a label
for this adventure.

Barrack-street) is prepared to take your
last half-crown this week, or you can trans-
form the coin into canned fruit or sweets
for Egypt as Easter gifts to General Booth’s
energetic section there.
The war hasn’t knocked out that highly
respectable and venerable institution, the
Philharmonic Society 7. It mentioned the
other day that it was feeling genteelly elated
over a credit balance of about £l5O/
> Mrs. 10. Wilder, a well-known Hawkes
Bay (M.L.) resident, spent Christmas in
England. She hurried there to help the con-
valescence of a son who was wounded in
Gallipoli. Lieut. Wilder's brother John was
killed on that tragic cliff.
The Red Cross Society is taking over the
management of Graythwaite (the soldiers’
convalescent home) forthwith.
Most Australian girls have a fairly thick
growth of hair, but the swimming damsels,
and especially those who bare their locks
to sun and salt wave, are especially rich in
what the unwashed poets call “woman’s
glory." But otherwise the mermaids who
competed in the recent carnival are out of
the beauty class. Exposure to sun and wind
dries their complexions and tans it to a
leathery substance. If you take note, too,
you’ll observe the heavy chin that aquatics
develop in the feminine. Fanny Durack,
who has .a handsome mop of tawny hair,
rolls it up in door-knob style.
„C. Beresford Cairns, a son of a forme!
X.S.W. banker, is in England. He’s trailing
up a commission in the R.F.A.
Gerald Griffin, the Irish singer at the
Adelphi. wastes some of his sweetness on a
desert of indifferent music. Such dulcet
tones might be employed to better advantage
than in lays like “Tura-lura” (or something
of the kind), “the song my mother sang to
me.”
That centenarian charity, the Benevolent
Society, holds its 103rd annual meeting this
week. Governor Strickland presides over
the tally of debit and credit accounts.
Yours affectionately.
VANDORIAN.
Someone in Melbourne is hatching a new
sort of war baby—the Middle Class League
—to be composed of all persons not rich
enough to belong to Liberal Leagues or
Trades Unions. Apparently its policy is
to be opposition to freetrade. dear food, high
rents and Government waste. Its name is
another way of spelling disaster in a good
cause.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, February 21, 1916.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The autumn meeting, which opened at
Caulfield on Saturday, was not blessed
by vice-regals. They stayed away, and
the barren waste of empty sacred
seats stared reproachfully at the sports
who still dare to buckle on their field-
glasses. In the surrounding gloom the
bookies seemed to apologise for taking the
punters’ money; winter costumes covered the
Very Best backs, and a large number of
wounded soldiers looked as if they didn’t care
whether they were flapper-nursed again or
not.
The gracious Dolores went out and sang
to the soldiers at Royal Park last Wednes-
day night. She is “out of business,” as she
expresses it, until after the war, and only
patriotism or charity will induce her to lift
up her voice. The long rest from money-
earning brings its artistic compensations in
the refreshed charms of her warble. The
sympathies of Dolores are widely distributed
among the Allies —a French mother and a
home in France; an Italian father, and a
thoroughly British sympathy.
The Australian Motor Transporters in
France coo-ee back thanks fot- some presents.
Many packets were sent and a fair per-
centage of them arrived, which is vei’y in-
consistent, all things considered. A parcel
of good things was convoyed by Mrs. James,
a squattress of hereabouts, who went to
France to be near her husband the Major.
Someone galloped down to the boat before it
left and heaved a bundle after her, shouting
that it was “Something for the boys!” If
you listen intently you will hear the echo of
the boys’ cheers.
Adrian Knox, K.C., is little the worse for
his five months’ service as Red Cross Com-
missioner. The chairman of the A.J.C.
visited Anzac and Lemnos, and returned
satisfied that* any gifts sent along to our
lads should leave by the first boat. The
stories about Red Cross goods being sold
illicitly are he says, romance, and Molly can
rest assured when she turns the last toe of
Claude’s socks that the boy won’t have to
pay three and six for them at the canteen.
.The West Brighton Club is an exclusive
man-haunt of great age and intense respect-
ability. Lots of people have never heard
of it because, you see, most of the members
belong to the sacred Melbourne, or Athen-
aeum, or Australian, and such refuges are off
the average citizen’s beat. The crusted con-
cern came out of its cave last week and gave
a party to which ladies were among the in-
vited. All sorts of patriotic dodges were
resorted to, and a heap of money was raised
for the Red Cross.
The John Langs, now of Eurack, in the
Colac country, opened their flower-garden
and shrubbery to the Western District public
the other afternoon. Johnstone, the still-
new member for that region, did his duty by
handing over a flag to be auctioned. The
garden-party panned out over £lOO to one of
the war funds.
Miss Hook, a Riverina girl with a Burne-
Jones face and a mouse-like manner, has
come to town to try and fipd some patriotic
work. She is a grand-daughter of Mitchell,
senior, of Tabletop and other Riverina
stations. The old gentleman is in his 90’s,
but is quite satisfied.
Fanny Dennys, a member of the old Gee-
long family, is helping the Empire by serv-
ing sandwiches to the industrial soldiers at
Woolwich Arsenal.
Geoffrey Horsfall, a young English cousin
of the N. S. Wales Horsfalls, has been killed
in action. He was in Westralia when the
war bugles blew, and enlisted at once in the
W.A. Light Horse as a private, thereby
giving an object-lesson to many half-Aus-
tralians. He leaves a young wiaow and two
babies. Social Melbourne remembers him,
amongst other of his virtues, as an agree-
able race-time visitor.
Major Charlie Mason, son of the late
Speaker, is returning from England, where
he went to be nursed after being peppered
rather heavily with shrapnel at the Gal-
lipoli Landing. He got his first experience
of war in South Africa, and was Associate
to Justice Hodges, when the call to arms
hurried him back into khaki with a cap-
taincy. Mrs. Charlie has been looking after
the veteran of two campaigns ever since he
reached London.
Mrs. Alec Landale’s Algy, having put his
Gippsland wool acres out to nurse, is hurry-
ing to England to help settle the war—this
at a time when Australia is trying to make
up that extra 50,000 and wants all its own
men. Brother Douglas fell in action last
year; but Major George and Lieutenant
Gordon Landale are still in the thick of
trouble, not to mention brother-in-law Cyril,
who is doing his bit. Mrs. Algy (who was
Jean Langtree) will also make the voyage,
and will, if possible, do some nursing. But,
strange to say, they don’t deliver over
sick warriors to the care of any untrained
but willing woman in a white cap and apron.
Such are mostly rewarded, if they persist
and really want to help, with humble but
none-the-less-useful jobs which would other-
wise take up the time of trained specialists.
One officer’s daughter gets no nearer to the
battered heroes than the wardrobe which she
sorts and folds in the hospital laundry.
Scott Sharp, of Berwick, thinks he is
rapidly qualifying for war-ambulance work.
Debarred from active service by an earlier
fight with rheumatic fever, he has been Red-
Crossing valiantly. Recently he has chanced
on three separate civilian accidents that were
as desperate as battlefield cases, and has
made speed-limit dashes with them in his
motor over corduroy and blue-metal to the
nearest hospital.
The French Red Cross snips and stitches
every Tuesday and Friday in Commerce
House, Flinders-street, in a long white room
where tables gleam with white oilcloth cov-
erings. These Frenchwomen are always
thorough and thrifty, and they just frighten
the life out of a timid Australian girl if she
attempts to throw away a thread of cotton.
Mrs. Homery and Mrs. Crevelli are two of
the bosses.
The Wounded Soldiers’ Club in the Cen-
treway came out in a rash of bunting when
Mrs. Sanderson gave her party. She sang
duets with actor Tommy Shelford and in-
sisted on some of the soldiers performing.
One whistled for about half an hour on end
and won an avalanche of applause.
Tennis-champion Rod Heath has got his
aviator’s certificate in England and is going
aloft.
Adelaide, chokingly, out of the smoke: —
It was hot enough before the hills set themselves
on fire; after that it was hotter. Some 500 soldiers
from the Mitcham camp eventually settled one big
blaze with green boughs and language and other
appliances.
Just before the world lit up properly, Payneham
indulged in a patriotic carnival, which commenced
operations with a credit balance of £3OO and was
opened by the Governor. That is about the total
of the week’s dissipation. You will see by this
that the place is rapidly becoming one of the world’s
playgrounds.
There has been an exodus to Melbourne. The
Makins, Mrs. Davies Thomas and Mrs. Ernest Good
have gone; also Mrs. Edmund Fosbery. Hubby is
to follow in a week or two. No one who can pos-
sibly get out of Adelaide for a holiday stays in it.
Miss Merfe Robertson, the Elder Scholar, is to
leave by the first boat in March. A complimentary
concert will occur at the Town Hall on the first of
that month.
An interesting shooting accident is reported from
the north, where a man was killed in mistake for a
kangaroo. The men concerned were shooting at a
clam on Euridna station, and were at opposite ends,
behind brush breaks, waiting for the kangaroos to
come to water. They had arranged for a signal if
either left the shelter of his break, and when, late
at night, one of them saw something moving at the
far end of the dam and heard no signal he took it
for granted it was a kangaroo, and fired, killing his
fellow-sportsman.
Premier and Mrs. Vaughan have come back from
Kangaroo Island singing Te Deums in praise of
its glories and full of plans for its future —-including
increased tourist facilities and greater attention to
the island’s agricultural possibilities. To the un-
sociable, one of the present charms of the island is
that there are no tourist facilities —neither mails,
telephones nor accommodation. It only runs two
mails a week, and less than that for anyone not
within walking distance of Kingscote or Hog Bay.
Miss MacPherson, who was responsible for the
purchase of the much-discussed “Sowing New Seed,”
has written from England to say that she has a
dealer’s assurance that after the war the picture
will be worth twice as much as the price she paid
for it on behalf of the Art Gallery. Let us hope
it will. At present it is hiding in the Board-room
of the Public Library.
Recently-deceased Herbert Holder was one of the
best bass vocalists this State gave birth to, his voice
having the genuine quality. He had not been heard
very much lately, his only recent appearance being
his singing at the Martha Bruggemann home-coming
concert. A brother of politician Frederick, after-
wards Speaker of the Federal House, he left the
civil service to follow music, and did well, support-
ing Antoinette Stirling in her tour of the' Com-
monwealth and Maoriland in 1893, and scoring more
press notices than his principal, who mainly con-
fined her attention to ballads, while his fine voice
was shown off in operatic and classical numbers.
Kathleen, his one daughter, is a fine pianist.
Mrs. Arthur Palmer, of Bendigo, is now
thanking her stars and a bald-headed cab-
man to whom time meant nothing. She was
bound to Hobart to attend a Women’s Con-
ference. Her luggage was safely aboard the
Loongana when she hired the gradual cabbie
to drive her to the wharf. He drove so
dilatorily that she missed the boat; also the
boat-train at the other end which went to
smash. I understand the cabman is to be
presented with an illuminated address and a
purse of sovereigns for his splendid ineffi-
ciency.
Harry Marks, the Bendigo auctioneer who
helped with the famous Easter Fair for
many years, died suddenly the other day
while reading the account of the Tasmanian
railway accident, in which Miss Margaret
Karras, a next-door neighbor, lost her life.
Greta Garnett, the jolly 18-year-old Eng-
lish girl who was staying with her Australian
relations before Armageddon leaped off
its heels, is engaged to a young military man
who, after being badly wounded, escaped the
undertaker by careful nursing. He was men-
tioned in despatches and won the Military
Cross. All these particulars are supplied by
his delighted fiancee to her former Melbourne
playmates. He is soon to get his captaincy
and then they will be married. (Three
cheers!) Bobbie Tennant, a young cousin
of Greta’s who was out Here, has blossomed
into a captain at 19 years of age. He was
the only officer left in his regiment and was
in sole command for over 50 hours in the
trenches. What doesn’t England owe to
these boys!
Jack Dunlop, after many years of patrol
duty on the Block, has chanced matrimony.
He and his bride (Miss Whitty) are honey-
mooning to America, via Honolulu. _ The
bridegroom partnered in a laud ami building
business witli the gorgeous Harold Hunt.
Helen Pearson, eldest daughter of the W.
Pearsons, of Ivilmenny Park, Sale, is booked
to marry Mr. Russell, son of the general
manager of the Bank of Australasia, London.
He is heir to a title of some sort, but that’s
not his fault. Mimf, the second Pearson
daughter, is to be married presently in Eng-
land to Pat Jackson, who is the eldest son of
a baronet, and he’s no worse for that. These
sudden prospects of titles-by-marriage are
nothing to the Pearsons, who can produce
social gloss with dukes and duchesses any
time by simply shaking the family tree. They
are among the toniest of nun long-settled
Australians, and the parents,' who are in
England agreeing.. to these alliances, pro-
bably think their girls are just making nice,
sensible matches suitable to their station in
life. (Fanfare of trumpets.) Helen’s young
man is in the Navy. A submarine is his
particular care. Mimi’s bridegroom has gone
through his gruelling in -France, and has
been wounded three times. $
Popular Ida Sell was quietly married the
other day to Lieutenant.. Maney Lake, who
returned damaged by the Turk. Ida is brown-
eyed and olive-skinned, and is a relative of
handsome Mrs. Jack Fisken, whose first
spouse was David McCaughey.
Carmen Harris, the eldest Pinschof daugh-
ter, has been grass-widowing hereabouts
lately. month she catches the boat
for Vladivostock, where her husband expects
to be able to earn the family crust better
than in more civilised parts. Sister Eliza-
beth, who married an American machinery
man of the name of Finley, is now living in
Los Angeles.
The Y.R.C. Leonard pair are gloating
over their very first grandchild, a boy which
the stork delivered at the Clive Leonard
household some days ago.
The High Court Duffys, including, of
course, Associate-daughter Mary, have left
the Cliveden barracks and taken a roomy
villa in Toorak. There they can arrest their
own roses and summons one another without
interruption.
Mrs. Fred Fairbairn, who seems to dash
backwards and forwards between here and
London about twice a week, is preparing to
do it again with husband and daughter Jess
for company. The family party will journey
through Canada, where its head has acquired
some property. Jess will try and get work in
an English hospital. Sam McCulloch’s Kathie
and Violet Thornley will travel in the
shadow of the Fairbairns. They, too, are
thirsting to nurse British soldiers. One
would think we had not any military invalids
of our own to look after.
The William Cooper knight is here from
England watcliing His new horses gallop.
Thirty long years ago he used to watch
other animals that are now equine ghosts
throwing the clods behind them with their
feet. To-day the seasoned sportsman looks
no older. He walks with a stoop ; but that’s
merely a touch of rheumatism. The Orien-
tal is his temporary home.
A handful of women journalists and a
small army of brothers-in-ink gave a dinner
to Kathie Prichard, the Australian prize
novelist, at the Caf<§ Frangaise. “Jack”
Davidson, editor of the Hekald. filled the
chair unloaded the complimentary re-
marks. Kathleen, who adorned the Herald
staff before she went to London to
seek her fortune, blushed her acknow-
ledgments above white muslin and Early
Victorian flounces. She gasped nervously
when the time came to respond to the
speeches, then collapsed in a heap behind
the table decorations. Later she recovered
her nerve, sat up and said firmly, “Now
I can say what I wanted to say!” And she
said it forthwith.
Mrs. Carlyle Smythe gave a tea-partv on
one of the dog days in the hedged gardens of
~ e Cnf6, St. Ivilda Esplanade.
A ycliffe used to be the exclusive home of the
Royal xacht Club until the domestic mariners
built themselves a cabin on the sands. The
hostess wore a dress composed of little Pais-
ley foulard frills with green extras; Mre.
Dwyer attended in creamy Brussels net with
uncountable yards of narrow insertion hand-
worked into the frills; and Carrie Haase
consumed refreshments in brown muslin.

The Lady Northcote Orchestra Fund i.
waxing quite sound on its vested interest
The committee, having a little money £
splash, intends to engage Verbruggen’!

Graham Moore has returned from England
with composers’ laurels wreathed round tZ
crown of his head. ll e is going to hi

The widow Phillips Fox is fixing un an
exhibition of her late husband’s paintings at
tbe Athenaeum Gallery, and Lady H e i en
Ferguson has promised to declare it open
next Monday afternoon. Mrs. Fox (she is
Ethel Garrick when she exhibits her own
work) wants to get back to Europe as soon
as possible. There are a lot of pictures that
should be taken off her hands before she goes
by admirers of the good Australian artist
who will paint no more.
Artist Berenice Edwell, who miniatures
the Very Best people, is back from her
native Tasmania, where she has been doing
some important commissions for nutshell
portraits.
Ida and Annie Rentoul are brewing a
new book between them. What isn’t pic-
tures will be verse.
Another argument produced .by the wor-
ried Melbourne housewife in her agitation
for sixpenny bread (the military authorities
get it for fivepence) is that chaff has fallen
£lO a ton since bread was 9d., during the
drought. The high price of flour and horse-
feed was then used by the baker as a double
plea for a dear loaf. Now, with flour down
from £lB 155., the maximum drought price,
to £l2 10s., liorse-feed also sinking,
water for bread-making just as cheap as it
ever was, and the 1915 insolvency list show-
ing a marked absence of bakers, consumers
begin to persuade themselves -that sixpence
is really a fair price for 41b. of bread. (You
never guessed I kept an eye on the price of
horse-rations, did you?)
A suburban matron is still bewailing an
accident that happened to her. She was
enjoying a leisured morning in bed, and
there being only herself and the maid at
home, she arose from her double-bed clad
only in a Ilenry James James “nightie”
made of morning mist and a ribbon and
sauntered dreamily into the parlor to get the
morning paper. Suddenly a male footstep
resounded in the passage, and the airy pro-
menader, finding herself cut off, dashed into
a cupboard containing empty trunks and the
gas meter. As she breathlessly awaited an
opportunity to slip back to her bedroom the
cupboard door flew back on its hinges, and
she found herself face to face with an
amazed gasman. How crowded the meto-
reading profession would be if such surprises
were frequent.
Blytheswood, the old home of David Syme,
was sold after the death of his widow the
other day. Son Geoffrey bought the place
and is preparing for a flitting from Banool,
where he caught the armed jewel-hunting
burglar. A huge new staircase is being built
into the heart of Blytheswood, and the old
house is being remodelled round it in such a
way as not to interfere with the garden,
which is a prize-winner even among the fine
Yarraside homesteads. Old Mrs. Syme used
to spend hours every day, under a huge,
shady hat, sub-editing shrubs and coaxing
seedlings with a trowel. When the place
was sold, all its contents (which were many
and various) had to be divided into six equal
portions and sold. The old lady had a mag-
pie love of collecting useful things. She
had immense stores of reserve kitchen-ware
packed away—enough to last another life-
time of even Mary Ann breakages. Another
collecting fad embraced vases, which sire had
by the hundred. One thing the family
especially appreciated was her accumulation
of diamonds. She loved to collect unset and
beautiful stones, and Mrs. Geoffrey now has
a-remarkable rope of perfect, round opals
set alternately with diamonds. She has
also an opal pansy pendant, with a diamond
set as a dewdrop. The stones were both a
joy and a worry to the mother possessor,
who had many ways of hiding them. For
a long time the treasures were kept in a
worthless-looking biscuit tin by her bedside.
Geoffrey Syme bought all the nearest per-
sonal things belonging to his mother, aud
gave them to his wife, along with the big
grey motor in which widow Syme took her
daily drive.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Merrigal”: Gadflies is the monniker
(technical one, Tabanidce ) of those winged pests that
dine en the head, neck, back and flanks of horses
and cattle, causing loss of temper and condition, but
no other injury. Their eggs are laid on the leaves
and twigs overhanging pools or running streams,
which are the domiciles of the juvenile gads. Spray-
ing the quadrupeds with a kerosene emulsion diluted
with 10 parts of water will damp the ardor of the
blood extractors.
THE ETERNAL FEMININE.
HE: “Well, I’m no wowser, but I just heard a remark that made me get up and leave
those fellows.”
SHE: “How splendid of you! What was the remark?”
“W.D.”: The toughest-bird competition
can end now and the iron cross be passed to the
Maori hen. When I say that, although the fowl is easily
caught and hangs around Henare’s camp, it never
appears on the menu at the tangi, my case is made
out. Boiling, baking, frying, cursing, praying, - are
all the same to the chook. He remains uneatable.
But the oil he produces is the best leather-preserver
I know; it is a sure cure for rheumatism, and if
made hot and rubbed into an axe-handle the wood
will last a new-chum almost a week.
“Pick” : I would caution any abo. who
has been sampled by a snake ag%inst trusting too
implicitly to Condy’s crystals as an antidote. Per-
manganate of potash, to some extent, will neutralise
snake-venom ; so, also, will sea water. At Merim-
bula (N.S.W.) I once saw a Chinese with the dis-
tinct punctures made by a black snake in the calf
of his leg ; and though he took no other precautions
than to give the part an hour’s bathing and rubbing
in the sea, he won. Dogs, too, when bitten take
to saltwater if it is available —and usually call round
for a bone some time later. The prime considera-
tion, however, is whether the rubbing or soaking
will reach the seat of the poison timely enough, and
that depends largely upon the class and virility of
the snake. At any time the bite of the black species,
though deadly, admits of simpler treatment than
that of the brown, the tiger, the copper head or the
death-adder, because the first-named lot has shorter
fangs, and the venom they inject is superficial and
readily reached. Young snakes, also, are less harm-
ful than grown-ups. When the full-grown, long-
fanged, angry snake has been on the job the liga-
ture, knife and strychnine are still the surest things
to head off the undertaker.
* * * *
“Bush Builder” : In B. 6/l/’l6 a returned-
from-S. America Australian commends what is known
here as pis 6 building. But the process as described
would only suit a rainless country. Owing to cost
of labor pisfj building is much dearer than wood, or
even brick ; but with a foundation built in cement
it makes a much cooler house, and, with a verandah
all round to keep the weather from it, a dry house.
But the up-to-date building for low cost and comfort
—if sand is handy—is reinforced concrete. Iron is
the cheapest good roof, with a non-conductor under-
neath. Straw or shingles on top spoil the rain-
water for use ; so do all the patent-roofing materials
I have tried. Every bush home wants a big roof,
underground tank, and wide verandahs for outdoor
sleeping. Casements are better than windows; they
give more ventilation. Even in cheap-labor England
(Hampshire) I know pise to cost more than brick.
Let anyone measure up the yards of material in a
house with walls two to three feet thick, and he will
be staggered.
“McG.”: It has been stated that the
toad fish is rarely found outside of Japanese waters.
In the Swan River and in the waters surrounding
Fremantle “blowy,” as he is named, abounds and
has earned the heart-hatred of every fisherman who
inhabits the district,. For many years past he has
been a terrible pest. In Dago Bay, on the Swan,
where the fishing-boats anchor, I have fed hundreds
of them from the hand. The brute usually swallows
the hook about down to his tail, and his toad-like
look and slimy feel often excites a feeling of nausea
in the amateur fisherman.
“Tropicus”: Whiting will stop most in-
sects, but it is not in the race against ants with
turpentine. Simply soak a rag in turps and wrap it
around the legs of the beer-case, or the rope of the
flourbag that holds it to the ridge pole, and you
will not be worried any more by the little pests.
In hot weather the rag needs dipping every two or
three days to counteract the evaporation. Another
dodge is to use citronella oil instead of turps, but
the latter is much better.
“Pick”: “Breast-Plate” (B. 3/2/’l6)
asks a remedy for sore shoulders on a horse. What
kind of horse? If a hard-fed animal in regular work
the remedy is a better-fitting collar with a depres-
sion over the sore until it mends, but if the trouble
conies from gall or sweat-boil on a fresh horse, or
one constitutionally soft (in which case the wounds
renew every time the animal is harnessed up), bath-
ing with tepid water and boracic acid after work,
and applying lard and sulphur ointment will work
wonders in under record time.
STILL HOPE.
EDITOR : “No, I’m afraid your sketch is no good for our purposes; your style’s too gro-
tesque.”
ARTIST: “Then you recommend me to give it up as a had job?”
EDITOR: “By no means! Why not take on a job illustrating fashions for women?”
“Sarto”: Never before have I seen the
bush trees here (Southern Riverina) looking as beau-
tiful as they do now. After last year’s drought one
marvels at their recuperative powers. Last year on
grassless hills and lowlands the trees and bushes,
large and small, old and young, looked gaunt and
hungry and withered, as if in the first stages of
some wasting disease. Now one sees all shades of
green, fresh, glossy and nourished, on dale and hill,
standing a foot deep in golden grasses. When sil-
houetted sometimes against a clear blue sky, some-
times against a sunset that resembles a huge palette
on which every color has been daubed, the bushman
has a picture that he loves and admires. Among
the trees feed sleek-coated horses and cattle that a
year ago were fighting for life. One sees also every
conceivable shape and form of insect —absurd crea-
tures, harmful or harmless, whose only missions in
life are to destroy fruit and vegetables and nourish
the myriads of birds that call aloud that the world
(or Southern Riverina, at all events) is good to live
in.

“Mulga”: Tell “F.C.” (B. 13/1/T6),
who wants to know what sort of pine makes the best
fencing posts, to use cypress pine. The boundary-
fence of Cypress Downs station, between the Bollon
and St. George (Q.), is of this timber, and when
writer last saw it, although it was over 40 years old,
there was no sign of decay.

“Barcoo”: “Dicey Iron” (B. 27/1/T6)
identifies the large black-headed snake I mentioned as
the N.Q. tiger, but Q. has no tiger-snake. lam natu-
ralistically acquainted with the crawler. He is the
larger of the only two species of true pythons found
in Australia. The black-headed rock python (Python-
ince aspiditoes) lives to a hoary old age and will
reach in rare specimens to the length of 18 to 20
feet. His commissariat department can deal with a
fair-sized wallaby, and when meals are handy the
beast is remarkably swift and vicious in attack. He
throws the coils of his body round the victim
and by constriction quickly makes his kill. This
Python will show fight to anything coming between
it and its objective, particularly so during the honey-
moon season. His only cousin on this mainland
( Pythonince amethystinus) is an even brown all over
and rarely reaches more than nine feet. But it has
all the characteristics of the relative, except that
it is not so vicious and, consequently, escapes being
dubbed the tiger also. It prefers taking up its
abode about or under buildings, rocks, or large heaps
of logs, coming out to forage during the night only.
* * * *
“Barcoo” : Bovril Estates in the Northern
Territory, with its herd of 150,000 head of cattle
running over an area of 9,250,000 acres, is easily
the largest cattle ranch in British Dominions, if
not in the world. The original proprietary was
Kidman, Forrest and Emmanuele, of West Aus. Big
John, on one of his little trips inland, spied out
this almost perfect grazing country on the Victoria
River, and gauged its possibilities. Rolling plains
of grazing land are blessed with natural water-
ing places and lagoons, as well as the finest
tidal river in Aus. to float stores up to within
a few miles of the head station. The property was
known as Victoria River Downs till Kidman floated
it into a limited liability co. in England, retaining
largie provisional interests and a seat on the direc-
torate. It is a second Bowen Downs, as that
station was 33 years ago, with 170,000 cattle,
occupying the whole of the Mitchell district (Cen-
tral Q.), and a portion of the Barcoo. The Mitchell
district now runs 5,000,000 of sheep and supports
■ half a dozen of the most up-to-date inland towns in
the Commonwealth. The prophet who would have
foretold that Central Q. wool would sell for 35Jd.
per pound in Brisbane in 1916 would have been care-
fully watched for further developments. The table-
lands of the Victoria River have been geologically
guaranteed to the directorate as being water-bearing,
and boring is to be proceeded with. The whole of
the harness used on this large station is produced on
the premises from the co.’s own hides, grown and
tanned on the ground. The lime for the first process
is burnt on the station, and the tannin is from a
liquid made from the bark of a local tree. The
station wheelwright and blacksmith produce all
vehicles used of local woods.
* * * *
“O’Phimerty” : The bush hermit is gener-
ally a broken-dowm alcoholic, but not always. I met
one in the ranges of Western Tas. who was working
a tin section. His claim was beside a packhorse
track, traversed about six times a year, and he
scarcely ever saw the face of man except when the
pack-drivers came along, bringing his supplies and
taking his tin. He told me he had a ten-year-old
grand-daughter and explained that he could live
cheaply in the bush and make enough to give her a
good “schoolin’ ” up in Launceston. Then he
pointed to the hills around. “There must be some
good shows there,” he said. “I’m out here lookin’
for them. Some day I’ll find one an’ then the little
girl can get all she wants.” I hope he found
a good mine before now. A few years ago at Port
Sorell, an abandoned convict station on the North
Tas. coast, I came across an ancient woman practis-
ing the hermit life. She said she was 80, and
looked it. Her husband had been an official of some
sort in the old days and had died there, and she
would be quite content to die in the same place.
Supplies were brought to her once a week from a
settlement a few miles away, but otherwise the old
lady lived in complete solitude.
* ♦ * *
“Scary Bird” : Allow me to throw in my
Bst. nothing in support of “Rag’s” assertion (B.
20/l/’l6) that “if it is native flowers we're after we 11
have to head for the Golden West.” Having zig-
zagged Maoriland and every State in the Common-
wealth, nowhere have I beheld such a profusion of
native flora as bedecks the auriferous belts of the
Murchison, Yilgarn and eastern goldfields and the
western falls of the Victoria and Darling Ranges
right around to the southern coast. Apparently in-
different to the scanty rainfall, everlasting daisies of
every tint intermingle with the pigeon-blooded Sturt-
pea,. the quaintly-beautiful flannel-flower, and an
endless variety of dainty flowerlets, the lot inter-
twined by the searching tentacles of the beautiful
red-runner to form a gorgeous carpet as far as the
eye can reach and exuding a fragrance calculated to
intoxicate. The more moistened coastal areas, from
Sharks Bay around to the Esperance, form a practi-
cally continuous garden, notably of the “kangaroo
paws,” with their dull beauty of variegated velvet,
rich orchids, cowslips, maiden-hair fern, mayflower, a
small bushy clump of innumerable miniature daisies
and the fragrant pink, yellow, brown and blac'
boronia of the sou-’west. Above all, and no less
exquisite, is the gorgeous over-growth of watt e
blossom, Christmas-bush, flowery gums, and the unique
spectacle of the “banshee-nut.” Many a venturesome
pioneer has had his lonely grave richly decorated y
Nature in this land of flowers.

THE BLACK RIDER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On the. slope of Crow’s Nest, one of the highest
peaks near Wellington (M.L.), there is a clump of
trees, known as The Black Rider.
Who says it is only a clump of trees,
Odd-fashioned and dwarfed, through growing
On a saddle that soars where the blue-gowned breeze
To the cloud-crested peaks is blowing?
We know that a warrior rides up there,
Be he cavalier, knight or Norseman;
For we’ve heard many times in the moonlight fair
The jingling spurs of a horseman.
Oh; Crest upon crest, the hills aspire
Towards clouds that forever follow
Their sombre shadows, and never tire
Of skimming o’er hill and hollow.
And straight up the brown, grassed ridge he spurs,
In the dawnlight towering larger;
Oh the warrior rider scarcely stirs
On the back of his restless charger.
The old folk tell how his bit-bars ring
In the grey of the twilight often,
Where, close to the knees of the ranges, cling
The twinkling lamps of Crofton ;
When the overland trains have thundered through
And Night hides levels and bridges,
And the tired winds rest in a bath of dew,
The rider yearns to the ridges.
If he reaches the crest of the ranges bold—
As he might, on a misty morning—
Will his trumpeters come, as in days of old,
To sound us a challenge or warning?
Oh! this is the word that the old folk speak—
And they are the ones for knowing-—-
When the rider has won to the lonely peak
The winds will for aye cease blowing.
You tell me that this, which I count as true,
Is only an old fools’ story—
That nobody rides to the skyline blue,
Or spurs ’neath the midnight glory!
Oh! sad are the people who see no way
From fanciful themes to borrow—-
There’s scarcely a night but I look and say,
“Perhaps he’ll win out to-morrow.”
MAORILAND.
WILL LAWSON.

The Evolution of a “Soul in an Instrument.”
From the dim beginnings of a great conception to the consummated
triumph of its achievement it is a long journey. But to-day it is possib e
to look back from the Aeolian-Vocalion (that last link in the evolution of
the Disc Machine) and see —the Talking Machine.
The metallic sounds are gone. The flaws of unco-ordinated parts are
gone. The lack of nicely discriminated character differences in tone, as
between instrument and instrument —is gone. The old maddening
monotony of set, unvarying records is gone.
What do these sounds mean in a Disc Machine? They mean that
through its Sound-box the Aeolian-Vocalion can develop a tonally perfect
balance hitherto buried in your records.
We invite you and your friends to visit Pianola Hall between 9 a.m.
and 6 p.m. Hear, in a private room, any records you desire —to be played
for you. If convenient, we suggest that you bring with you one or two
of your own familiar records.

EAGLE STREET, BRISBANE.
“Bad Australian”: I rise to state with
deference and in a conciliatory spirit that “Pick”
and other Bulletin writers are talking through their
lids when they say that the glib mimicry con-
stantly attributed to the lyrebird is a delusion. I
don’t know anything about “Pick’s” fowl that is
hatched out on the Tafttawonglo, hut I do know
that his blood relation who builds his mounds between
the headwaters of the Buffalo and Howqua rivers
(Vic.) does imitate the call of the wonga, just as he
would that of the whampoo if he lived in the
Bananaland scrubs. As well as the black cockatoo’s
querulous growl, he has learned the ear-splitting
screech of the sulphur-crested white cocky, the clear,
cheery song of the magpie, the musical call of the
bellbird, the short, sharp snap of the whipbird, the
chatter of various kinds of parrot, and the great
brown kingfisher's demonstration of a vacant mind.
He is not quite perfect in his imitation of “.Jack’s”
derisive hilariousness, but the wr.y he hits off
a suddenly-startled flock of lories and the harsh,
grating cry of the galah marks him as a true
mimic. I have listened to “Superbus” in his
native lair for months together, but I could never find
that he had any particular statement of his very own
to make except the monotonous “plirk-plirk, plirk-
plirk-plirk” with which he greets the early dawn
and ushers in the dewy eve. (Copyright in U.S.A.
and Latin Amurrica.) By tlie way, I’ve never seen
the lyrebird and scrub-turkey scratching in the
same gully. What is the northern limit of the
lvre, and where does the other mound-builder hit
his southerly “thus far and no farther” ?
“Barcoo”: What about the straightest
wood in the Australian bush, I hand up for exam-
ination, from Queensland, rosewood. Workers in
cabinet-making generally refer to this timber as
“wild mahogany,” on account of its close resem-
blance to that valuable vegetable when worked up.
Trunks of rosewood running from 15 to 30 feet in
length are as straight as a lead-pencil, and the log
does not taper, or very little, being of a compara-
tively uniform thickness from the butt to where
the top limbs branch. These carry little foliage,
and the bark is the thinnest known to timber
experts. The outside of the wood is covered with
a protective layer of white-looking sap-wood, resem-
bling hickory in texture and just as tough. It is
about an inch in depth and underneath it is the heart
1 of the tree, which is exceptionally close-fibred and
heavy, blood-colored wood. It lends itself admirably
to cabinet-making, furniture-making or wood carving
of any description. After being worked up it will
take a very fine high polish and, if properly dry,
does not shrink. Rosewood is to he found all
over Queensland, along the slopes on either side of
any of the main ranges, particularly towards the sum-
mits, and appears to thrive luxuriantly amongst the
rocks and gravel of the heights.
“J.G.”: Re adobe or mud-brick houses
(B. 6/l/’l6). There are thousands and thousands
of Australian homes built of these sun-dried bricks.
Upon the northern Vic. plains there are any amount
of them, ranging from two-room cottages to eight-
room villas. The bricks made in l ie. are mostly
18in. x 9in. x 9in., and they are variously termed
sun-dried bricks, German bricks, and Egyptian
bricks. AVhen well made a house of these cakes is
practically everlasting, being immune from white
ants, fires or other destructive agencies. It is
related that the only building that resisted the big
San Francisco earthquake and fire was a century or
more old adobe building erected by Spanish priests.
In building the danger to he guarded against is damp
from the foundation. This is done by giving the
foundation a liberal layer of coal tar, or one of the
patent dampeourses on the market. I built the first
house of the kind where I am now living, and the
natives thought I was a harmless sort of lunatic
playing with wet mud. When I had finished making
and drying the cubes everybody for miles around
assured me that a building erected of such material
THE FIRST MOVE.
NOVICE: "No, I’ve never played dominoes.
How do you start?"
OLD HAND: "Well, mine’s a whisky and
soda.”
would come down, like a heap of sugar, when the
first rain came. After the shack was finished it
rained for about a week solid, and the neighbors
turned out to see my heap of ruins. I was really
sorry to disappoint them. For the outside of an
adobe building nothing is better than to smooth
the walls and then give two or three coats of cement
wash, that is, a little cement in a good drop of
water, mixed like ordinary whitewash and applied
with a whitewash brush. Or the walls can be
planed smooth, given a couple of dressings with oil
and then painted any color.

“Lagani-Namo”: “Maskee’s” par (B.
13,/1/’l6) re W. African driver ants reminds me of
an experience on the Brown River (Papua). The wet
season introduced the ants, and for three days they
came in systematic order, thousand by thousand,
million by million, billion by billion, trekking through
tlie scrub in continuous line. The main body occupied
about eight inches from flank to flank, and every
three inches along each side of the column were
what I assumed to be the lieutenants. Each regi-
ment was, I should say, some ten feet long, then
there was a gap of about a foot where the battalion
commanders tramped along with their despatch
riders, buglers, etc. I didn’t see any of the field-
marshals nor the generalissimo, but such of the
officers as I did see were . quite distinguishable by’
their size, actions and dignified deportment. It
would have been risky business for any’ creature
smaller than an elephant to have stood in the way
of that cavalcade.
* * * *
“Coolibah”: “Myall” (B. 13/1/’l6) de-
scribes the note of the black cockatoo as being simi-
lar to that of liis white cousin. Except that they are
about equally discordant, there is no more similarity
in their noise than there is in their mode of living.
Black cockatoos of both varieties (there are two,
identical in all' respects save that one clan affects
crimson trimmings to its underwear whilst the other
displays pale yellow) come into the coast' scrubs and
swamps in dry’ weather to feed on the tea-trees and
drag the soft, white grubs out of swamp oak and
mahogany. They frequently eat away large limbs
and even fair-sized trunks of dead mahogany in
search of their favorite diet. Scrub plums and red-
oak acorns supplement the menu, but a maize patch,
though it draws white brother in countless hordes,
has no attractions for blackie. He is a dolorous-
looking creature, and his plaintive wail and lacka-
daisical flight seem to fit his general character. His
constitutional objection to the floods of the coastal
regions prompts him to leave hurriedly at the first
breath of the monsoonal season. Wherefore the
coast selector when he hears the dolorous wail high
in the heavens and sees the black cockatoos rising
and falling like dark-hued butterflies between him
and the’ sun while they make ever west, is satisfied
that there will be “something doin’ ” in a few days
—and there usually is.
“Darvel”: If that returned Australian
who mentioned the matter (B. 6/l/’l6) will go
Outback, particularly in W.A. and Victoria, he will
find that a large number of houses are built of adobe
and tapia. They 7 go by the name of “bat” houses
or “pug” houses, and are made of soil mixed with
straw or stubble, or, in some places, with fine
gravel, which makes them very durable. Strips of
hoop-iron or bale-iron prevent them from cracking,
which is one of their chief faults. Pug walls are
made the same way, sometimes over wire-netting.
A good plan to keep the outside walls weather-proof
is to give them a coat of boiled oil.
“Bull Ryan” : O’Grady had engaged Cas-
sidy to dig and burn out a paddock of blind stumps,
i.e., trees that had previously been burned, off at
ground-level. The rule is that the boss inspects the
holes before the earth is finally filled in. The price
was one shilling each, and O’Grady was astonished
at the speed of Cassidy in finishing the job, but he
found no roots left and told Cassidy to fill in and
then get his cheque. O’Grady discovered Cassidy’s
method of speed when he started to plough. The
latter had opened a hole close to each stump and
burned a few brambles in it for O’Grady’s inspection.
The thrown-out earth hid the stumps until that was
over, and now there is a wild-eyed cocky mourning
for a man who will never return.

“Wal” : The wild and woolly Papuan moun-
taineer is a creature of strange habits and unplea-
sant morals, but in most matters he displays a
childlike innocence and ingenuousness. During mv
ramblings through the Owen Stanley Range 1 came
on some people who were “wanted” for the oblitera-
tion of a contemporary tribe. After capturing the
principal criminals I had to investigate the causes
of the massacre, and found it had arisen through
the death of a woman resident in the raider’s vil-
lage. This woman had been on a friendly visit to
tlie other tribe, and on her return home was attacked
by an illness, probably dysentery, that took her to
the boneyard. As she lay dying she informed her
relatives that she had dreamt that her illness was
caused by an old man, noted as a sorcerer by the
hillmen, and she asked that her brothers should go
down to the sorcerer’s village and “take- payment"
for her death. This was solemnly promised by the
assembled savages, and then the lady passed in
cheerfully. My informant, an old chan much bedaubed
with paint and very much in need of a bath, toi
this yarn : “After waiting for the proper time following
the woman's death, and eating all her husband’s pigs
and yams, we got the warriors together and went
on the warpath. We hid ourselves close to the medi-
cine mail’s village, and after a wait until mid-
day we caught a woman and let the young men of
our party blood their clubs on her. She was
an old woman, so she was not worth taking as a wTre
for any of our young men. We laid all day fn the
grass and watched the village until just after sun-
set, when we surrounded it and cut our way in
through the stockade. This we did without noise,
and then we threw spears through the thatch of the
roofs and sides of the houses and made the people
come outside, where we clubbed them all. They
were easy to kill because we had many more men

HIS VALID EXCUSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
His name was Bill; just Bill. He had
A dial hard as any brick.
His record was distinctly bad,
And recently he’d tried to kick
Tlie head clean off his little wife.
The dame, in terror of her life,
And hoping for a lasting peace,
Called in at length the local p’lice.
He stood before the Beak, red-eved ;
Six foot at least there was of him.
The copper told how he had tried
To tear his missus limb from limb,
And how he drank, and never gave
A penny piece her life to save.
Complainant swore the tale was true,
And showed her bruises two by two.
The Beak began to moralise: .
The man was big, his wife was small.
“Why not hit someone who’s your size?
You ! ve surely heard your country call?
If you’ll--enlist, we’ll let you go.
It’s up to you to smite the foe.”
“Yer right,” 'said Bill. “They orter get me—
I’d ’list, but, ’struth, me wife won’t let me!”

Business, Robbery, etc.
“W.A.R.”: The question is whether J. B.
Suttor, the Ma State’s Commercial Com-
missioner to the East (which means the
North) ought to be interned as a traitor to
his country. “As a result of the efforts of
his office,” Sydney Daily Telegraph
reports that prominent Jap mining engi-
neers are about to visit Australia to dis-
cover what commercial ores this continent
is capable of supplying to Japan. Aus-
tralia, having managed to get rid of the Hun
control of our metals, J. B. Suttor proposes
to hand them over to Japan. Can’t the
Australians ever do anything but excavate
the most valuable portions of the continent
and ship it to foreign countries?
* * * *
The Sydney house of Lipton’s received the
surprise of its life the other day when a
local firm of agents passed on an order for
some cases of tea to be forwarded to the
“Hamburgische Sudsee-Aktiengesellschaft,
Rabaul.” The co., however, wasn’t taking
any risks, and rushed the incriminating
document off the premises. Inquiry at the
Customs revealed the paradox that selling
goods to the Hamburgische Sudsee-Aktien-
gesellschaft was not trading with the enemy.
Why wasn’t quite clear, though presumably
the business is now in the hands of an offi-
cial trustee. But considering how unplea-
sant the local loud-mouthed, cold-footed
Hunstrafer can make things for the firm
with Hun customers on its books, it would
be much more satisfactory for everybody if
the Administration granted such a trustee-
managed concern the temporary use of an
inoffensive trade-name such as “Smith, Jones
and Co.” Just at present a Hunnish order
in an Australian business house is less
acceptable than a bad debt for a couple of
thousand.
* # * #
A “naturalised” German barber in Mel-
bourne who employs German labor was fined
£l5 last week for having changed his name
to the “Globe Hairdressing Saloon,” which
was a characteristic suggestion of Ger-
many’s proposed world conquest, anyhow.
Also a number of German bakers (less open
to secret service suspicions than barbers
are) have been fined for trying to change
their identity. But liow about the number
of German commercial agents —travellers or
indentors —who are changing a letter or two
in their names, and calling themselves loyal
citizens of Swiss or Danish parentage? The
eye of the law that so easily detects the
shopkeeper’s trick should be turned towards
the Ilun who conceals himself in an up-alley
little office.
* * * *
“Norbell”: Consider, the neat little printed
slip I just peeled off a collapsible metal tube
of allegedly Australian-made dentifrice. “It
is regretted that a key is unavailable for
this tube owing to short shipment from
Great Britain. A supply is expected shortly.”
I don’t know what it cost to prepare and
print this elegant apology for a poor little
piece of foreign ironmongery, but I do know
that any school kid with a pair of pincers
could fashion the missing key from a bit of
wire in two minutes. Still, it’s early yet;
when we have learned to make clothes-pegs
we’ll practise on a few other odd jobs.
* * * *
R. S. Webb, late U.S. Forestry Bureau
Investigator, now in Australia, reckons that
this country has an asset that should be
particularly handy after the war. There
will be, says Webb, such a call for building
timber throughout the fighting zones that
it is hard to see where it is all to come
from. Certainly the U.S. won’t be able to
produce it, because even now the local
building trade is hobbled by short supplies.
Jonathan, however, is directing a reflective
look towards the future, and has commis-
sioned Webb to send home seeds of various
Australian trees for re-afforestation pur-
poses. What that may yet mean to Austra-
lian trade may be guessed at by anyone who
remembers South Africa’s acclimatisation of
the wattle. Meantime, Webb calls upon the
N.S.W. Forestry Department to get busy,
because
Australia should have a golden opportunity of supply-
ing a large amount of ironbark, tallow-wood, black-
butt and spotted gum where strength and dura-
bility are required, with our beautiful indigenous
oaks, blackwoods, rosewoods and others where orna-
mental, fine grain and woods of delicate textures are
needed.
Unluckily, a vast percentage of “our beauti-
ful indigenous,” etc., is ballooning up in
smoke, and no one with the needful autho-
rity seems unduly worried about it.
* * * *
Albert Mullett, possibly stimulated by
some of the patriotic remarks on Australian
trees and natural resources which, as Gov-
ernment Printer, he observes from time to
time in the Victorian Journal of Agricul-
ture, is persevering with his inquiries and
experiments relating to the manufacture of
printing-paper from local wood-pulp. The
thing can be done, he thinks ; but the mar-
gin of financial advantage may at first be
small, or nil. Mullett’s idea is that, by
keeping the subject alive, he may yet com-
bine the right machinery, raw-material and
chemical-treatment. Anyway, the matter
will be considered shortly at a meeting of
State political satraps.
* * * *
“Spraggs”: Simpson the traveller told
me the story. Blobb and Co., his firm, once
sent a ton of flour up the north-eastern line,
and through some mistake kept no account
of the customer’s name or town. The office
staff searched the books and found there
were 27 customers on that particular stretch.
After consultation the stray consignment
was tacked on to each of the 27 accounts,
and all hands held their breath and settled
down to wait. They expected 26 customers
to deny having received the supply ; and one
to pay. But they fainted when 18 custo-
mers sent cheques without a murmur, while
nine declared they knew nothing about it.
I always had an idea there was money in
flour.
* * * *
“O. J. 0.”: In some Bolivian towns there
is an ingenious system in vogue to keep
the bakers from cheating the public. Bread
is sold in small rolls at 10 cents (say 2d.)
each, but the Law says nothing about the
weight of the roll. Outside municipal build-
ings there is a large blackboard, on which
appear the names of all the bakers in the
town, with the average weight of the rolls
that each is selling that day. It is a fine
example of misguided energy. The police
do the weighing for recording purposes in-
stead of for purposes of discovering offences
against a fixed weight law. Still, we who
live in this enlightened country had better
not, when making fun of such a system,
mention the law that allows bakers to make
and sell “fancy” bread (which includes all
bread not made in a particular shape) any
weight they like, without even the check of
the blackboard.

“Rg”: There are several ways for the
modest crook to pursue the dollar on safe
and easy lines. For instance: One adver-
tises that he has money to lend on real
estate, and after negotiations have been
opened with a likely borrower insists on a
valuation by Blank. Of course a couple of
pounds is handed over as a valuation fee.
Then Blank thoughtfully inspects the pro-
perty and regrets he cannot recommend the
security. A sovereign each every few days
these bad times is very handy.

“Hontex” : Bill has been with the firm 40
years, and as he was until lately pulling
down the same money he did 10 years before
he put it to the boss that a shilling or two
more every week would help things along a
bit. Bill got 12d. after prolonged talk. Then
I appeared on the scene and the boss men-
tioned how ungrateful some men are “To
think!” he said, “that when his old master
died we gave William the highest honor in
allowing him to lay the final wreath on
the coffin, and as soon as he is gone
he asks for a rise in wages!” I was so
affected that I left him without asking what
about my shilling.

“Copabella”: I spotted it hidden away at
the bottom of the drummer’s sample bag.
“Oh, ah!” he said (his nose outstood, and

Colonial stuff! Glass sugars, with cover
fsew industry, an’ all that. Very crude!”
They were crude all right, and the glass

the thing for our climate! I, therefore, din
my flag to Australian exhibit B . and may
the manufacturers go further and clarify
their raw material more. I like our sage-
tinted foliage sun-bathed in the “deep
glorious silences of the bush” ; but it should
not shriek amid the water-clear brilliance of
imported glass.

“F.N.T.” : Australia has plunged ahead in
the manufacture of writing-ink. The latest
examples of the fluid put on sale by an
ancient stationery firm in Melbourne con-
tain at least 25 per cent, more writing
material than others, either local or im-
ported. This is the result of healthy com-
petition, and the ability to obtain the raw
material from several markets. One black
mark stands against the new production. It
carries no label to show that it is made in
Australia.
