“Copabella”: I'm beginning to smell
something. English enamelware not offer-
ing, I bought enamelled mugs last week,
allegedly from Japan, and I’ve just opened
them. I hope they’re Japanese; but the
decorations are suspiciously reminiscent of
the Vaterland. Snow-covered Tyrolean-
looking habitations, with children toboggan-
ing in fur caps, long scarlet mufflers and
round Caucasian faces, don’t inspire confi-
dence. Given the Jap gift of imitation, is
it not an excess of artistry to give us Bava-
rian landscapes and Grimm figures? Any-
how, what about an Australian mug—with
a koala or kookaburra in the foreground?
* * * *
“Saint”: One result of the war is a short-
age of all classes of glass, especially fancy
colored lines. This trade was practically
controlled by Austrian firms, and for quality
and price their product could not be touched
by other countries. English-made glass was
rough and heavy in comparison, besides
lacking the beauty of design that was found
in the foreign article. But now America
and Japan are taking a hand. The Yankee
gas and lamp chimneys and the like are yet
far from fireproof; the Japanese is bad in
finish, design and quality. Will the Ger-
man be going full steam ahead hereabouts
again when the conflagration is over? A
Sydney indent agent told the writer that he
was not disposing of his German samples.
“They will come in handy again,” he said.
Even now, large quantities of chimneys
marked “Made in Denmark” are on the
market, and it’s mighty doubtful whether
they came from that land of cows and
cheese. For bottles, it is threatened that a
rise is coming, so there’s a sad day looming
when an extra tuppence will have to be paid
for the stout which accompanies the eray.
Strafe this war!
* * * *
“Copabella” explains the mysteries of the
grocery store :—-
“Percentage” is the retailer’s bugbear, his Holy
Grail, and the foundation of all his calculations; his
career is a long (or short) struggle to strike the
happy balance between expense-percentage and profit-
percentage. Expense-percentage is the ratio of
working expenses to turnover (i.e., total trade). If,
at his annual balance, the expense percentage proves
lower than the retailer has calculated, he takes the
family to Hampton or Manly for a fortnight; if it
has been too high, he calls his creditors together or
has a fire.
The trouble is that he works in the dark, more or
less, from stocktaking to stocktaking. At that fate-
ful epoch he balances his year’s trade and finds that,
for every £lOO worth of trade he has done, his
working expenses have been, say, £l5. In other
words, he needs to make that much on every line he
sells to merely pay his way ; anything over that is
“bunce.”
There are, however, in every business a few bed-
rock lines that hardly ever pay 15 p.c. In grocery,
for instance, flour is often sold at a fixed turnover
of Is. per bag; to-day that would be 51 p.c. on
cost. Kerosene is sold for the “case” ; value, 4d. to
6d., or 5 p.c. on cost. Jam, perhaps, clears Id. a
tin = 121 p.c. on cheap lines, 8 p.c. on dearer kinds.
Provisions command 2d. a lb. gross on to-day’s
markets for butter, bacon, cheese and eggs = about
13 p.c. And so on. All these are “loss-lines,” and
recognised as such. The loss on the loss-lines must
be made up by a higher percentage of profit on the
“good” (profit) ones. All this needs very delicate
adjustment, for the latter are mostly the slow and
low-priced, and for every £ of “good” trade the re-
tailer may expect 10 to 20 of “cut” and “catch”
business. Every week the shopkeeper can, by simple
calculation, arrive at his expenses percentage; but
he can only guess at his average profit, unless he
keeps a system of costs-dissection far too detailed to
be handled by the small suburban robber. He knows
his wages are about 10 p.c. ; he thinks his other
exes, are about five, and his bad debts and eontir
gencies about two, and he should be getting another
three for himself; so he puts on 50 where he can
(he can’t often), and struggles hard for 25 on “own-
namers” and similar plums, and gets as near 20 as
he can on quick-sellei's and general business, and
pinches and saves on paper and string and overtime
and weights and discounts, and trusts to luck and
economy and the computing scales to put him on
the right side at stock-taking. It is only at that
annual boiling-down that the numbers are really up.
At the end of the hypothetical year the retailer
finds that, owing to unforeseen circs, or miscalcula-
tions, maladjustments, wage-changes, carelessnesses,
etc., his expenses have climbed up to 17 or 18 p.c. of
his turnover. And he’d worked all the year fixing
his prices on the 15 p.c. estimate. Too late to make
it up that year ; but he’ll try to carry a little more
margin next! Pretty hard to do, w r ith so many at
the game. If only he could get a bit more on sugai
or kerosene, or jam or some other of the loss-lines how
much easier it W'ould be to carry on! So he sees
Ballance and Waite and Coffey and Butters and a
few more of his competitors, and they arrange to
lower the loss on sugar by 5 p.c. And then all Hell-
Five newspapers, 17 unions, 13 crank-societies, 2093
customers, 42 suburban councillors, eight multipt
shop firms, nine women’s leagues, and Henry IV on a
rise up and curse him for a snatcher of crusts fror
the lips of the orphan and the wounded soldier, arc.
all his assistants and the driver ask for a rise!
SONG O’ SIXPENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When I was in a draper’s shop
I went (it was a comic fix)
To work at eight, and had to stop
When it was six.
Penned up among the meek, sleek men,
With one-and-twopence in my pockets,
I dourly drove a dubious pen,
Dissecting dockets.
The totals were a curse to me:
I’d often feign (the sin was mine)
That four-and-eight plus two-and-three
Made one-and-nine.
(A little coin I needed then—
Being by Fortune quite unkist—
And so I was, ’mid common men,
A journalist.)
Pitt-street was calling just outside
Right wistfully—my heart upon it!
Softly, to cheat the Ragman, I’d
Indite a sonnet.
Had he but known, with anger bleak
He had divorced me from my job,
For which he paid me every week
Just thirty bob,
And nevermore my hand with glee
Had faked wild totals for the ninny.
(Two sonnets —in his time—brought me
One blessed guinea.)
So there I toiled from morn till night
And marked the dizzy figures hopping,
Or slyly watched (most gladsome sight)
Siim ladies shopping.
About the floors I’d trudge and scan,
Without affright or special loathing,
Those mysteries (alas! for man)
Called Underclothing.
I’d view the fleecy things that stood
In piles, and others hung in rows,
And wonder much how Venus would
Have looked in Those.
Sometimes I’d ask a dimpled maid,
Dull Commerce’s demurest minion;
But never would that damsel staid
Give her opinion.
Her hair was gold, her eye was blue,
Her mouth —Ave, Imperatrix ! —•
Was ecstasy. (But now, ehu!
She’s forty-six
And matronly, I make no doubt,
Plump symbol of a world gone wrong;
She wed some useful placid lout
At Wollongong.)
But even love could scarce illume
Those dreary days’ abhorred procession,
And with glad songs did I resume
Mine own profession.
And still in dreams on stuffy nights
I see the little drapers prance
And caper—those forlornest wights
Of circumstance.
It makes me very tired and sad
To see them as they dodge and snuffle;
But life is somehow, good or bad,
A ghastly scuffle,
And even drapers have to live
(I don’t know why: it does seem wrong):
So Christ ha’ pity 1 Polks, forgive
This sad, sad song....
Once I slaved in a draper’s shop.
I went (here was a comic fix)
To work at eight, and had to stop
When it was six.
N.S.W.
SELWYN RIDER.

DRAWN WIRE

LIGHT IS
A TOOL.
It is the most important
tool in the industrial
plant. It is the tool
by means of which all
other tools are run.
Working efficiency de-
pends upon light. If

Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort, and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
beeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.

eally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).

ON ACCLIMATISING ARMAGEDDON
AT DARWIN.
“\[ujik” : After-the-war problems are only
going to be trifled with until they arrive.
Then the one that is hurriedly grabbed from
the accumulated heap and is made the most
noise about is the one that is going to re-
ceive immediate attention. This makes the
opportunity for Australia to formulate pro-
posals to be submitted to the Peace Con-
ference which is expected to some day
assemble. It is no use the Commonwealth
leaving consideration of what she is going
to say and do at that tribunal until the
last" moment. The attitude on all questions
should be'determined beforehand; the pleni-
potentiaries armed with schemes that will
reflect the growing altruism of humanity,
the ameliorating influence of civilisation and
the pacificatory: Briefly, Australia has the
opportunity, the leisure and the materials
for leading the way in a great universal
reform by regularising war.
What is John Bull fighting for? Because
the German leaped into Belgium. What is
France in the fray for?. Because the Ger-
man leaped into France. Why are Italy and
Austria hard at it? Well, long ago Austria
sprang on top of Italy and tore a lump out,
and Italy is now leaping back to tear the
piece back again. Why has Germany
sprung into Russia? Mainly because Rus-
sia was trying to spring over the Carpa-
thians. And Servia. Turkey, Montenegro
and Bulgaria have all been jumped on or
are struggling to jump on someone jelse. It
is a story of wars being fought in the wrong
places. The world’s greatest need of the
future is the localisation of war, the mark-
ing out of boundaries within which cam-
paigns may be waged, and where the fate of
armies and nations may be determined with-
out interfering with non-combatants, the
peaceful pursuit of trade and the develop-
ment of science, religion and art. There
was no military reason why Germany, for
instance, should have leaped into Belgium
if she could have achieved better results by
leaping somewhere else —and they would
have been better if, by projecting her multi-
tudinous armies in another direction, her
trade and commerce with the rest of the
world had proceeded unimpaired while the
disturbance raged on to its appointed end.
Germany and other countries are 'in the
same boat on this matter of leaping and
being leaped upon. They would listen to
reason, and Australia has the key to the
situation —the Northern Territory. The oppor-
, tunity of reconciling the world to President
Wilson’s Notes, and of being relieved from
disorderly conduct on the premises is unique.
The world is sick of the Notes and of war
interfering with imports and exports. Let
Australia provide the remedy.
The reform could be put in the simplest
terms. “Here,” Fisher or AY. M. Hughes
could say to the Peace Conference, “is a
record of what has happened during the
war.” (Then the speaker would read the
impressive statistics of ruined towns,
families, commerce, banks and paddocks.)
“Here, on the other hand, is what Australia
offers —a, fighting field half a million square
miles in extent —a territory at present occu-
pied by a horse doctor and half a dozen good
unionists who could be prevailed upon to
evacuate it —which can be transferred to
an International Commission on lease for
999 years, subject to renewal at valuation, as
the place where all future wars shall be
waged, without prejudice to the parties con-
cerned or imperilling the lives of people who
have nothing to do with any disturbance
which may be in progress. In fact, there is
room there for 15 disturbances to proceed
simultaneously.”
A new impetus would be given to academic
thought by this proposal. Diplomacy would
be robbed of nearly all its possibilities for
evil. The reign of law would hold sway
without damage to the ineradicable warlike
impulses of Kaisers, militant democracies
and nations with a mission or a punch. The
possibilities of such an offer being made to
the world are almost limitless. Supposing,
for example, that in a year or two Mexico
and Roumania wanted to knock one another
about. It would be a monstrous violation of
law and order if they started hacking their
way through other countries and blockading
each other and blowing up Scots and Swedes
and Italians and holding up German ships
bound in ballast for Maoriland. By regular-
ising the conflict all these objectionable fea-
tures of war could be banished. Mexican
and Roumanian could simultaneously leap
into the Northern Territory, fight their
quarrel out there amid ample spaces and in
a congenial climate, and having settled
matters restore the scenery to its previous
position and return to their respective coun-
tries, where industry would have, meanwhile,
been proceeding as usual. Not only could
Australia thus humanise war, but there is
the further and quite as cogent consideration
that by an act of stupendous sacrifice, gener-
osity and enlightenment she could, at one
great master-stroke of policy, bring herself
into the forefront of nations, stimulate her
u dustrial development, convert a huge slice
of the continent into a place* where there
would be tremendous demand for Australian
produce, secure a free gift of cash for build-
ing the Centralian railroad, and relieve the
Federal Government from such painful
necessities as appointing more Royal Com-
missions, squelching strikes at Darwin, or
saying what they proposed to do about the
matter of the Territory generallv.
unless this offer is made to a
world exhausted by promiscuous Rap-
ing madly into unauthorised places, what
js Australia going to do with the
territory? So far, the only proposals
on hand are to get more reports on the posi-
tion, the 2703 reports on hand being inade-
quate, and Kink O’Malley’s hair-brained
advice to Mahon to make himself immortal
b.v trapping a river in Papua and conveying
it to the Macdonald Ranges and establish-
ing an ornamental lake for propagating
Murray cod. Fisher’s policy about the
Territory was to valiantly say nothing at
great length. Hughes’s seems to be to handle
the situation by rooting out Germans. The
policy of Mahon, the man in charge, is to
accumulate documents and keep an open
mind. Liberal policy is to “promote
natural expansion." which is about as in-
telligent as to recommend breeding billy-
goats. But all this confusion and states-
manlike muddle is of little consequence now.
The hour is at hand, and it only requires
the man to rise and clothe in adequate lan-
guage a great project of world-redemption—-
one which will be worthy the genius of our
race and might as a colonising people—that
will vindicate the national claim that Aus-
tralians are pioneers in the region of human-
itarian experiment and in conservation until
the psychological moment of the N. Territory
for public and international purposes were
guided by far-sighted devotion to the cause
of peace and realisation of the back-yard’s
potentialities. What if the hour is allowed
to pass and another sort of psychological
moment arrives? Well, of course, a report
can always be secured, and the easiest way
of settling matters for five minutes anyhow
may be made the subject of earnest and pro-
longed discussion.
Among the many books and pamphlets and
magazine articles dealing with the German
Menace that were published prior to Arma-
geddon the most informative is “Germany’s
Swelled Head,” by Emil Reich, an Angli-
cised Austrian and a learned student of his-
tory. Reich’s book came to light in 1907.
He was not so exactly prophetic in his views
of war happenings in the near future as some
of the late “alarmists” —Robert Br.AfcH-
ford, for instance. He wrote of Germany
versus Britain, without a strong combination
of military powers against the Kaiser’s
forces. But he reckoned on the assistance of
Austria and Turkey as inevitable factors in
German aggression, and in a purely unmili-
tary spirit he discussed Britain’s perils of
public ignorance 'to weak, peace-at-any-price
government. The text of Emil Reich’s
book, which applied to all English-speaking
countries wflth equal force, proved its truth
within seven years of its utterance: —
The Germans are afflicted with the severest attack
of swelled-headedness known in modern history. The
English are practically ignorant of this dangerous
state of mind in their greatest rivals.
The preacher’s explanation of the causes of
the swelling in Germany’s hatrack was as
convincing as his comment on the danger of
it to Europe : —•
Six hundred years ago most of the Prussians
proper were still heathens talking a Slav language.
For centuries the German princes were used as
flunkeys by the older and more powerful nations
around them. Now it is common knowledge that of
all kinds of intractable pride the pride of the upstart
is the worst, the least amenable to sound limits.
The preacher of 1907 did not. as afore stated,
foresee the sending of British troops to
France for the ultimate defence of Britain,
but he was imaginative enough in his theories
concerning Germany’s future naval policy :
An entente between Turkey and Germany is even
now (1907) virtually in existence The Turkish
army, which can very well reach Egypt by land, is a
most serious factor.... It is far from impossible to
assume that the Kaiser will first create an Egyptian
war before he launches his fleet against the British.
Maybe Emil Reich was quite wrong in his
estimate of Germany’s capacity for warship
building in comparison with Britain’s capa-
city, but his .1907 opinions of the German
naval menace are a cold douche for those
optimists who assume that the many German
shipyards have turned out nothing new
except submarines since the building of Ger-
man merchantmen came to a full stop, 19
months ago. The optimists laugh at the idea
of the Kaiser’s “bottled-up” fleet being
launched against the British. Emil Reich
suggested that it might not happen until war
had been “created” in Egypt. Well, the
Kaiser is about to accomplish that creation
seemingly. And then perhaps the bottled-up
fleet wili uncork itself, and the strength of
Germany’s marine menace which alarmed
Reich nine years ago will be put to the
test. The fate of the British Empire will
turn on a big battle or two in the North Sea.
The question as to whether we have under-
rated Germany’s possibilities on the sea, as
we have under-rated the possibilities of her
land and air forces, will be settled definitely.
Meanwhile the conclusion at which Emil
Reich arrived in 1907 is not comforting. The
slackers and shirkers are advised to take it
seriously:—•
Should Great Britain, misled by men who have
never made a serious study of either history or of
the continent in its present configuration, misread the
signs of the times and continue to treat the German
question lightly, then indeed the Germans’ dream of
Pan-Germanism will not remain a dream.
Then will come Britain’s nightmare brought
on by an undue appetite for anti-conscrip-
ticn.
Parson Garnet Sliaw, who has recently
been laboring in that part of the vineyard
known as St. James’s, East St. Ivilda (lie.),
has beaten his pruning-hook into a bayonet,
and goeth forth v-itli other Australian sol-
diers to Armageddon. Shaw does not intend
to go back to the pulpit after the trouble is
over. He gives as his reason the conviction
that, though the Church is right in its prin-
ciples, it does not follow the_ principles into
practice. The clerical soldier was born at
Ballarat (Vic.) in 1877, and after he had
matriculated at Melbourne Uni. at 14 he
went clerking. Then he drifted over to
W.A., and after working at various jobs, in-
cluding a spell as an orchard hand, he pre-
sently found himself in the Nor’-West pearl-
ing fleet, where he became mate and subse-
quently master of a lugger, taking an occa-
sional turn below in the diving-dress. In
1906 he turned south, and did his course at
Melbourne Uni., and was ordained as an
Anglican parson by Archbishop Clarke.
When the Australian navy was born Shaw,
then a curate, was the first chaplain ap-
pointed, and was sent to the training-ship
Tingira in Rose Bay (Sydney). He resigned
because he differed from the Navy Office s
views of religion, and particularly because
he wanted a voluntary flock, and objected
to a system which compulsorily rounded up
the sheep to a Sunday muster.
For the first time an Australian becomes
warden of one of the colleges at Sydney Uni-
versity, the usual suggestion to import hav-
ing been turned dowm. Canon Arthur Garn-
sey. who is to take charge of St. Paul’s
College now r that its former head, Dr. Rad-
ford. has girded on the Anglican apron at
Goulburn, is the second son of now-
deceased Charles F. Garnsey, who punched
the pulpit Bible so long at Christ Church,
Sydney. Thirty-odd years ago the father,
who was reckoned very “high” in those days,
was the first to introduce to Sydney the
candles and chasubles which these times
keep Archgaiters Wright awake o’ nights and
give rise to the ever-recurring vestments
storm in Anglican teacups in the Ma State.
Son Arthur doesn’t take after dad in that
respect, but is a “broad” churchman. His
breadth was shown at a synod a few years
ago, when his wasVhe only voice to oppose
the usual motion deploring the opening of
Sydney Show on Good Friday. Going up
from Sydney Grammar School to the local
Uni. in the early ’nineties, he collected most
of the available scholarships and prizes for
classics, and, after getting his M.A. degree,
went school teaching. Though a parson, and
a Scholar iu Theology of the Australian Col-
lege, he has never pushed a collection plate,
but has earned a crust as chaplain and classi-
cal master at Melbourne" Grammar School,
and for the past seven years as head of a
theological college at Armidale (N.S.W.).
Construction of the Esperance (W.A.)
railway still hangs fire. Money was
voted by Parliament for it long ago, and on
the strength of this many families settled on
the land thereabouts. Old Gropers opposed
the line, frankly admitting that once it
started to work, trade would be taken from
Fremantle, and farmers in the S.W., where
Bigjohn Forrest originated, would receive
less for their produce because Esperance
would compete for goldfield business. But
when Scaddan and party were seeking power
nearly six years ago they swore mighty oaths
that come what would the line would be
built; also that while
it was delayed
farmers in the dis-
trict would have
their produce trans-
ported at reduced
rates. Since that
time Scaddan and
his mates discovered
that for them to re-
tain office they had
to keep in with the
Perth and S.W. bri-
gands, so promises
were forgotten and
the money voted
was spent elsewhere.
Meantime the Espe-
rance eockies have
accumulated large
stacks of wheat and
hay, etc., and heaps
of fruit and vege-
tables, all of which
are rotting.
A memory of the late
JOHN MATHER,
Of Melbourne, artist.
“O.K.’s” reflections :
Listening to the comments on Wilson and
his interminable correspondence with European
Governments, any unthinking person would imagine
that the alleged simple, scholarly, flabby Wood-
row got into his billet by accident, and is cer-
tain to be thrown out of it next November. The pro-
Allies section of the American press and public tries
to maintain its own self-respect by professing to
accept the Woodrow Wilson of fiction as an untimely
fact. But the real Wilson is no accidental shuffler,
but a pushing professional Yankee political wire-
puller, who must have known as many tricks of the
game as his rivals. He is a natural, not an unnatural,
growth of America’s political system. He made a
successful bid for German and pro-German votes
before going to the poll, that’s certain; and his re-
nomination for the Presidency (placed with the Ohio
Democrats) shows that he is going on the same tack
again. His birthday greetings to the Kaiser last year
were not the artless compliment of an innocent-minded
peace-at-any-price doormat, but the deliberate mani-
festation of an old, cold tactician's desire to have the
wealthiest and most unscrupulous schemers working
for his re-election this year. Were Woodrow Wilson
only a little bit of the canting simpleton that the
outside world (or some of it) charitably thinks him,
he never would have got into American politics and
pushed himself into the front seat. Nor would he
after being there in these unpeaceful times have
dreamed of bidding for re-election.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Commonwealth Treasurer Higgs has had
printed in a pamphlet the report of the
interview at which three Melbourne bankers
begged him to let them export gold to the
United States—not to India or the U.K.,
which both want gold, but to Woodrow
Wilson’s country. Higgs scored heavily on
that occasion, and he might score still more
heavily if he would reprint an address
delivered by the chief spokesman of those
three bankers. In August last, addressing
the Chamber of Commerce, he quoted Lloyd
George approvingly:—
Anyone who, for selfish motives of gTeed, goes
out of his way to attempt to withdraw sums of gold
and to appropriate them to his own use—let it be
clearly understood that he is assisting the enemies of
his native land, and he is assisting them more effec-
tively probably than if he were to take up arms.
“I think therefore,” then said this gentle-
man who, eight months later, badgered the
Treasurer to let him make a little money by
exporting gold—“I think therefore we should
welcome any workable scheme which tends
not only to keep within the Empire what
gold we possess, but which will add to it.”
What would that representative banker have
felt like if Higgs had been able to t’ row
that speech at him after he had spoken?

The 16 public enterprises run by the
Westralian Government are leaking at the
rate of £144,498 a year. Some of them are
hoses. For instance, the revenue from the
sawmills for seven months has been £127,663
and the expenditure £149,333; from the im-
plement works £31,685, expenditure £84.993.
Meanwhile unhappy Jack Scaddan is shout-
ing that the Treasury figures form an unre-
liable guide to the situation and he is throw-
ing his own figures about in large, trans-
parent lumps.
Seeing that the Melbourne wharf-laborers
promise to decline to handle flour for export
elsewhere than to the British Government
until bread is reduced locally to 6d. per 41b.
loaf, in defiance of whatever the Arbitration
Court may say, and seeing that the contin-
ued retention in the country of any of its
exportable produce is a mighty unprofitable
proceeding, it is up to Premier Peacock to
consider the position. The wharf-laborers
contend that the local price of bread ought
to be 6d, per 41b. loaf, and in justification
of their demand point out that several public
hospitals are being supplied at sd. Also,
they say that the flour which they refused
to load for Java would, if shipped thither,
be converted into bread and there sold at
3|d. per 41b. loaf, which is a difference that
even the cheap labor of Java will not account
for. Possibly the Java master-baker dilutes
his flour with sawdust. Anyhow, as this pol-
icy of the wharf-laborers of constituting
themselves an unofficial Price of Foods
Board threatens to extend, the Peacock Min-
istry’s usefulness seems further on the wane.
When an outside body takes on the work that
ought to be done by Ministers it is time
these latter tried to earn their salaries else-
where.

“The Oldest Inhabitant”: Apropos Earle
as Attorney-General of Tasmania (B.
17/f)/T6). The first Attorney-General of
New South Wales was, also, not a lawyer.
Saxe Bannister had been an army captain,
and not having a military appointment to
give him, Lord Liverpool’s Government ap-
pointed him A.-G., and sent him out to
Brisbane, Governor of N.S.W., 1821-1825.
Wentworth and Wardell were the only bar-
risters in Sydney then, and they took fre-
quent opportunities of pointing out the
deficiencies of Bannister. No doubt some
such criticism led to the bloodless duel with
Wardell, because next morning Bannister
threw up his brief and £l2OO and left for
the Cape of Good Hope. Eventually he went
to London and engaged in literary work.
Hugh Campbell, M.L.A. for Glenelg
(Vic.), led a heavyweight deputation up to
the Minister for Agriculture the other day,
and asked that part of the Victorian wheat
harvest should be loaded into oversea
steamers from the new Portland pier. Dele-
gates from 10 shires and four boroughs were
included in the assemblage, with one M.H.R.,
two M’s.L.C. and four M’s.L.A.; but the*
best Minister Hagelthorn could do was to
say that “it might be possible to get a
tramp steamer to load wheat at Portland.”
Pinned firmly down by Edward White,
M.L.C. for the Western Province, the writhing
Hagelthorn at length definitely promised one
tramp steamer, “if not more.” Allowing for
the difficulties in the way—and the first and
last of these seems to be the high freight-
rate, which makes shipping pirates inde-
pendent and haughty—it looks a pretty poor
attempt at carrying out the State Govern-
ment’s alleged policy of decentralisation.
The Peacock Government has begun to
economise by turning off the baby’s rations.
After having the nerve to squander £7oO on
the Parliamentary picnic to Gippsland, it
took Melbourne’s breath away by withdraw-
ing the yearly grant of £lOOO to the Lady
Talbot Institute, which is unable to continue
its charitable distribution of milk. _ Peacock
is a splendid sort of financial penitent.
This is the revised pledge now demanded of
its members by the Political Labor Council
in Vic.: “I hereby pledge myself to the
P.L.G. of Victoria to faithfully uphold to
the best of my ability its constitution and
platform, and to work and vote for the
selected candidate. I also affirm that I am
not a member of any other organisation
which selects or lends support to candidates
for public positions.” As the P.L.C.’s chief
business is, and always has been, the selec-
tion and support of candidates for public
positions, in order to push its own political
barrow, it seems a reasonable thing to seek
such an assurance from its members. The
game of running with the hare and hunting
with the hounds has frequently been played
by certain P.L.C. members of late years, and
the new pledge should help to abolish the
distracting practice.
“Pint Pot”: I am not one of the aged
and experienced red-tapists whom “Stenog”
entreats (B. 17/2/T6) to tell him why
typewriting is barred on birth registration
forms; but I can tell him that when
the typewriting machines first appeared
there was doubt as to how their
script would stand the test of time. The
permanent legibility of original documents
dealing with births, etc., is obviously all-
important ; and public departments in charge
of stich records rightly insisted on the use
of the long-proven pen and ink as against
a new-fangled invention which had still to
make good. Experience shows they were
right. It is not uncommon to find that type-
written documents of those days are now so
faded as to be barely (if at all) legible,
while manuscript of the same date is as
clear as when first written. As there is no
evidence thus far that the work of present-
day machines is any more permanent, Red
Tape shows an unusual streak of horse-sense
in continuing the embargo on the typewriter
for official forms.
“E.F.”: Announced that the policy of the
Vic. Railway Commissioners .as regards
casual German or Austrian employees is
that there should be no “victimisation.” Oh,
dear friends, let us avoid victimisation. Let
us not remove any casual German
or Austrian from their billets. “No
victimisation in national warfare!” It is
the creed of the big gun which smashes the
beleagured city and citizens; of the torpedo
that sinks the passenger ship; of the Zep-
pelin bomb that kills the British baby in its
bed but spares the Vic. Railway Commis-
sioner—for the present. No victimisation !
Chalk it on the wall of the German Club!
Write it on the back of the confiscated trade-
mark ! No victimisation. Let us be pre-
pared to suffer it ourselves, dear friends,
as often as the Germans can give
it to us. By the way, what is vic-
timisation? The family of five that got
wiped out by a Zeppelin bomb in Stafford-
shire the other w r eek might have explained
the signification of the term; but they are
silent on the subject.

Premier Vaughan, of S.A., has been hav-
ing a look round Kangaroo Island, Austra-
lia’s biggest isle, which was settled before
Adelaide existed, but has more or less stood
still ever since. He told the residents that
their mostly-ironstone country does not seem
fitted for cereals; but every place seems to
be set down as “can’t grow wheat” at start-
ing, and certainly K.I. produces a lot of
barley already. Root crops and fruit do
well. “A million acres with a climate equal
to Tasmania” is the official claim, and
Vaughan does not see why a lot of returned
soldiers should not go on to it. Also the
isjand, which has several decent rivers, and
some of whose wildest countrv is set apart
as a reserve for the native beasts and birds
which the accursed fox is wiping out ou
the mainland, should he a great tourist
resort. Someday, when the mainland rail-
way creeps down as far as Cape .Tervois. and
the present eight hours’ voyage is cut down
to one hour, it may be.

“Harrison O.”: As one who has voted the
Labor ticket for years I would admire to
know if the Australian Ministry has secretly
decided on a policy of “preference to
foreigners”? If not it is time O’Malley,
the serio-comic eagle from the Rocky Moun-
tains, was thrown down a steep place or
handed over to the public hairdresser. The
other day it transpired that two Australian
railway engineers are now in the U.S.A.
(the Mecca towards which O’Malley faces
when he kneels in prayer) on behalf of the
Commonwealth, studying “transportation
problems,” and, it is alleged, with authority
to enter into contracts for the supply of
cars. The latter allegation is formally-
denied by the water-lily from the Rockies,
but he admits that he intends im-
porting “sample cars” in connection with the
transcontinental railway. For about 40
years Australia, with railway workshops in
all the State capitals, has been turning out
rolling stock as good as —and in some in-
stances better than —any imported. Vic-
torian Railway Commissioner Jones says
that he has travelled over the principal rail-
ways of the world and has never seen any
cars to beat the best on the Victorian lines.
The Commissioners state definitely that
there is no kind of car which cannot be built
at the Newport workshops. And yet
O’Malley has the hide to remark: “It will
be necessary, when the Commonwealth sets
up its own workshops, that we should have
officers who understand rolling stock con-
struction.”

“It behoved every true Liberal to be up

hurst,” Wade continued, “was the only elec-

maintained the flag against the recent ad-
vance of Germany.” (Again, the D.T. ren-
ders Germany as “Socialism.”) “There
were a great many well-to-do people who
could give valuable assistance, if not by
membership, then by contributions, in the
matter of the War " Loan.” (The D.T.’s
account substitutes “propaganda work” for
War loan.) “After all, it was the electors
who suffered by the advance of the wild
schemes of Germany rather than the de-
feated soldier.” (Again there is the mis-
print of “Socialism” for Germany.) “Mr.

enlisting by each one being responsible for

ship” for enlisting, and you get the mangled

[Obviously Wade meant “men at the

Hire of taxi-cab for Mrs. Holman, £1 10s.; decora-
tions for Mrs. Holman’s at-home, £1 Is. — Items
of N.S.W. Government expenditure challenged by
Auditor-General.
A dragon with a thousand eyes
The city lies across the night;
I see its breath in smoke-clouds rise
And from it fire-trains take their flight.
Towards dark wharves that check the seas
Ship-shadows glide with gifts of coal,
And on the wings of ev’ry breeze
Are wafted sounds that sear the soul.
An unseen taxi hoots its pain,
And then no more is heard. A bell
Rings faintly once, and I am fain
To think the guests are eating well.
Familiar things take evil shapes,
And old, old noises make me jump.
(Since I have read of party drapes
The country bought, I’ve got the hump.)
A falling star but makes me think
Of taxis for the Premier’s wife;
Out of the blue I watch it blink,
Cutting star-traffic like a knife.
Perhaps we too will end like that—
One incandescent dash through space,
Shedding fierce auditors and fat,
And spilling bills about the place.
N.S.W.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Two of the results of last Saturday’s
racing at Caulfield were encouraging to the idea
that imported horses will Cut a foremost dash ir
several forthcoming events. Tullia (Bst.), the Eng
lish mare from Sydney, got away unexpectedly with
the Oakleigh Plate, in which Eurobin (9st. 111 b.)
took principal honors by running into fourth plac(
after being rather badly placed. • And Cyklon wor
the 9-furlongs St. George’s Stakes in commanding
style. Traquette, who made most of the running as
usual, may not have been at her very best, but ir
giving 51b. to the Victorian flier and beating hei
two furlongs from home the imported racer showed
himself in great nick, anyhow. Cyklon and Eurobir
will be backed to beat the unproven Redfern when
they meet, but if this other vaunted Britisher is
the gee that fancy paints him he should at least
capture the rich Futurity Stakes next Saturday.
Grand Fleet (late Klinger), the ex-hurdle
racer that Eric Connolly owns and trains, captured
the Bond Cup with a determined effort at the finish.
The performance will hardly shorten his previously
short price for the Australian Cup. A 71b. penalty
for this win brings Grand Fleet’s Cup weight up tc
Bst. 41b. He has a big back for carrying a moderate
burden, and he appears to be built for long journeys;
so if he goes on well in the meantime the ex-hurdler
must have a good chance on March 7.
* * * *
Every blindfolded punter in the country
ought to have backed Tullia for the Oakleigh Plate.
Brunton’s mare ran second to Athenic at Warwick
Farm, and then started second favorite for the Chal-
lenge Stakes at Randwick, and for five and a hali
furlongs shovved her heels to everything. Then she
collapsed. Since then she has had lots of work put
on to her. The Oakleigh Plate is five and a half fur-
longs. One lucky punter had a fiver on at starting
price—33 to I—and1 —and thought 5 to 1 would be a fair
thing. However, he transhipped nothing back to
Jerusalem. Sydney-owned horses have brought home
the Oakleigh money four years in succession. Meri-
mee is under the same roof as Tullia, and has the
Newmarket in view.
Among the war-loan investors was Ned
Moss ( £20,000), who made nearly all his pile on
the racecourse, and not as a Bar Von either. In his
early day's Moss sold racebooks for a living, and got
his start through backing a double. Many' men in
the betting game began with as little as nothing.
One big bookmaker used to get ss. a night for tak-
ing tickets at a boxing hall, and another used to be
a “runner” on the outer-outer in the old pony days.
The biggest of them all was a hairdresser in a town
further west than Sunset. And hundreds of others
have come from the broke stage to affluence. Yet
the game is no "ood. They say so, anyhow.
**‘ * *
“Penciller”: Melbourne’s \x>lice are
doing their best to make a sti'angled industry of the
bobkmaking that goes on outside Jack ‘ Wren’s
Richmond (Vie.) racecourse, but the profits of the
traffic are such that it is not seriously impeded by
the fines imposed. Further, there is no beastly pride
about the kerbstone bookv, one man scorning not to
accept a bet of all even threepence on the favorite in
the Sisters’ Purse. The real settling-dav was at
Richmond Court on 14/2/’l6—St. Valentine’s morn-
ing—when three men were fined sutis aggregating
£9O for street betting at the benefit meeting for
charities on November 11. Last time there was a
similar field day, fines running into £lOO were im-
posed, but only £3 was recovered from the finees.
Oil the present occasion the highest bet noted by the
Spion Ivops, as Alf Vincent once called them, was
3s. on a 2 to 1 chance. Previously, when eggs were
Is. 6d. a dozen, a dairy produce-dealer who was pass-
ing the course in his cart pulled up and wagered
half a dozen of the wobblers that his fancy would
win. The pony failed to justify its choice, and Ikev
carried home the hen output.
* * * *
“ Vic”: Speaker Frank Madden, brother
ot the voluble John, has just been reminiscing after a
jaunt to sporting Maribyrnong to see the mili-
tary camp. He attended the auction of Hurtle
fishers Maribyrnong stud in 1866, when £28,000
was realised. Madden remembers seeing Fisherman.
horse won about 60 English races. A weight-
for-age race was then almost as safe for him as a
walkover. Handicaps were comparatively few He
was angular, brown, almost ugly, but hardy,
clean-limbed, wiry, and reached Australia sound as a
bell after all his tremendous work. Madden tots up
with a characteristic touch for all of the men
he recollects having seen at the Maribyrnong auction
btandish, v\ aldock, Lang, Goldsbrough, Keighran
Wilson, Yeomans, Chapman, Bond, L. L. Smith, the
lowers, Greene, Pearson, Yuille, Craig, Morrison,
Fugate, Chwnside, Cleeland, Cox, Coker, Goyder and
Ihompson.
* * * *
One well-known M.L. trainer of gee-gees
has a tale of long ago. He was then a lad in the
Harry Goodman, and once had to waste
slightly to ride. The morning of the race he was at
the right poundage, but mighty vacant, so Goodman
locked him in a room at the pub to make sure he
wouldn’t get to the dining-room before the race.
Biit, 10, when the kid was liberated he was heavier
than when he went in. Goodman always referred to
this case as a perfect mystery and proof that there
was a lot to learn about the formation of tissue.
J he boy never breathed that a housemaid had handed
an apple-pie through the window.
* * * ' *
A sport went to the la§t Wellington
(M.L.) races and he coupled Panmure in a double
with a nag that was immediately scratched. Next he
coupled Panmure with every moke in the Chip except
Bee, which he considered didn’t have a dogV chance
Bee romped home in record time. He had been
given £ 1 by a girl to invest on Bee, and as he forgot
to carry out the commission, he had to hand the
maiden £lB out of his own pocket. After that he
decided to leave things alone, and retreated to the
grandstand, but had just reached the landing when
the grandstand collapsed, and Death just missed him
by a whisker. At latest advices he was talking
about taking shelter in khaki.
* * * *
At a meeting at Karlsliorst in Germany
on the last Sunday in October £35,565 was rushed
through the tote in the day, which doesn’t look much
like general bankruptcy. Nineteen horses weeded out
of the Royal Graditz stud averaged' £127 apiece at
auction, while 24 rejects at Hoppegarten averaged
£lOl. At Vienna lately the biggest race was for
4160 sovs. War or no war, the gee-gee seems to be
going as usual in Germany and Austria save that
the animal is confined to fewer courses. Waiting for
these two countries to drop dead from exhaustion
seems likely to be a long business.
The surprise of the Hobart Cup meeting
was the hat-trick performed by Master Alwyne, a
five-year-old animal that had hitherto been consist-
ently arriving at the wrong end in poor company.
But in the three days of the meeting he started in
four races, and fled so rapidly round the course that
nothing could catch him over six furlongs, seven
furlongs or lj miles. And the stake-money accu-
mulated by all this hurry amounted to the stupendous
sum of if 220.
“Barcoo”: As a pilot of gee-gees this
sounds very near a record for Australia. Jimmy
Rogers, well known on metropolitan tracks, but now
riding in Central and Northern Queensland, rode
from June to December, 1915, 36 firsts, 33 seconds
and 20 thirds. There was something well-lined dur-
ing those six months for Rogers.
The most successful stallion during the
just-closed racing season in Austria was the English
nag Wool Winder, by Martagon, who, by the way,
is the father of Martian, the leading sire in Maori-
land. Wool Winder’s progeny accounted for 27 good
races in Franz Josefland during the season, which
were worth £13,500. Herr Mautner headed the list
of winning owners, his prads collecting £lB,OOO
during the season, but whether he got hard cash or
had to take the Kaiser’s lOU is not stated.
Les Darcy got among the heavies on
Saturday when he encountered Harold Hardwick at
Sydney Stadium. He also got one of his teeth
knocked out. Which is about all that happened,
except that in the seventh collision Harold was up-
ended three times in succession, whereupon his sec-
onds delivered the towel and the fray discontinued,
it is understood that Hardwick will now cut the
game and get back to being an accountant. He did
fairly well, financially, while scrapping, but never
ceased to be an amateur. He could not become a
hard-nailed pug. His bash was undermined bv affa-
bility.
* * * »
The Ryans had a family gathering at
Melb. Stadium on Saturday night, when Tommy put
Dodger in his place at the ninth round. The occur-
rence was as exciting as a sewing-bee, Thomas per-
mitting the more excitable Ryan to do all the bul-
locking, while he held a right in waiting and brought
the distressing affair to an end with a hefty smite to
the jaw. It was a nicely-timed wallop and gave the
crowd a chance to get a drink before the pub closed.
* * * *
At Brisbane on Saturday the Mick Iving-
“Red” Matson affair spread over 20 weary rounds,
and the referee said it was a draw. But earlier
than that the crowd reckoned it was about the limit.
The crowd was right.
FRANK THORN,
The Queensland pug who has been out after
championships for the last 50 years or so.
George Brown, the Greek, who came to
this country from Yankeeland with “Knock Out” as
an unchristian name, hasn’t done much in the knock-
out line. He spends much of his time in the ring
chewing gum and says “vow,” which is understood
to be the equivalent for “yes.” Also he doesn’t mind
whether his antagonist is black, white or brindle.
Neither does he care if he weighs a ton. When the
casual stranger was recently matched with Pat
Doran, the Melb. heavyweight, it was suggested to
K.O. that the descendant of Irish monarchy would be
two stone heavier. “What about it?” Brown was
asked. “D’you think you can beat him?” “Yow,”
said “Knock-Out” contentedly; and he may, too.

* * * *
Arthur Mailey (Balmain) was under the
limelight in Sydney grade-cricket on Saturday by
tumbling down eight wickets for 98 runs against
Gordon. Mailey is the ablest “curly” bowler of the
season, and thus far has taken 69 wickets at an
average of 16.17. The only other trundler to acquire
50 scalps is C. V. Grimmett (Sydney)—ss at an
average of 17.92.
* * •* #
On Saturday last, at the Sydney Cricket
Ground a 14-year-qld prodigy named Noel Levy
scored 100 not out for Waverley 111. v. Randwick
11. Apart from the score itself the manner of the
making thereof was a revelation. There is no stroke
that this youngster cannot play, and the power he
imparts to each is astonishing. He also uses his
feet with the ability of a veteran. He is squat and
sturdy, and should develop into a great player.
Earlier in the present season young Levy scored a
century for Bondi School against Paddington School:
Another promising , 15-year-old lad otit Waverley
way is Stevens, who will take about 100 wickets in
grade-cricket this season. He took four for 44 for
Waverley first-grade club against North Sydney.

“First Slip”: Those English opinions of
Trumper’s batting (B. 10/2/’l6) were interesting.
This is what Sherwell, the S'Afriean wicketkeeper,
had to. say at the close of Trumper’s 214 in the
third test against his team: “Well, I have seen the
best the world can show.” Writing in an African
paper, Sherwell said: “His timing was such per-
fection that to bals pitched on his off stump I fre-
quently had the ‘How s’ of an appeal out of my
mouth only to hear the- click- of the ball as he
brought off that marvellous under-leg glance.”

“F.S.”: About twins on record in
first-grade cricket (B. 3/2/’l6). Herewith my entry
of A. L. and F. Kenny, of South Melb. C. 0. A.L.
has represented lie., and plays regularly with the
first eleven. F. plays for the first, though more regu-
larly with the second. They are as alike as the off
and leg stumps—the same mannerisms, the same old
blue-serge suits and black hats, wet or fine. And what
The Bulletin suggests might happen with the
brothers Riley did actually happen with the Kennys.
While a S.M. eleven was playing against Clifton
Hill, A.L., who is the better batsman, having put
up his score towards wiping the opposite total off,
was sent in again with still 30 runs to get and two
wickets to fall. “Hey,” said the captain as Kenny
took block, “you’ve batted already.” “Oh, no,” said
Ken, “that was my brother.” “Fetch vour bro-
ther, then.” At the call Fred came out. “Well, I’m
hanged!” said the skipper. “It u>as this bloke, all
right—box on!” A.L. resumed his stand at the
wicket, and the score was promptly passed.
* * * »
Jack Barrett, who died at Peak Hill
(W.A.) recently, toured with the 1890 Australian
XI —a team which included Murdoch, Turner, Ferris,
Blaekham, Lyons and Hugh Tremble—and in an
extremely moist season finished on the batting list
second to Murdoch with 1305 runs at an average of
25.51. Barrett’s stonewalling worried the English
trundlers; and when the second innings of the test
ended at Lord’s (in which he scored 67 out of 176
in 4hr. 40min. of weariness) there was applause
worth hearing. After that trip Barrett left cricket
and settled down to a doctor's busy life. He had
only reached the half-century when the Umpire
called.
* * * «
In first-grade cricket over at Christchurch
(M.L.) the other day a team went to the sticks
requiring 171 to avert an innings defeat. One of
the first pair hurriedly biffed up 20, but the rest
of the calamitous team, including the reliable Extras,
could only collect six more. Their main explanation
was that R. Read insisted on getting seven sticks
for nine runs before they could dig themselves in.

ated in U.S.A. recently, when H. E. Vollmer of
Columbia ’Varsity, reduced the 150yds. record’ for
25ft. baths, by getting oyer the stretch in lmin.
29 4-ssec. Charles Daniels set up the previous
figures (lmin. 32 2-ssec.) in 1911. Australia’s long-
swimming stretches knock out any need of worrying
over Yank records in a dish scarcelv big enough to
drown a cat in.

“Nymsie”: The Ma State’s duck-slaugh-
tering _ season opened a fortnight earlier than the
Victorian shambles. Therefore, as the NSW law
permits unlimited killing and a Vic. statute limits
the bag to 10, a regiment of “sports” motored across
the trickle and in the swamps where the wildfowl
breed had a real gory time. A party of four from
the Northcote Gun Club, and a similar number from
Bacchus Marsh arrived at Thule from the scene
of assassination a week after the State Governor’s
party opened fire. They had accounted for ”1200 ”
ducks, mostly teal and wood-duck, after relieving
the monotony by slaughtering snakes. In Vic.
most of the birds shot were infested with large-sized
lice, but were in good condition. The 15-bird limit
is a farce. Parties pool their birds and lookers-on
share in the diversion. It is time the States fell
into line regarding conservation of native game,
with certain wide expanses of river and swamp
reserved as inviolable sanctuaries.
The bowls championship of South Aus-
tralia has been won by Clarence Moody, formerly
of Adelaide Register and foundation-editor of the
Mail. It was a popular win, for Moody— who hqs
just come back to the green after several seasons’
absence — was practically the introducer of the game
to the holy city.

Another good score was put up under bad
conditions at Rand wick last Saturday. Shooting at
900 and 1000 yards VV. A. Duly, allowing from “30
to 34 of wind,” scored 32 aiid 33—65 out of a
possible 70. Apart from the difficulty of holding a
rifle steady against such a breeze (and the least
movement of a rifle at 1000yds. is a miss), 34 of
wind at 1000yds. means pointing the weapon at a
spot about 29ft. from the object aimed at. In good
hduds the modern military rifle is a marvellously-
aecurate weapon. Pity Billjim does not get more
training in its use. Apropos of accurate shooting:
The. writer on the "morning of Lone Pine charge in
Gallipoli saw a lieutenant who was directing opera-
tions in an Australian trench put his hand on the top
bag of a parapet and say “I want the sandbags put
there.” A Turkish sniper immediately shot the
hand nearly off.

“C.” : Young Donald Fraser, a Double
Bay schoolboy and junior member of the East Syd-
ney Swimming Club, will be on top of the heap
before many years are past. Even now he is crawl-
stroking his way amongst the best that haven’t
shouldered a gun. His performances in the last few
weeks stamp him as a top-notcher. Short and
sturdy, 13 years old, game, possessing a natural
crawl stroke, he has all the essentials for the making
of a champion. His good heart was expressed by
himself recently. Said he, “When I’m racing and
I feel tired I know the others must be that way too.
So 1 just hang on and generally manage to beat the
other chap.” That’s the spirit of champions more
than anything else. Fraser is certainly the most
promising of all the boy swimmers in N, S. Wales.
And Ma will need somebody to take the place of
Longworth and Healy, both gone to the war.
When Charles Byron, a returned M.L-
soldier, was sued the other day for a debt of £7>
he informed the magistrate that he had awakened
that morning to find he had won £lBOO in Tatt. s.
Unsuccessful investors in the same sweep complain (
that Fortune needn’t have used a drayload of coin
to get a man out of such a small financial hole.

“D.F.” : Premier Earle (Tas.) has got an
idea about financing returned soldiers who
want to go on the land. He suggests that
the Commonwealth or Imperial Govern-
ment should capitalise the pensions of those
men who have something in that form com-
ing to them, and that the lump sum thus
obtained should be put into improving the
farm and keeping the farmer going till he
can make his living off the soil. Earle
thinks the money might be paid over to the
State in which the soldier is going to settle,
and that the State could advance it as
required by the settler and pay him interest
on the rest. Where the Commonwealth is
going to get the money for the lump sums
Earle doesn’t say. The usual place
probably.

Lewis Waller in a will of 200 words dis-
posed of an estate valued at £5545. Probate
has been granted to bis brother Victor
Lewis. The estate is divided equally between
son Edmund and daughter Nancy, but Nancy
cannot draw her share until she is 21, unless
she should get married in the meantime. It
is a hundred to one that Nancy will put in a
claim two or three years before the time-
limit arrives. If she doesn’t, it won’t be
due to lack of offers to care for and cherish
lier.
Jimmy Glover, conductor at Drury
Lane, was again in his seat on Boxing Day
drawing out the music for “Puss iu Boots.”
James also talked a couple of columns about
his quarter-century’s association with the
theatre and its neighbor, Covent Garden.
One of his yarns: In the height of the
operatic season the prima donna for a certain
evening suddenly fell ill. It was Melba’s
night off. She had to be found to fill the
breach. Jimmy knew she was somewhere
on the river in a fashionable house-boat. But
there were scores of these boats, and he had
uo idea which of them was Melba’s. So he
cruised about among them shouting “Melba !”
“Melba!” When she heard him she replied
with a coo-ee, climbed into his canoe and
was paddled to the rescue. James says that
King Edward when Prince of Wales used
to go behind the scenes “to observe the
working of the comic effects at close quar-
ters.” Of course. What else was there to
observe at close quarters?
“Quidam’s” narrative:—
When the Crocodile diggings were booming
in the ’sixties, a theatrical actor-manager,
whose leading (and only) lady was his wife,
decided to try a season there. The goldfield
hall was built of calico, and the dressing-rooms were
very small, but Art, when stimulated by promise of
abundant shekels, despises such trivialities. The
hall was packed to the doors, and most of the super-
fluous population stood outside seeking for inter-
esting bits of shadow-work. All was going joyously
until, during a scene when the manager was doing
some hero business on the stage, while the heroine
slid into a change of costume in a dressing-room,
the lady happened to lean against the wall. With
pretty wit an outsider instantly jabbed a pin into
that part that bulged the wall the most, and with a
wild shriek and very little else the injured actress
bounded into the centre of the stage. When the
true cause of this indelicate and energetic action
on the part of his spouse had been conveyed to the
stricken hero, he dropped the curtain, and indicated
in vehement and eloquent language his opinion of
the nature of the savage of Crocodile. Old residents
aver that the melancholy incident concluded the first
and last theatrical venture on Crocodile in its
palmy days, but no member of the audience com-
plained of not having received his money’s worth.
Bland Holt’s old success, “The Derby
Winner,” has been embalmed in flicker in
Fogland and will probably be seen on the
screen in Australia. The lead is taken by
Eve Balfour, who hails from Christchurch
(M.L.). Her real name is Eva Hulstou,
and a few years back her dad had a small
draper’s shop in the Flat City. Eva, a
languid, willowy beauty, married an artist
named Balfour, who spent a few years in
Ghch doing portraits and figure studies.
Eight years ago Balfour took his wife to
The Smoke, and there she decided on the
stage. She got a start with Tree, worked
hard, and that, plus good looks, rushed her
along. In a short time she was playing
leads in Shakspeare, and made a hit as
Katherine in “The Taming of the Shrew.”
Recently she was handed the leading part
in a picture representation of “Three
Weeks,” and made a great success of it.
“Macko”: A one-act play, “War Mates,”
was recently produced at the Victoria
Palace, London. The author, Herbert de
Hamel, is a soldier and was in the famous
L< ndon-Scottish charge last year at Y.,res,
where he was badly wounded. “War Mates”
should be given a place on the programme c*
every theatre and music-hall in the Com-
monwealth. You get an idea of the tren-
chant dialogue and fine purpose from this
speech of a young V.C. to a striker: —
You have your strike. Stand up for your'rights!
Why, a two-days’ strike will only mean about 4000
of your mates following Tom and going under. Ain’t
this a free country? You ain’t bound to serve nor
work your damnedest unless you blamed well choose.
Hang the munitions! Hang your pals! Hang your
bloomin’ country! You stand up for your rights.
Don’t let any dirty non-union men come in and make
the shells that’ll save your sons’ lives. Your son is
being blown to ribbons —choked with gas—fried with
liquid fire—-tortured body and soul. But don’t you
do an hour’s overtime to help him until you have
squeezed the last penny out of your bosses. Stand up
for your freedom —till the Germans come and take it
from you. I’ve seen what they do to the villages
they’ve captured, and, by God, it’ll be a damned
sight worse if they get here!
“Harrison O.” : It is typical of the Yan-
kee playwright that if he brews a farce he
must empty in a pint or two of bathos. Wal-
lingford the crook, after getting rich quickly,
held high converse with a/detective anent
the joys of regeneration and the beauty of
domesticity, with special reference to the
cheild. “Broadway Jones” ended with the
ridiculous oration about the “Great White
Way,’* which is inhabited by sweet-faced
women with ’ard ’earts, and hard-faced men
as gentle as doves. Now we have “It Pays
to Advertise,” which makes a virtue of irre-
sponsibility, but has the usual two pints of
bathos. The young man who cheerfully pro-
motes a bogus company must towards the
end acquire unnecessary qualms and become
sorrowful at the thought of his wickedness;
his millionaire father, who has told the
youth that he must shift for himself, allows
the native hue of resolution to be sickbed
o’er with a pale cast of sentiment, and, find-
ing that blood is thicker than bishness, goes
about helping his offspring in an absurdly
surreptitious and expensive manner; and
even the brazen-faced, leather-lunged can-
vasser, who has started the young man on
the crooked path, grows maudlin and offers
in husky .tones to break with the lad and
let the dollars go if it will help to reconcile
pa and his boy. It seems to be a fixed idea
of the Yankee dramatist (and presumably
he has justification for the faith that is in
him) that it is not safe to offer even a farce
to the great neutral public unless it has a
suppressed sob lurking beneath its surface
frivolity.

The Bar of New South Wales, following
the example of the Bar of Great Britain,
has decided against the manufacture of
more silk gowns (K.C’sj while the war
lasts. The Attorney-General is agreeable,
excepting that if a K.C. gets on the Bench,
is killed at the Front, or is otherwise posted
missing, a successor may be appointed.
The active K.O’s at the N.S.W. Bar number
14-—Wade. Ralston, Knox, Shand, Owen,
Lamb, Campbell. Gannon, Garland, Loxton,
Canaway, Leverrier, Rolin and Blackett.
Another historic old estate, Vine Lodge,
the home of the late F. A. Badgery at
Exeter (N.S.W.), goes to the auctioneer’s
hammer. James BadgeFy, the pioneer of
the family, had his first home at Badgery’s
Creek, at the head of South Creek, west of
Liverpool, and died there in 1821. His
eldest son Henry, aged 18, thrilled with the
glowing accounts poured into him by nearby
neighbor Dr. Throsby, who had just returned
with Hamilton Hume from an exploring expe-
dition through the Bong Bong and Argyle
country as far as Lake Bathurst, pushed
out and selected his home at Sutton Forest,
where he got a grant. He called his place
Exeter, because his father had come from
Exeter in Devonshire. Young Badgery
made his*property a wealth-producer, mar-
ried and fathered a dozen squatter sons.
He died at his old home in ISSI. Most of
his sons have since followed him into the
shades and the old estate will probably be
split up for closer settlement.

j yvhear Roberts on the luck of mining :
Kielsle fortune plays more fantastic tricks in
mining than in any other industry. In the diggings
■lavs it was a common occurrence for men to aban-
don shafts before bottoming, and for others to go
down and with a few strokes of the pick strike it
rich The frequency of this gave rise «to the Irish-
ism' “Never leave a hole until you’ve sunk it a
foot deeper.” There were instances of new-chums
handling gold, and, not knowing what it was,
rejecting it. These instances were much more
numerous than might he supposed, a* many a
puddling- machine man can testify.
I have a vivid recollection of a remarkable example
0 f . w hy the vividness of it should remain after
a lapse of nearly 50 years will be understood by all
gold-men when I state that the forerunner of it was
the panning-off of a dish of crushed cement for over
40oz. of gold. 1 was the “nipper” of a party
that was working in one of the table-hills on the
Loddon (Vic.). We were cutting up the cemented
bottom in worked-out ground. Results had not been
too good for some time, and the show was to be
abandoned. One of the pickmen struck a patch of
cement as black as jet, and a dishful of it was sent
out for the nipper to pound up. He soon realised
that he had something tougher than usual to deal
with, and when the men took a hand at the job and
found that they were trying to break up lumps of
gold-Mrom the size of walnuts to filberts —the yells
of joy might have been heard miles away. There
were not more than a couple of barrow-loads of this
stuff, but it yielded nearly £lOOO worth of gold.
It was seemingly an isolated patch of that sort of
cement, and it had been passed over by former
workers as worthless. All the gold was heavily
coated with manganese oxide.
One of the party remarked that in one of the
gutters in an adjoining hill, in which he had worked
in the earliest times, all the gold was coated like
that, and he suggested that the site of the first
waterhole where panning and cradling had been
carried on might be worth prospecting. It was
promptly tested, with the result that from about 50
loads of debris sent to the battery over £l2OO worth
of gold was won. There were nuggets up to 20oz.
flattened out under the stampers. Quicksilver was
not used in this mill, and I shall never forget the
enthusiasm of the men when the cleaning out of the
box was in progress as lump after lump of glisten-
ing gold was extracted. The sight of another rich
clean-up would be a more effective heart-stimulant
for me than a 30-gallon barrelful of liq. strych.,
digitalis and other drugs the doctors have been pour-
ing into me for a long time.
Misses in lode mining have been none the less
remarkable. Here are a couple of instances within
my own experience.
A few years since C. H. Kerry and myself owned
a lease at Yerranderie (N.S.W.), known as the Pyra-
mid. It was an end-on block to Bartlett’s, but did
not include the outcropping of the lode. We sank a
vertical shaft in the expectation of striking the lode
at about' 200 ft. We reached that depth, but without
meeting with the slightest indication of the prox-
imity of the lode. For its full depth the shaft was
in dense, jointless felsite. We made a proposal to
the owners of the outcrop block for an amalgama-
tion, with the intention to drive in their direction
from the bottom of our shaft, but we could not come
to terms. Then we decided to abandon the show,
and it was sold to Bartlett for less than the cost of
the plant. In a few weeks the extension of the level
from his mine revealed the fact that had we sunk
our shaft 13ft. more we should have hit the largest
and richest pipe of ore ever seen on the field.
An incident in connection with this mine reveals
the mysterious ways of the N. S. Wales Mines De-
partment. We asked that it should exercise its
powers under the law, and make an underground sur-
vey of the adjoining mine so as to determine the
general trend and dip of the lode. It was not with-
out some trouble that the survey was made. Natu-
rally, as this work was done at our request, we
thought that we should be supplied with the infor-
mation which it disclosed, or, at any rate, such of
it as was relevant to our block. But on making'
application for it we met with a point-blank refusal,
it being alleged that it was strictly private, or,
lather, “for the use of the Department only.” Of
'that earthly use it could be to the Department to
consign such plans to a secret pigeon-hole one can-
not imagine!
At Wyalong a syndicate with which I was associ-
ated had done a lot of prospecting in the hope of
finding a downward continuation of some of the rich
•uns of ore. To expedite the work we had an air-
compressing and rock-drilling plant installed. We
>ad driven over 80ft. on a pencil-streak joint which
was supposed to be the track of the lode, and as we
were then a long way past where we expected to get
or ®’ w as decided to discontinue the drive. The
or er to stop was given just before crib-time, when
'•ght holes had been drilled. It was thought that
1 would be a waste of good dynamite to charge
lem ‘ Had they been charged and fired that day
e s ‘ 10l, ld have seen the wedge-end of a block of
re which produced over £50,000 worth of gold. It
"as left to the luck of others to make the discovery.
The wiseacre after the event will declare “shock-
mgly bad judgntent.” Granting that that is a good
assumption, one may honestly add that it is devilish
had luck to have such faulty judgment.
Recent events on Ivalgoorlie Golden Mile
(YV.A.) show how worse than weak some
people with big jobs have been. Since the
beginning of the war labor unions and the
Ivalgoorlie press had persistently complained
of the employment of unnaturalised enemy
subjects on the mines. On a few occasions
a score or so of objectionables were interned,
and the statement was given out that no
more were left. One pressman who affirmed
the contrary was practically told he was a
house agent. Defence Minister Pearce when
appealed to made evasive answers. Then tAie
mine workers got to business in their own way.
They threatened to strike if a solitary for-
eigner turned up to toil without a naturali-
sation certificate in his hand. The result up
to last week was that over 200 enemies had
been put off the Mile alone. When affiliated
localities are dealt with the number will
probably be doubled. These enemy subjects
were not onlv unnaturalised, but arrogant as
well.
“Hamer”: Air. Justice Ferguson made the
proud boast the other day that every boy
whom the members of the N.S.W. Supreme
Court Bench had in their families fit and
eligible to go into the khaki ranks is there,
His own second son is about to leave.
Colonel Norman McLaurin was 12 months
in Ferguson s chambers; Major Jim McMan-
amey shared Ins chambers for many years ;
and Lieutenant Lawrence Street was his
Associate. These three the war has slain ;
and two more of his Honor Fergusons
Associates have moved on to the Front.

oi low alcoholic strength which will brnfg

So the wowser has done a good thing, hut

John Win thro p Ilackctt, who died in the
West a few days ago, was introduced to life
in 1848. Twenty-six years later he was
called to the Irish Bar, and in the following
year reached Sydney. As briefs did not
overwhelm him he did some leader writing
for the Hekat.d, but soon tired of it and
moved ou to Alelbourne, where he was vice-
warden and lecturer on constitutional law
and logic at Trinity College for six years,
Then he had to retire through standing for
a country seat in the Assembly in opposi-
tion to the wishes of some of the College
heads. In that election Hackett lost his
deposit. He put up a better tight a year or
two later at Sandridge, when John Madden
dished him by only a narrow majority,
Hackett reached the‘West in 1882, and after
failing at sheep farming owing to drought
he joined the late Thomas Cockburn Canjp-
bell in running the West Australian, then
a tri-weekly. The paper was issued daily from
1885, and two years later Hackett became
editor. When the State obtained constitu-
tional government he got a seat in the
Council and sat uninterruptedly until his
death.
Last week’s heat-wave in Adelaide 92,
95, 99, 102, 103 and 100 ou consecutive days
—enraged a hush-tire that closely threatened
the National Park. It was also touch-and-
go with Long Gully and Upper Sturt sta-
tions on the inter-State main line. This
was the chance of the soldiers in the camp
at Mitcham , Nvho we re right on the way to
the disturbance. To a call for volunteers all
handg responded. Commandant Sandford
hurried to the front with 400 men, and Camp-
Commandant Stuart took up another big
squad . That settled the trouble.

“Fort Scratchley”: The Commonwealth
having re-established the examination of
mas ters and mates at Newcastle, the Ala
, ancien t pilot steamer Ajax is running
. . , L . , , ,
. <,n the job. About a dozen pilo sta
ia turn to be examination officers, two at a
time. The work comes as a godmothei send
to the pilots, as, owing to ships not visiting
Newcastle frequently nowadays there is but
l l ttle piloting work available, lor this tvxtia
work the Commonwealth pays them,idOO
a year* Ihe anomaly about it is
that two of them, Barron and Y\ hiteman,
volunteered for active service as privates, and
were accepted at Gs. a day. lhe State pays
them the difference between their _ old
salary and the six shillings. So the position
boiled down is that pilots who stay at home
in secure jobs receive an extra £3OO a year ;
while those who are about to risk their
lives in the trenches receive nothing at all
in the shape of additional pay. This shows
what is sometimes possible in an intelligent
democracy.
SIX O’CLOCK.
THE UNKNOWN PATIENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Within the heart of Jones is joy—
Although the Kaiser does not cease
The world with slaughter to annoy—
He reckons this a time of peace.
For now, when midnight sees him home,
His wife’s objections he can mock:
He was not on a beery roam—
The pubs are closed at six o'clock !
Now office cares can keep us late
And never cause a wifely sneer ;
All talk of bars is out of date—
Our sober honesty is clear!
From hard work homeward have we slunk;
And, if with some fatigue we rock,
It calls for sleep—we can’t be drunk—■
The pubs are closed at six o’clock 1
Let bottlemakers reign as kings
And levy tribute on each cup;
The order new an ally brings.
Our stock in truth is looking up ;
For we are stayed by accident,
An office call, or tramway block :
On sprees we could not well be bent—
The pubs are closed at six o'clock !
But Robinson has spoilt his game,
And wrecked his hopes by careless play,
Because the tale he tried to frame
The evidence put quite away.
He waved a bottle in his hand,
Last of the evening’s shining stock —-
“Not dhrunk,” he said, “you un’erstand,
Sh’ pubs all closhe at shix o’clock.”
N.S.W.
H. M.

holidays and health.
The object of a holiday is usually to gain
renewed energy for another year’s work. A
difficulty is that holidays are generally not
sufficiently long to accomplish their purpose.
Taded people go away for a fortnight when
tHev need a month or six weeks. Very
often a holiday becomes a round of sight-
seeing that is as wearying as work, and
the holiday-maker returns as unfit as when
he went away.
If possible the holiday should be started
in reasonable health, and if you are feeling
tired, weary and limp, as most people do in
our ’Australian summers when the blood
becomes thin and impoverished, you should
not wait for your holidays, but begin now
to build up your blood with a true tonic,
and so invigorate and refresh your system.
Nothing is more valuable for this purpose
than Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. Over and
over again they have proved to be as good
as a holiday to jaded men and women. If
you cannot take a holiday, the value of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills is even greater, for
they make good the waste caused by illness
and the wear and tear of life. _ Any dealer
can supply you with Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills.— Advt.
Constipation or Comfort ?
There can be neither comfort nor good
health where constipation exists. Most
people suffer from this cause at intervals;
many are chronic victims.
The mistake many people make in treat-
ing this complaint is in adhering to the old-
time nauseating doses of some powerful pur-
gative that leaves the condition worse than
before. In the new laxative, Pinkettes, is
presented a dainty sugar coated oval that
is free from unpleasant effects, does not up-
set the stomach nor gripe, but simply gives
nature the needed assistance. You can get
a free trial sample by mentioning The
Bulletin, and applying to the Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co, Sydney; or any dealer will
sell you a full-sized bottle for Is. — Advt,

BUSINESS AS USUAL.
The Management of the “Australia”
Hotel are gratified to state that, not-
withstanding the upset caused by the
recent fire they are able to announce
“business as usual.” The service in
the Grand Dining Room, on the
Balcony, and in the Winter Garden
is being conducted in the same style
that has made the “Australia” Hotel
so popular with its patrons, while the
arrangements for Private Dinners and
Suppers are working with their accus-
tomed satisfaction.
Throughout the holiday season
arrangements for Afternoon Teas will,
as usual, be carried out in the Winter
Garden, which has been running con-
tinuously throughout.
J. URE SMITH, Manager.

IN BANANA LAND
“Mulga” : Twenty years back the number
of artesian bores in the Commonwealth was
very small. Now there are close on 2600,
with an estimated flow of 650,000,000 gal-
lons daily. Bananaland has the deepest
bore, the flow being struck at 5045 ft.; also
the shallowest (10ft.). At one bore the
traveller has the billy practically boiling for
him, the temperature being 208 degrees. The
coolest is 60 degrees. Two bores threw over
5,000,000 gallons daily on being first struck,
but were muzzled later and urged to be calm.
As a result of the recent perturbations in
sugar prices and the unfavorable conditions
for the old-time producers who still survive,
the sugar-land about Ingham, the Herbert
and the country north of Mackay generally
is passing rapidly into the hands of Greeks
and Italians. The old owners sell on long
terms, and the alien cultivators succeed by
toiling longer hours and putting up with
harder conditions. The plantations are
worked chiefly on the share system, so labor
troubles are unknown. If Antonio or
Demetrius employ a unionist they work be-
side him to make certain he carves out full
value for his log wages.
“McNorth”: I see it is proposed to set
aside certain areas of prickly-pear country
for the use of returned soldiers, no doubt as
a cheap means of expressing the country’s
gratitude for services rendered, and, at the
same time, doing something towards destroy-
ing the vegetable octopus. It seems a heart-
felt way of giving thanks, especially as
prickly-pear lands can be had almost for the
asking, and Billjim is likely to cast much
lurid language at the proposal. If the Gov-
ernment honestly wants to make a cocky
of the returned soldier what’s wrong with
introducing him to the Far North, where
there are no vegetable or other curses?—only
calm, blue seas, sunny skies, healthy climate,
fertile lands and everything else that is truly
Australian.
“Nemo”: Itev. Eugene Walsh, who was
boss at the R.C. Cathedral, Cairns (N.Q.),
for eight years, has had a good look round
before settling down as chief of his Order
in Kent (Eng.). He first w-ent to the
Philippine Islands, thence to Hong Kong,
Shanghai and Japan. From Yokohama he
shipped to America, and explored that
strange land before starting for Liverpool.
At Manchester Father Walsh discovered a
number of wounded soldiers from his Aus-
tralian parish, and great was the handshake
thereof.
“Dinkum” : Camped at the Warren, Mar-
rickville (N.S.W.), is the Queensland sec-
tion or the 3rd Field Artillery Brigade re-
inforcements. They are as fine a body of
men physically, mentally and morally as
any country could produce. Aud the finest
of them come from the plains and table-
lands of North Queensland. Anyone who
has been through that country knows these
men, but here they are brought right to the
doorstep of the bloated city sentiment and
its parrot cry about the impossibility of
developing a healthy race in Northern Aus-
tralia. Sydney should take this oppor-
tunity to see the men whose daily bread is
earned in the districts it regards as unfit
for white men. One of the Bananalanders
who provides a striking instance of how
white men cau work and thrive in the North
in Bombardier Donald Simpson, late mana-
ger of Bonnie Downs station, near Hugh-
enden. Simpson is a long, loose-limbed,
sun-hardened young Australian. A Southern
Queenslander born, he is a Northern man by
adoption, and he has worked on almost
every run of any size in the upper portion of
the State and in the allegedly unhealthy
Gulf country. This is his judgment: “I
would rather live in North Queensland than
anywhere else in the Commonwealth.”
