FIRST TRAMP (to mate who has just returned from a legging expedition): “Is that ’ere
notice ‘Beware of the dog’ a gag ?”
MATE (screwing himself round to show the tattered condition of his garments): “Does
that look like a gag!”
Private Downs, of Brisbane, who lost his
sight through a Gallipoli shell-burst, is now
taking pianos to pieces and handling felt-
hammers with deft fingers. “Getting eyes
in my fingers already,’’ he says with a smile.
Anglicans are not going to let the pulpit
become a refuge for those afflicted with cold
feet. Archbishop Donaldson has announced
that for the remainder of the war he will
not accept any candidates for sky-pilotage
certificates unless they are guaranteed unfit
for military service. All the students at his
Gaiters’ training-college except four have
enlisted, and the college is being temporarily
closed down, the lonely four being sent to
the Anglican College at Brisbane Uni. under
Principal Bakei\ Meanwhile, Canon Mick-
lem, of the training-college, goes to England
to super-vise the publication of his Biblical
commentary, recently mentioned in The
Bulletin.
"Burcoo” jut a down the great natural
resources: —•
Every known mineral and metal exists in Banana-
land with the exception of radium—and even it may
be there, according to some of the metallurgists.
The range of precious stones is remarkable, appar-
ently the emerald being the only gem missing. At
the just-closed 'Frisco Exposition Bananaland’s show-
cases of precious stones and gems threw the experts
of all nations in the world into a state of frantic
excitement. Any American who knows gives Banana-
land the cake for producing some of the finest
woods in this universe, and Uncle Sam consistently
backs his opinion by importing Bananaland fancy
woods to make his most expensive inside furnishings
with. Enormous coal areas, sometimes 100 miles
square, and practically opening up on the surface
with 20ft. to 40ft. in the face, eclipse other known
fields. In centuries to come, at the rate the older
fuel-producing centres are petering out, the major
part of the world’s coal production will be from
hereabouts. Where one mountain of gold, silver and
copper exists, as at Mount Morgan, it’s logical to
expect there are others. Big lumps of iron are to
be found in many places, in some instances with the
coal on the surface. Sugar, beef, mutton, wheat,
barley—all can be grown to perfection, and Straw-
berry breaks all records.
And yet, unless the local Government _ can
cadge a million somewhere to provide jobs,
the blessed place reckons it will have to go
on two meals a day and see its boots perish
from senile decay.
The Senate of the University has always
been nominated by the Government;' but as
the institution has now 50 graduates the
hitherto-suspended constitution for seif-gov-
ernment comes into force. The graduates
form the council which is to elect the Sen-
ate, though the Government will still be
represented by 10 nominees. The graduates
met for the first time last Tuesday to choose
Iheir warden or president, and the senators
.will be elected in June.

Passin’ It On.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
W’en Satan battling with the Lord
Gave up ther fight an’ broke ’is sword
’E didn’t whine an’ cry ‘Fergive’
An’ crave ther right ter crawl ’n’ live.
’E simply dived an’ settled down
Beyond the sight o’ Gabriel’s frown,
An’ ’stead o’ sittin’ down to dream
’E ’atched a low an’ cunnin’ scheme,
So that w’en ’ope was dead an’ gone
’E yet could pass the trouble on.
Fer since the day w’en life was born
Between the midnight an’ the morn.
W’en smiles are turned to tears an’ frown
We pass the trouble on an’ down.
W’en Adam at ’is girl’s request
Eergot the Lord an’ ’is behest
An’ et the fruit that ’Eav’n forbid
Ter find the joy ’is madam did.
’E left the Garden sad an’ meek
A tear upon ’is manly cheek,
An’ as they et the bread of toil
An’ sweated o’er the stubborn soil
They sang a song —“Our Eden’s gone
An’ we must pass ther trouble on !”
An’ just because they fell from Grace
A blight is on the Human Race.
An’ so to-day in field an’ town
We pass our troubles on an’ down.
An’ kings an’ coves of ’igh degree,
As well as chaps like you an’ me.
They do their dash an’ just the same
They ’and along ther little game ;
Old Prussian Frederick’s murd’rous way
Is Kaiser Bill’s fer sure to-day ;
Them Norsemen ’ow they drank an’ tore
An’ sailed their ships to ev’ry shore,
An’ just the same old Jellicoe
’As swep’ the seas of ev’ry foe;
W’en life is spent an’ breath is gone
Our faults an’ virtues still live on ;
No matter ’ow we rail at fate
She runs the show right up ter date.
From first to last she does us brown —-
We pass our troubles on an’ down.
We swear as once the people swore
W’en life was fresh on ev’ry shore;
We’re out ter spoil an’ rend an’ rive
As they did who were then alive,
W’en men would fight with club an’ stone
An’ brain a man to steal ’is bone;
We breathe an’ brag—we live an’ lust
Because it’s inborn in the dust,
An’ w’en our zest in life is gone
We simply pass the battlin’ on;
An’ w’en we’ve done with jest an’ joke
We ’ide our ’eads in mother’s cloak,
An’ cry, “We’re sick o’ Cross an’ Crown—
We throw them up an’ pass ’em down!”
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.

Kollo Aytoun, son of a once leading mi];
tary satrap of Queensland, who was re
ported wounded and missing since Mons L
now' known to have died while a prisoner of
war in Germany. The news comes from
French nurse who only recently escaped

On Thursday night the committee of the
Queensland Soldiers’ Comforts Fund took
advantage of the moonlight and pay-day at
the camp to hold a corroboree on the river
As a result over £lOO was gathered in fr um
the sale of tickets, and another £l5 f rom
the sale of sweets. So Tommy Banana-

Mrs. Campbell Riley and a hard-working
committee have started a Baby's Kit
Society. The affair got into working trim
only last Monday, but already 40 members
a fair stock of material and some cash have
been accumulated. Mrs. Pring Roberts is
chief cutter, Miss North is sec. and Miss
Bruce Nicol holds the funds.
Marjorie Brown, a Toowoomba girl, has
taken out a license to drive a taxi and has
already begun business. Taxi ladies will at
least have the advantage that no male cargo
will dare to argue over the fare.
The women’s Peace Army is making stren-
uous attempts to suppress the Australian-
produced Nurse Cavell picture, on the
grounds that it will stir up bitterness of
feeling. How these peace societies do love
to get into a scrap.
A new' musical society, the Brisbane
Operatic Players, is at present hard at work
rehearsing “Tambour Major,” under the
baton of Leonard Francis. It is to be pro-
duced in the winter.
The panto. “Dick Whittington” is with
us. A packed house each night learns that
its worst fears as regards the bare-legged
ballet are doomed to disappointment. Never-
theless, the booking continues good.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Some men can't live without a grievance.
The sanitary inspector of the Fitzroy Shire
lately complained that three Chinese had
dug out the remains of a defunct fellow-
countryman and removed them as passen-
gers’ luggage to Rockhampton. • The sani-
tary man’s grievance was that they had left
him the grave to fill up. Apparently he
expected them to cart away the grave as well
as its lodger.
“D.F.” : Officialdom favored the idea of a
parti-colored Australia almost as far back as
the first page of the history of this con-
tinent. In a letter to Lord Ilobart, dated
August 14, ISO 4, Governor King wrote: —
Respecting the first of those objects (i.e., the inter-
course with China) it would be attended with the
most desirable consequences in introducing Chinese
into these settlements, which from the knowledge
your Lordship has of the industrious character of that
people, and how much the Dutch settlements in
India have profited by their residence among them, I
presume might be attended with great advantage to
this country, were it only for the certainty of their
applying themselves to raising cotton for the China
market, which may be done a very few degrees to
the northward of this place.
“Itan” : The Crown law authorities hav-
ing had their attention called to the scan-
dalous stuffing of the Water Board roll
which has been going on for years unchecked,
find that the Government is powerless to
move except by an amendment of the Act.
On* the new roll there are no less
than 700 cases of duplicate enrolment, and
in one instance IS voters are enrolled on the
one qualification. The flaw in the Act
makes the compiler of the roll powerless
to the number of names which can be en-
rolled for any unregistered association. For
instance, every member of the Show Asso-
ciation, probably anything up to 1000, could
have been legally enrolled, and the Labor
crowd could also have enrolled every trades
unionist in Brisbane as partners in the
Trades Hall. It is all the more smellful
when it is realised that upon the roll thiS
year’s elections must be conducted. When
Parliament meets next they will make short
work of the scandal, but in the meantime
Labor is at a disadvantage in fighting the
four seats.

celebrated what she called her 100th birth-
day early in January, but, as is usual at

was missing. What is certain is that she
was living iu the Bay of Islands with hei
husband when Heke took to the warpath ni
’45. Then she shifted to Auckland, ana
when war was over settled in Waiuku.
Even if the lady is not a centenarian she
has had some real adventures, and has one oi
two good yarns. One of them : In those ver)
early days her mother-in-law had only on
needle and one thimble. She also possesse
one turkey. While sewing in the garden s e
dropped the thimble, and the bird swallowe
it. The nearest shop that stocked thimhi
was at Sydney (N.S.W.), but to kill trw
turkey that was destined to be married an
become the mother of generations of turn
was unthinkable. So an operation was
cided upon, the turkey’s crop was careru .
opened, the thimble retrieved, and the wou
sewn up with the lonely -

A Wasted Night.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The spider moon across the sky
Had spun her cobwebs thin and fine
To catch my light-winged fancies shy
And smear them over with her shine;
The world was hushed, the night was gay,
A young breeze tossed the drowsy grass
That made complaining of his play,
Then turned and slept and let him pass.
The moon, the night, the wind, the sea,
The laughter of a careless throat
O’er shining waters borne to me
On all my waiting senses smote.
It was a yellow night for love,
A night to make all kisses fair.
With nose pressed close to nose above
The simple rapture two may share.
Oh. take the dull brown nights for beer
When stars are hid and waves are loud.
Then o’er your pewter you may hear
The mingled voices of the crowd.
But in the smooth, sweet time of spring.
When warmer dreams get in their work,
Let young romance have room to sing
And keep as sober as a Turk.
These things the spider moon approves,
The while on high she waits to snare
Each little mothlike dream that flits
In cobwebs bright as angels’ hair.
She lightly gilds her honey nights
And calls aloud that all may hear,
And young love, scorning her delights,
Of choice selects that hour for beer.
A night of nights was lost to bliss.
I waited long. He did not come.
Upon my lips the unspent kiss
Withered in silence and w T as dumb.
But well I know that if the rain
Had poured in torrents from the sky,
He had been making efforts vain
With one wet coat to keep two dry.
Ah. well-a-day! I cannot fan
My righteous anger hot too long.
These things but teach my life to scan
Like some well-labored stave of song.
For George is useful when we meet,
Ilis sins are never past repair,
The while he buys me things to eat
And on occasion pays my fare.
n.s.w.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTIL.
A Helping Hand for Party Government.
Dear Bulletin, —While a couple of excited
politicians were throwing verbal bricks at
each other lately one of them, who
happens to be N. S. AVales Attorney-Gen-
eral, complained that the other fellow had
not. when the cow cocky “was denounced”
as a child sweater, joined in the chorus.
Which suggests that the Government ought
to take drastic action. How about this:—
A BILL
To provide for the better government of the Ma
State in respect to affairs not covered by legislation ;
to extend the powers and functions of citizens, magis-
trates, police and members of Parliament; to estab-
lish a good defence against prosecution for riotous
language ; to give all dodgers, wirepullers and politi-
cal no-goods a fair go, and for purposes consequent
thereon and incidental thereto.
Be it enacted by the King’s Most Excellent
Majesty by and with the advice and consent of the
Legislative Council and Pledgislative Assembly of
N.S.W. in Parliament assembled and by the authority
of the same as follows:—
Division 1—Short Title.
1. This Act may be cited as the “Denouncing Act
1916.”
2. This Act shall, except as herein otherwise pro-
vided, commence and come into operation on the
first day of April one thousand, nine hundred and
sixteen.
3. This Act shall be divided into three part •: —
Part I.—Preliminary.
Part II. —Interpretation.
Part III. — Penalties.
and as many more old parts as may be added from
time to time by a Council of Jay Pees to be here-
inafter appointed.
Division 2 — Interpretation.
In this Act, unless inconsistent with the subject or
matter,
“Denounce” shall mean to curse in any language
of Europe or Asia, dead or alive, to stigmatise, decry,
libel, castigate with words, execrate, anathematise,
menace, abuse, bully, bluster, talk big, speak as a
German.
“Person” shall mean any individual, wowser,
woman, alderman or police constable.
“Thing” shall mean whatever is not a person or a
place.
“Place” shall mean whatever not being a thing
or a person as defined by 7654 decisions of his
Majesty’s courts, Judges, magistrates, Privy Coun-
cils, the Sunday Trading Act of Charles II. and
amendments thereof, but excluding every public high-
way, cart, box, gate, back fence, domesticated
animal, newspaper, pulpit, platform, chair, or any-
where or nowhere authorised not to be a place under
the aforesaid decisions.
Division 3 —Penalties.
Every person who after the passing of this Act
shall fail to denounce when his or her party leader
denounces shall be liable to a fine of £100, that is
to say, omits to denounce
Fat, bloat, Freetrade, Protection, the Liberal
party, the Labor party, the Red Feds, voluntary ser-
vice, conscription, loans, taxation, economy, waste,
amendment of the Constitution, saving, spending,
Ikey Mo, the climate, the States, the Commonwealth,
P. Enterprise, booze, the water-waggon, the railway
timetable, Cocky, motor-cars, the Censor, the neigh-
hoi's or any other item.
Every person who after a first offence shall be
guilty of again failing to denounce shall be adjudged
to have committed a breach of the War Precautions
Act, and be interned as a person assisting to em-
barrass Party Government.
A measure of this sort is urgently required,
anyhow, to give Party Government a longer
lease of life. At present its scope is too
limited. Under the Bill I have outlined it
could rage about 75 things all at ones, none
of which matter, and then knock off to
bellow over 76 more until somebody started
to yelp at something else, in the "midst of
everything that does matter could be for-
gotten except, of course, the pawnshop.—
Ycurs, etc.,
WILLIAM STRAIGHTJACKET.

The Wine Cup.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
GREAT Cleopatra, who was skilled in
love,
Towards Antony leant smiling at the
feast.
And, ere the leaping of his pulse's ceased,
Lifted her lovely wanton hand above
The cup and flung a pearl into the lucent
wine.
IS.nee she within the niche of Time was set
Is nojw how many hundred years ago?
I am no royal queen with pride aglow,
I have ho gems to east away—and yet
This tale of Cleopatra is but mine!'
For when your arms about me make a cup
Filler} with love’s vintage to the curved
rim,
In quick delight, too keen for careless
whim,
I feel the heart within me lifted up
For one sweet instant out of time and space.
And then into the bowl my heart is flung,
Swims and is changed to liquid raptu-
rously,
r Melts- in the wine that men call ecstasy,
Turns nebulous, is no more old or young,
But calm as moonlight in a windless place.
N.S.W.
NINA MCRDDOH.
Filling Up the Census Card.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
fTV IE - night is still young as father,
” clad in patriotic pyjamas, shuffles
through the door en route for the
bath. He is aweary and his corns call out
for soothing. Circumstance has enmeshed
him in the scorching labor of mending the
King’s highways. Uncongenial this, and
since bedaubing the pavement with the first
dollop of hot tar this morning a vision of
the cooling waters of the bath has hung,
mirage-like, ’twixt him and the shovel.
• Father was a blacksmith once, long since,
and when he ceased to use liis muscles and
sinews amidships, that portion, straining
now in the embrace of the Belgian stripes,
diverged far from the line of grace.
We, his family, are gathered in the dining-
room as he passes through. Amid a welter
of census cards we are proving by incredible
blundering how unequal is the contest be-
tween ourselves and the Government Stati-
stician ; for most of us are in the mellow
stage of life, and our green years were spent
in study of the Cow instead of the Book.
“Chaps in the office in town are wading
through ’em up to their armpits,” uncle is
saying as dad comes through. lie alludes
to Census returns. “Done yours yet,
Henry?” he asks.
“No !”| answers father. “Time enough—
got to the fifteenth.”
“Well, it’s the fourteenth now,” rejoins
uncle, always -glad to confound anyone.
“What date d’ you think it w r as?”
“Fourteenth!” ejaculates father. Then
in amazed unbelief: “Why, it wants a week
of it yet! Look y’ere? Toosday, nine,
ain’t it?” He waves the newspaper.
“Paper’s a week old,” answers uncle suc-
cinctly.
We would all like to laugh, but dare not.
“Never laugh at father’s mistake” is a
home-made axiom we learned at mother’s
knee. But uncle—who can escape at -will
—cares not. Says he: “You’ve been going
about with your eyes shut, old man. If I
hadn’t looked in to-night you'd have failed
in your duty and would have made yourself
liable—in law—to a penalty.” Uncle is
addicted to the remark legal.
No one heeds him save grandma, who
may be said to be age-proof against any
bore. Father, though, is a high explosive.
Impact with uncle causes an explosion at
any time.
He hurls the old newspaper into the grate.
“Well, I’m damned !” lie roars. “Never was
such a place as this ’cummalating old noos-
papers an’ to-day’s always loaned out to
somebody.” This at Mrs. Malone, in to
p borrow the scissors. He savagely forces his
’striped bulk into a chair. Mother, who has
been in training so long that she knows his
thoughts before he can think them, planks
before him two Census Cards. “I told you
this Jiad to be done a week ago—the day
you said / "had all your money.”
“An’ who a better right?” pines grandma,
her cap a-quiver at the recollection of yes-
terweek’s wrangle. “Is he wantin’ to give
it to some other person, M’ria?”
Father ignores the slur on his morality.
Grandma is a gnat, nipping but harmless.
But this Census Paper—now. when he had
planned surcease from pain for his corns!
Its perusal, added to uncle’s goadings, is the
last touch to his tribulation.
He strikes a clerkly attitude, or what
passes for such with us—shoulders hunched,
elbows out, jaw working. With fingers
stiffened by tar and shovelling he records
his name “Ilenery” (he has always spelled
it thus), glares angrily down the list of
inquiries, and acknowledges himself beaten
at the start. As a family we are not up
to normal as readers or politicians; there-
fore our idea of the Census and its object
is chaotic. But uncle is present.
“About the valuation now, Henry? What
d’you value this place at?” he asks."
“What do vou value it at, mother?” says
father promptly.
“I dunno,” she answers, trying to look in-
telligent.
(ir ;‘V ut a small price,” says Mrs. Malone.
“Thing is to make yourself dog poor in a
case like this. 'Less you own, less you pays.
See. Eh?” '* ■
Together our parents make an estimate.
We stand frankly declared paupers. But
uncle snorts scorn of this theory. “That’s
where you're wrong. You don’t read between
the I think (mind you I’m only sur-
misin ; I’m not in the Government’s.; confi-
dence), in case o’ propputy bein’ comman-
deered by th’ authorities fer th’ war fund —
I think it’s best to put a ’igh price on it.
Make it ’igli, ’igh. . . . Put it low if ye like.”
lie adds, surlily, “an’ let th’ authorities buy
from you at yer own price!”
Consternation ! Father starts to erase.
“What you got? £3OO. Not enough, not
enough! I should make it the five. Or,
better still, a thou. •Yes.!' put th’ house
down at a thou.”
“Oh, dammit, dammit, I carn’t alter it
a gen. I’ve made the two into a three, an’
if I alter, it agen the flamin’ thing’ll be in
a hole. There !. I knoo it would. Give me
a noo card, M’ria, an’ let me put down five
hundred”
“You offered five hundred for this place,
Af) r - a ’-2 a 1 no * letting Henry take it,
M na.” pipes gran. “You’ll never see such
a chance again. He won’t refuse it with
my consent.” She looks vindictively at the
penman. That weather-vane, the beaded
flower in her cap, begins to tremble, sure
sign of approaching tear-floods. “Not with
my consent, ’ she repeats impressively.
Father makes impatient sounds. It is no
matter. Grandma’s consent is not currency,
anyhow we do without it- quite commonly.
‘Wn’ this ’ere about dependents now?
Ow many ’ave I, ’sides you an’ the young-
sters? What about th’ ole lady?” He jerks
bjS ( thumb towards gran. “I keep ’er, don’t
Here grandma has a cloud-burst of thin
pbl tears, just as her cap had foretold.
‘ Here ’m I,” moans she, “seventy-seven
years old, and dependent. I got nothing,
no _ income, no stocking. This”—she
indicates a lean pocket—“is fer me
noo teeth. No property, nothin’ ’tall, an’
no ome but what’s begrudged me !”
Much time is lost in restoring gran’s self-
respect, and in dad’s refutation of the charge
ot brutality.
“Oh, or-right. or-right!” dad says. “It’s
always me! Sometimes I feel like —like
enlistin’, an’ what’d you do then?”
‘I think I could bear it,” answers mother
acidly,
“Ob, fer Gorsake lemme do this infernal
thing. How about occypation of employer?”
he appeals. “Now, ’ere’s me workin’ fer
the Council.”
<( ‘ good, steady job.” comments grandma.
‘I don’t see how you’d better it by taking
the shilling.”
Father grinds his teeth and takes strong
hold of his patience. “Do I put ’em down
singly an’ be name, or do I put ’em”-
“Collectively, Henry, collectively,” advises
uncle.
Father records it, dimly conscious the
while of an altercation between mother and
the younger set anent an unlatched gate
and the consequent fowl among the onions.
. Confused, worried with the urgency of
his task, writing, reading, tearing and
swearing, his mind seems incapable of direct-
ing his pen, as, when he would write
Henery” the word “fifty” appears as by
the black art. The pen, too, something of
a buck jumper, has a secret spite against
him, scattering blots on cards and company
alike. Tearing and casting forth, he is now
ankle deep in “throw-outs.” , „Census enve-
lopes curling in the heat of the fire
lick his bare insteps stealthily. He wipes
sweat from his face, and then, after havmg-
submitted the card to uncle’s inspection
tears it thrice, pitches it to the floor and
yells for more. See him then grovelling for
the remnants for copying purposes.
“Here is another aspect of war— what
alternative occupation could you undertake,
llenry, should occasion arise?” uncle asks'
•Blacksmithing? Horseshoeing for the
troops ?”
“No, no! I couldn’t do smithin’ agen.”
says father determinedly. "’Count <>’ me
corns, an’ me eyes—subjec’ to blight. ’Sides
which. I m hopin’ ter snap that bowlin’-
green job. It’s mine be rights o’ seniority
o service, an’ it was as good as in me anil
last year only fer that bit of a row with
Councillor Bills over the poundin’ of his
Ow was / to know th’ bally cows was
Dills s. It didn’t carry no card, nor yet
wear Bills’s fortygraph tied to its tail.”
'Course they said it was bad language lost
me th’ job, but I know ’twas th’ cow.”
Uncle considers this, but grandma, wak-
ing from a nap, is a positivist. “Civility
corsts nothing,” she mumbles. “That’s what
I’ve preached to my family. But some’ow
’Enery always knoo better.”
This is true. Humility was never father’s
weakness nor yet his strength. The sharp
answer which stirreth up wrath was ever
his. He states his general unfitness for any
service save that of nurse to a bowling green,
and uncle, the goad, beats him back to Coun-
ci 11 or Bills : *'
.“But how’s Bills got all the say in the
job? He doesn’t own the Gardens, does
he?”
“No, but Goramighty, he thinks he does !
Y’ see his first wife ’ad money, an’ she pre-
sented a pagoda t’ th’ Council —had it built
in the Gardens at Bills’s expense, an’ this
pagoda”
“Perogola,” corrects mother.
“That’s wot I said, perrygolia.”
“Pelargoniums!” interrupts grandma
shrilly. “Well, that’s not much to give fer
charity. Pelargoniums grew like weeds up
’ome. You’d ha’ thought as she ’ad money
she’d ha’ made it roses, or camellias, or
orkids.”
Father’s old slippers rustle the throw-outs
in exasperation. . Nothing but the urgency
of his task keeps him in the vicinity of his
parent.
“What’s this now about firearms? What
can they want to know?”
“Fire-irons, ’Enery. Three sets —all solid
steel,” adds the old lady explanatorily. “I
had ’em when I first married yer father.”
The general oninion (unspoken) is that a
gag would meet grandma’s case, and
father, writing his answer, “No. I ain’t
got no gun,” adds gloomily. “Often, wish I
did ’ave.”
“And is the pergola still there ?” we ask.
“Yes! She’s there all right,” answers the
penman. “Y’ can sit under it any time, or
y’ could if th’ Council ’ud let yous. Y’ see,
they walled it in with a ’edge an’ attached it
t’ th’ bowling green, an’ though th’ first
Mrs. Bills meant it fer th’ public, only the
Councillors’ donahs can rest theirselves in
it without a ticket now —y’ can see ’em
there any ’ot afternoon —avin’ tea—lookin’
at their ’usbans playin’ bowls. An’ it’s
cause o’ this Bills ’ave all th’ say in th’ job.
It’s mine, be rights. But Vs got me
snouted. .There was that time wen a man
was wanted tempory at the green, an’ Bills
come ’urryin’ down to the ’all. I was there,
working. ’ls eye lit on me, an’ e’ was just
about givin’ me th’ office, wen I, being”
“Drunk,” puts in mother, with a sigh and
a shake of the head.
“Yes.” admits the narrator, in a genial
glow of recollection, “I was lathered, an’
pretty mellow too; an’, just as ’e got near,
I mislaid a dollop o’ ’ot tar, an’ it caught
Bills fair in th’ middle. x\n’ Bills, mind
you, a tidyisli man. Course ’e wouldn’t
believe it was a’ accident. But I’m pullin’
is leg now. Doin’ a bit of Sir-ing an’ keepin’
me mouth shut, cause 1 want that job. I
know ’orses, an’ t’ see th’ chaps there, drivin’
th’ roller crooked over th’ lawns —sawin’ at
the old mare’s mouth —everythin’ out o’
plumb—struth ! An’ there’* me standin’
round with ’ot tar —I’d like th’ job, potterin’
about th’ grass. Sawft, ye see, fer me corns,
an’ green fer me sore eyes.”
Before the pathetic beauty of this idyll we
sit in silence, broken presently by the en-
trance of Henry, junior.
"A hat you stewin’ over-there, Dad?” he
asks, with the easy familiarity so beautiful
to see in our family.
“Census.” growls the scribe. “On th’ last
lap, thank God! You go out an’ post it.
Dyer hear? Fourteenth t'niorrer, -returns
gutter be in.”
A hat! Didn’t you know ? Time’s ex-
tended.” says Junior. “You got till The

The room is empty when the first violence

WEEROONA.
The Beginning of the Blowfly,
A TALE FROM GENESIS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was fifteen (15) minutes after the
Creation, which space of time is a
quarter of an hour.
Adam was scuffling around with a note-
book and a pencil taking down the names
of the animals.
lie took down the labyrinthodon paeliy-
nathus first, because that beast had an
appointment with a girl at the Tivoli and
wanted to get away.
All this was fifteen (lp) minutes after
the Creation, and the place was the Garden
of Eden, in Mesopotamia, where the Turks
are now (may ill-luck befall thefn!).
And when Adam had finished taking down
the name and visiting card of the laby-
rinthodon pachynathus he began to register
the other animals.
He made a note of the ichthyosaurus, and
the plesiosaurus, and the whale, and the
crocodile, and the sea-serpent, and the fox-
olofoden. and the eohippus, and the mam-
moth. and the dog that was ten feet high,
and the conger-eel, and the prawn, and the
elephant, and the kraken, and the woman,
and the cheese-mite
At least lie was just going to write down
the cheese-mite when something bit him on
the back.
In those days Adam wore no clothes, for
it was only fifteen (15) minutes after the
Creation.
And Adam, being so busy taking-down the
names of the animals in-his notebook, hadn't
been able to go to his tailor, or his hatter,
or his bootmaker, orchis haberdasher, or to
tin public-house where he bought his beer
by the keg. And he hadn’t even opened an
account with his fishmonger.
In fact, everything was just climbing out
of chaos, and paying 2d. in the £.
Adam, being bitten between the shoulders
as already mentioned, leaped three feet
(3ft.) in the air in Mesopotamia, beside the
Euphrates, fifteen (15) minutes after the
Creation, which was the first jump since the
world began, and lie shouted in Hebrew,
which was the first shout on record (though
many people have shouted three penn’orth
of beer since) : ‘‘Blow that fly.”
And the animal lias been called a blown,'
ever since. ,
Eve was also naked, though she hart
cabled to Baris for a new hat.
Eve lias been naked ever since, because
the Bank of Jerusalem refused to pay 11,11
cheque for the hat. , ,
Adam lias jumped ever since on geneia
principles, in Mesopotamia, beside tn
Euphrates. . .<■ b
And the reason of all these tilings is P f
comprehension.

The Inky Way.
THE HERMIT.
[Fon The Bulletin.]
The house is lonely, and dull and drear, and filled
with a deadly gloom:
For all that is cheerful and bright and sweet it
would rival an ancient tomb.
In fact, I find that at night the ghosts of the whole
of the district’s dead
Arise and skirmish around and raise the hair on my
shrinking head ;
For whether you hold to agnostic views, or favor a
future state,
There are times at night in a lonely house when
you strangely miss a mate.
But though there is little to soothe by night, or
to charm the soul by day,
Just think of the things I have written about since
the wife and kids went away!
I have thrown my boots at the pirate fowls, the cups
at the sneaking cat
That thinks far more of a stolen chop than of cap-
turing mouse or rat;
And the half-grown chickens my wife adores—for
vice of a varied kind
The paragon pests that a harried man in the whole
wide world could find ;
They’re up on the table, or pecking the bread, or
hooking the butter or jam,
With an eye for the missiles I vainly send with
many a fervent damn.
What with poultry and pussy and general woe my
life is a sombre grey—•
But think of the things I have written about since
the wife and kids went away!
For whether it be that the brain works best when
the body feels down and out,
And genius fed on attractive fare is subject to men-
tal gout,
Or that dirt, discomfort and wrath give spur to the
powers of a jaded mind—
Whatever the cause of the wondrous thing, ’tis cer-
tain (at least I find)
That never in happier, full-fed days had I easier
work to hatch
Fine Jhoughts and phrases and splendid words than
now when I’m doomed to batch ;
And with pride that thrills, though I know the price
is a fearsome one to pay,
I think of the things I have written about since the
wife and kids went away.
N.s.w. QUIDAM.
* » * *
London Daily Sketch is shouting
hosannas over the capture of Will Dyson as
its cartoonist. So the Australian now has
an audience of a million and goes on the
stage simultaneously in London and Man-
chester.
* • * *
There is to be placed a mural tablet to
the memory of Corporal (Rev.) R. H. Pit-
tendrigh in the Methodist Church Hall, Neu-
tral Bay, Sydney. “Bob” Pittendrigh trod
the Inky Way before he became a preacher,
lie served his apprenticeship as a comp, on
the Molong Argus with R. J. Baker, now of
Gosford Times, and with J. C. L. Fitzpat-
rick, M.L.A., who bought from Baker and
sold to the present proprietors. Corporal
Pittendrigh proved that he could use the
pen as well as the “stick,” but this talent
was not known until he got to the Front.
The tablet is not exclusively a Church affair
and friends wlm knew Bob in other trades
may cut in.
* * *
“Harrison O.”: My opinion that the
U.S.A. has, apart from Henry James, failed
to produce a really great novelist was worth
expressing if only for the unconsciously
humorous rejoinder it has called forth from
one “O’Phimerty” (B. 3/2/T6). “Has
‘Harrison O.’ never heard of George. W.
Cable, Joel Chandler Harris, Hopkinson
Smith and Edith Wharton?” asks this critic.
Not only have I heard of them, but being
acquainted with -the works of each I feel
impelled to remark that the man who can
see greatness therein is suffering from
arrested literary development. Cable, knows
the Southern States and the indigenous
nigger, and writes cleverly of them ; Harris s
nigger folk yarns were entertainingly done;
Edith Wharton affects the paraphernalia of
a literary Dreadnought, but her earnest,
somewhat stilted stories certainly cannot be
regarded as great; while Hopkinson Smith
is a Best Seller, or thereabouts, and would
doubtless rather remain so than become a
mere genius. In my turn I would ask
“O’Phimerty” if he has ever heard of Ana-
tole France, Gustave Flaubert, George Mere-
dith and Thomas Hardy? Let him compare
his quartette with mine and he may appre-
ciate the difference between literary great-
ness and talented mediocrity.
* * * *
How many ’farers along the world’s inky-
way have laid down the pen for the sword?
The organ of the English Institute of Jour-
nalists publishes a roll of honor showing
that 1468 working pressmen are on
service, including 65 from oversea; 55 have
been killed, 71 wounded, 11 reported miss-
ing, and 10 have been decorated for gallan-
try. Also, many women journalists are
serving as nurses.
* * * *
The well-informed Dublin Freeman’s
gives the late Joe Winter, of Melb. Advo-
cate a testimonial that may keep him in
purgatory longer than he deserves. He was,
it explains, editor of the Argus, and through
the medium of his paper collected ±45,000
for the Irish party”—which looks like good
defamatory libel on both Joe and granny.
* * * *
According to the Argus: “Barrier miners
boast that Broken Hill has sent 2500 men
to the Front. That is very creditable and
honorable —to the 2500 men. It is no proof
of anybody else’s patriotism. It is a curious
process of reasoning that which enables one
to assume credit for good deeds achieved by
others.” Barrier miners might remind the
Argus of this bit of cold logic when next
that paper is spluttering with enthusiasm
over the honor and glory shed upon the
aristocratic gloss of Britain by the appeai-
ance of a certain proportion of “titled
youths” in the firing-line.

Backache! Why?
Do you rise in the morning with a groan? Do you feel that you are
getting old? Having made up your mind that you are a martyr, do you
vent your ill-humor on the family? After a while, the pain and stiffness
leave you, and you forget your troubles until the next morning, when
the same dull pain in the small of your back, the difficulty of bracing
your nerves, brings back all the dire forebodings of the day before.
This is the way Kidney Trouble creeps on its victim, and you are
starting on a long journey towards ill-health and infirmity. The
Kidneys are overtaxed with the work of cleansing out the impurities
from your blood. Each day there is an accumulation of waste which
must upset the body. It forms Uric Acid, and Uric Acid is the most
insidious form of poisoning that can take place in your body. The
number of ills to which it leads is almost limitless, and though middle
age is the most favorable season for their activity, even the yo.ungest
child often suffers, and sometimes dies from the different manifestations
of Kidney Trouble.
A change of diet is an advisable precaution, but a reliable, specially-
prepared remedy for Kidney Trouble, .such as Dr. Sheldon’s Gin Pills, is
the safeguard for your health. Dr. Sheldon’s Gin Pills stimulate your
sluggish kidneys to reaction, and assist the work of removing impurities
from your blood, which the kidneys have been unable to accomplish
unassisted. Then their tonic action so revives the kidneys that are
filtering the blood that the work is done automatically as by a perfect
machine. Your back straightens up, the pulling, dragging feeling
gradually leaves you ; in the morning you can leap from bed, feeling a
glow of health and vigor, and meet life with a smiling face. It is the
duty of every man, not only to himself, but to the world at large, to keep
his health in the proper condition for his daily task ; and for the victim
of Kidney Trouble Dr. Sheldon’s Gin Pills give just the little boost
his organs need to make them his slave, instead of his being a slave to
ill-health.
Gin Pills Will Help You.
If you suffer with Pains in your Back or Sides, or have any signs
of Kidney Trouble or Rheumatism, such as puffy swellings under the
eyes or in the feet or ankles ; if you are Nervous, Tired and Run Down,
or bothered with Urinary Disorders, Dr. Sheldon’s Gin Pills for Backache
and Kidney Trouble are the Remedy which will quickly relieve you of
your misery. You can secure an original bottle of Dr. Sheldon’s Gin
Pills for Backache and Kidney Troubles from all Chemists and Store-
keepers for 1/6; or, in larger bottles, holding more than twice the
quantity, for 2/6 ; or, if not obtainable locally, will be sent post free on
receipt of price in postal notes or stamps by the

The Man with the Silver Head;
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
-CXTALTER ROSSITTER and his friend
VV Judson rode at the long, easy can-
’ T ter which saves the horse and eats
the miles. They were out of Walter Rossi t-
ter’s towri; his country of Mt. Remarkable
is 400 miles or so south-west. Rossitter
looked with interest at the new world of
great cave-pierced limestone rocks, blackened
by exposure and weathered into turrets and
pinnacles. It was newer to Judson; the
red earth, the black limestone —high and of
interminable length—the great bottle-green
figtrees, each with its colony of cooing
pigeons and each marking the entrance to a
cave. Down that cave felt and faltered the
hundreds of feet of the fig-roots, dropping
a sentient feeler over every shelf and stalac-
tite and searching for water in the dark.
The sunlight lay upon the maze of lime-
stone hills; heavy yellow in the half-
shadows and white as arclights on the
summits. The day was blue, gold and mag-
nolia-green. Along the red track between
the hills ot lime a big chestnut cantered
lightly to the weight of a small blackboy,
his only garment a man’s waistcoat, worn
upside down and buttoned up the back. The
small boy’s legs were through the armholes
and the waistcoat covered all of him but
essentials.
“Who’s the kid, Wally?” asked Judson, as
they cantered lightly along the red track to
Girofla.
“I don’t know his real name. lie belongs
to Manson, and Manson calls him Cock-
roach.”
“And who’s Manson?”
“A fine chap—we’ll meet him at Station
Creek, most likely. lie owns a lot of coun-
try round here and a station on the Einas-
leigh, too.”
They cantered into Station Creek at near
enough to lunch-time, and Manson joined
them before the tea had stewed to undrink-
ableness. He was a tall, spare, fair-bearded
man, apparently beloved of the blackboy,
whose lustrous eyes sought Manson’s face
with all the unquestioning fidelity of a dog.
Manson spoke little. Also, although the
day was hot and they lunched in the shade
of a great rock, so that Rossitter and Jud-
son bad doffed their hats, Manson sat with
his hat on and seemed shy in the presence
of the stranger, Judson. After lunch he
said he had business at the homestead a
mile or two away, and that his boy Cock-
roach would go on with Rossitter and Jud-
son to Girofla. It was a long speech for
him, and. immediately after delivering it, he
sent Cockroach to resacldle his horse. When
it was led up to him he threw a very long
leg over the saddle and rode away as if
horse and man were Centaur.
“Good cut of a man that, Wally!” said
Judson. “He’ll never be prosecuted for talk-
ing out of his turn. But does he live in that
hat? It seemed as much a part of him as
he is of that big grey gelding.”
“lie doesn’t take that hat off because he’s
sensitive about the injury to his head. He’s
got a silver plate in it as big as the palm of
my hand.”
“What’s the yarn?”
“He told me ; but we’re old friends. He’ll
never tell you, Juddy ; so I will. It’s only
about Manson and four hundred head of
cattle , and Arunga—a black he had as
drover’s boy. Arunga was Cockroach’s
father.”
“Was?”
‘‘Yes— he’s outski now.”
-Ynd then Walter Rossitter told Judson
me story of Manson, the Man with the
mlver Head, and of Arunga, the father of
ockroach, who wore only a man’s waist-
coat, upside down and buttoned up the back.
Manson, with 400 mixed cattle from his
.le station on the Einasleigh, fared slowly,
1? the heavy rains of the monsoon, through
if glue of the black soil between Quartz
■U and Fossilbrook. The rain fell for ever
na ever; patient, persistent, inevitable;
)e , eB.ttle, slowed down by good condition,
by the track and the moist
?t’ floundered through the black soil at a
rb 11 °t a mile an hour or thereabout. Man-
v “ rode sitting down in the saddle, as if the
f avy F ai r> had sunk him. Rain dripped
om his hat-brim, from his glistening oil-
in' in fl coat ’ Horn his well-washed boots, from
W tla jFV'. a, the reins, and from every
Wofi 0t J l 's.Pointed beard. Arunga, a sullen
cack of middle age, rode on the right wing
t ‘, morose man, hating the madness of

i, ‘• '. ,t one of them heeled the pack-
s in Play- At four o’clock in the after-
noon-—which might have been eleven in the
morning, or six in the evening, for any nota-
ble alteration in temperature or outlook —
Manson, with a wave of the hand, motioned
Arunga to steady the mob on a long, grassy
flat by a sandy creek fringed with pandanus,
and bearing in its channel two feet of sap-
green water. lie dismounted, caught tlie
pack-horses, rigged the tent-fly, and made
camp.
They had finished eating before the swift
night closed on them ; Arunga sitting at the
other extreme of the tent-fly, the ten feet of
distance representing the difference of caste.
A blue dog lay near Manson, gnawing at a
beef sinew as tough as hide; and a black
dog stood in the rain, his mouth leaking for
scraps; his tail describing its movements of
ingratiation and his anticipatory eyes
hungry for provender and recognition.
Manson lighted his pipe, and Arunga fol-
lowed the lead ; and Manson instructed him
with native brevity in the work of the night.
Arunga had never before been on the track
with cattle ; and his hatred of the new and
uncomfortable conditions of work in the rain
and the darkness increased his moroseness.
He dangerously approached insolence —more
of manner than of word.
“Arunga,” said Manson, “you watch good-
feller first time. Byem-bye I been wake;
then you been sleep. Rain stop little feller
time. S’pose big feller thunder, lightning
he come, altogether cattle been frightened.
You gettem alonga horse, been ridem round
that one ; watchem big roan piker; watchem
black bullock; watchem big feller straw-
berry—then him been all right.”
“1 been wantem ’trorberry jam,” said
Arunga, sullenly, his greedy eyes turned to
the paclc-bag.
“S’pose good feller, you gettem t’-mor-
row,” replied Manson so definitely that he
closed the argument. “Now! You gettem
horse —ride round that one, s’posbn he been
frightened.”
The sulky Arunga withdrew from the
little fire he had made to squat over.. It
sputtered no longer, for the rain had mirac-
ulously ceased, and a heavy, thick, moist and
breathless air replaced it, sinking on the
men like the tangible darkness of a tomb,
oppressing their lungs, so that they breathed
like men in the stress of wrestling or fight.
The air grew thicker, and painful as its
electrical saturations proceeded. A sinister
intelligence seemed to dominate the. dark-
ness ; the cattle moved uneasily, as if they
felt the presence of death among them —the
suggestion of spilled blood, full of terror to
these grass-eaters of the plains. Habit
made these phenomena familiar to Manson.
Lving comfortably on his nap under the
tent-fly, he put his pipe away and slept like
a tired child.
The gloomy Arunga, with the dogs follow-
ing—brave and willing as the man was not,
for training had made them single-hearted
to duty—-rode around the cattle. They moved
without aim or direction, uneasy in. the
electric air, smelling the thunderbolts in it
and feeling that for them it was charged
with doom. Arunga tried to forget his
fears of the darkness —not the fear of a
coward, but the transmitted horror of the
unseen, brought to him through a hundred
generations who had shivered at their devils
of the darkness and strengthened by super-
stition, for ever expanding and concrescent.
A sigh came over the plain. It was but a
variation of temperature; but Arunga
thought it the breathing of a devil, and
shivered. The air trembled as if itself were
afraid. Arunga’s heart came to his throat,
and his throat rose into his mouth; every
hair of him stood up in fear: his flesh trem-
bled in the cold sweat that broke upon his
skin. The fear of man and cattle extended
itself to the horse —he, too, trembled as he
padded around the unquiet herd. Only the
dogs—one ahead and one following—went to
duty steadfastly, although the younger dog
once whined his disbelief in the wholesome-
ness of things.
A great black shape suddenly lifted itself
at the offside of Arunga and the horse put
both forefeet rigidly before him, and sat
back upon his quarters, snorting his fear.
The black shape swerved away from them,
in a fear greater than that of the horse
Other shapes arose and began to call to each
other —not the contented cry of cattle in
safe pasture, but muttering bellow r s to tell
each other of danger and the menace of the
night. It completed the fears of Arunga.
He rode around the mob, looking behind
him for terror and ahead for devils; trying
to look all ways at once. He circled the
cattle and returned to the camp, feeling that
he must have the presence of the white man.
To be nearer Manson he dismounted; and,
sitting with his back against a tree, held
the horse by the bridle-rein and listened to
the cattle settling down —not as if reassured,
but in despair and resignation. Then Arunga
promptly fell asleep.
The old dog went half-way on the right
flank of the mob and then, discovering that
he had no man to direct him, returned to the
younger dog by the tent-fly, lay down with
his head between his paws and dozed.
A wind arose and blew in gusts and died
again. The air became moister and heavier
—as if it were laden, not with rain, but
mercury. There was a distant flash of light-
ning—a crepitation as of fire among dry
sticks and then a few great drops of rain fell.
The cattle were uneasy again. The white
man slept quietly; Arunga was dead to the
world. Suddenly the night was split in a
hundred lines as a window broken by a
stone. The terrified cattle were shown a
world of madness, lighted by the horror of
blue flame; a tremendous concussion shook
the firmament; the darkness was torn again
by lightning and the rain fell in torrents.
Then the stags of the herd—the big roan
piker and the black bullock and the big
strawberry—expressed all the madness of the
mob. With tails aloft and heads down, they
rushed through the lightning-broken night,
crashing into trees as if possessed of devils.
Manson awoke to the deluge, to lightning
so frequent and intimate that it was as if a
thousands hands in the darkness offered him
lightning-flashes at the length of walking-
sticks, to thunder so high that it seemed as
if a million drapers were tearing calico at
his ears. A great lightning flash showed
him the flat bare of cattle and Arunga still
asleep, though his horse had pulled away at
the alarm of the cattle. Between the thun-

ders Manson heard the crashing of the last
nf the mob through the trees of the plain.
Then he understood. The loss of the work
of a year out-stared and maddened him. He
went to Arunga and kicked that morose
shirker’s buttocks. Arunga awoke.
“I been tellem you keep watch—watchem
alonga. big roan piker, big feller black bul-
lock, big strawberry. Where all that one?”
“All that one,” replied Arunga—sulkily
but with absolute fidelity to facts —“all that
one been go all about.”
“By God! I give you all about,” said
Manson.
lie tore a stirrup-leather from his saddle,
and, holding the'iron, he beat Arunga with
the leather until rage left him. Then, throw-
ing down the leather and iron, the philoso-
phy of the bush, and his learning in the
University of Hard Places, came to help.
_ “No good till daybreak,” said Manson to
himself. “When there’s nothing else to do,
go to sleep.”
He lay back on his blankets and was soon
lost again to all that Hell of light and noise.
Arunga lay nursing his stripes, until he
heard Manson’s measured breathing. Then
he approached cautiously, though mad with
the lust to hurt. His eye-white flashed in
the lightning as he leaned over the sleeper;
and then, taking up the stirrup-iron by the
leather, he struck murderously at the top of
Manson’s head with the iron. Thrice he
struck and was answered by one groan.
Then came silence.
Arunga knew that he must take to the
bush and quickly ; and he was, furthermore,
afraid to stay with the dead man. Yet greed
delayed him. He lighted a fire under the
tent-fly, opened the pack-bags and took from
them all his soul had lusted for —the straw-
berry jam, a tin of kippered herring, a wedge
of cheese, biscuits and tobacco. It was not
blackfellow’s tobacco, the black sheep-wash
running 26 sticks to the pound, but the white
man’s tobacco—broad plugs of it, dark brown
and faded yellow. He took also a red ban-
dana neckerchief which he had desired for
these three days, and in it he wrapped his
spoil. Then he sighed relief and satisfaction
—and looked up.
By the firelight he saw a terrible Tiling
facing him—something blood-covered, in-
human, but indomitable to the death —Man-
son broken and frightfully injured, but
armed and ready. Arunga had scarcely a
moment to feel the paralysis of fear when
the revolver cracked, and he met Dead Finish.
The slayer fell forward fainting, but re-
covered himself. And then the unbeatable in
man began to do the impossible. His skull
had been broken by Arunga, but, half-con-
seiously, he felt thankful that the bone was
not pressing on the brain. He held his head
in both hands and tried to think. Then,
groaning, he crawled to the _pack-bag, took
out a towel and the bag of flour. He made a
thin paste of the flour and- the rain, soaked
the towel in it and wrapped the towel
around his head.
* * * *
“That’s the spirit,” said Judson. “Fine!
What endurance ! And then, I suppose, he
sat down and waited for somebody to come
up the track?”
“Not on your life, he didn’t!” replied
Walter Rossitter. “He crawled around till
he found his horse; and he rode a hundred
miles to hospital.”
“Incredible !”
“He did! The flour poultice kept him'
clear of losing any more blood; but I’m told
they had a terrible time cutting the towel off
in the hospital. It was baked hard by the
sun. They lifted the bones, and then found
they had to put a plate into the skull.”
“I see! The man with the silver head.
He’s a wonder.”
“The bush made him.”
“And that kid ahead is Arunga’s son?
Well, well! But he seems to worship Man-
son.” •
“He does! Let’s ask him about his father.”
They cantered to the little blackboy ahead.
He looked up at them and grinned.
“Cockroach,” said Walter Rossitter, “you
been likem Misser Manson?”
“Yowi —Marmi! I been like him alto-
gether.”
“Biit he kill your father, Cockroach.”
Cockroach laughed contemptuously.
“Yowi! He been killem father belongem
me altogether,” said Cockroach. “All right
that one! My father been very saucy feller.”
He jogged ahead again, leaving the white
men for their nearest view the skin
of Cockroach shining through the discre-
pancies of the waistcoat he wore upside
down and buttoned up the back.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
The Confessions of an Un-
frocked Solicitor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE telephone rang sharply, and it
proved to be O’Shaughnessy from
Fowl Flat.
“Is that yerself, Mr. Short—l’d loike to
see ye in the mornin’ widout fail. The
police was up here last noight and there’s
the divil to pay.”
Our district was no-license, and police
raids were as common as second mortgages,
and for me equally 'profitable.
I slipped out of the office and went over
to the police station. It was as well to get
the facts at once, as nothing would induce
the Fowl Flat stouthearts to speak the truth
even to their counsel, and I had often got
into court to find half the representations in
my brief quite untenable.
Our sergeant was unique for his job—a
cultivated Scotchman, pleasant and easy-
going, but liable to be duty-struck in mat-
ters connected with liquor. As far as Tau-
whata knew he was a total abstainer, but
he went occasionally . for a secret jag, and
once started it was terrific. Ilis wife used
to consult me when an outbreak occurred,
and the resulting task was not. light. I got
rattling good costs for many an easier one.
At the second day he would collapse and
creep to bed a white and shaken wreck.
He was in his office when I called, in good
trim, with every button shining. He was
reading the able report of Constable
Maloney, who had conducted the onslaught
oa Fowl Flat. Things seemed conclusive.
The constable had called in plain clothes,
found, a crowd of people in a closed room
drinking, and £1 Is. 6d. on the counter. The
“take,” three bottles of English beer and
eight bottles of whisky, stood on the ser-
geant’s table.
O’Shaughnessy called in the morning and
we went into committee. He was quick at
suggestion, and I mentioned the matter of
birthdays. He thought it might have been Pat
Kennedy’s, and would see him. Annyway,
the witnesses were all good and shtout, and
he would wire his brother, who could bring
“three rale good wans” from Christchurch.
Everything seemed promising but for the
ineffacable £1 Is. 6d. Wednesday was court-
day, and we arranged a dress rehearsal for
Tuesday, all hands to stay that night at
Archibald’s boarding-house, the only one in
Tauwhata where it was certain there was no
liquor. Mrs. Archibald’s face was a guaran-
tee of that.
I was mixing myself a nightcap on Mon-
day night when my front-door bell rang
shrilly. Mrs. Johnston, stout and comely,
stood there wringing her hands.
“O Mr. Short,” she said, “do come round
at once ; Mr. Johnston’s on it again.”
Johnston was in bed in a pitiable state —
glassy-eyed and speechless. His wife was
bewildered. She had kept her usual relent-
less watch on him, and —where had he got it?
I went outside to the station office. The
lock-up was wide open and the feet of Mick
Kelly,, the local drunk, poked across the step.
I folded him up and shut the door on his
snores. The office door was ajar, and the
light of my candle solved the problem in
quick time. On the table stood one bottle of
beer. Johnston had drunk the evidence!
The two first necessities now were to get
Johnston right by court-day and to replace
the whisky. I hunted about for an hour or
two to find if there was a cache anywhere.
He could hardly have drunk eight bottles
in one day, and he had a habit, once well
filled, of hiding bottles from the hostile eye
of his wife. The search was useless; he was
thorough in all he did.
The whisky of the take had been Dewar’s,
and I had none in stock. Robinson, of the
Imperial Boarding-house (late Imperial
Hotel), proved to keep Watson’s. I raided
every friendly place in the town, but my
total gathering by the day’s end was five
bottles of the right brand.
By Tuesday night, after a day’s unremitting
toil, Johnston was making progress, full to
the neck with bromide tablets. On the
morning of court day I found him in fair
shape, but quivering like an aspen. Now it
was my turn to put in some good work for
ray clients. The point of the diseussioin
was the £1 Is. 6d. At the dress-rehearsal
everything had gone, as O’Shaughnessy put
it, “as shmooth as a lady’s shmoile.” The
witnesses proved to be up to specifica-
tion, and a bona-fide celebration of Pat Ken-
nedy’s birthday was quite evidently all that
was happening when the ill-mannered con-
stable broke in.
I conferred at length with Johnston (see
bill of costs). I had lifted twenty-five
pounds from O’Shaughnessy for “general pur-
poses,” and under the circumstances reckoned
to bag all of it. After a deal of marching
and counter-marching the sergeant agreed to
leave out all mention of the £1 Is. 6d. from
his report, and if Constable Maloney forgot
all about it his own memory would also
prove to be bad. Pat Kennedy reckoned he
knew the mahn, and that it was aisy.
And so it was. The only untoward inci-
dent in the proceedings was O’Shaughnessy’s
explosive whisper to me when the sergeant
gravely produced the “take.” “Holy Moses!
but where’s the other three bottles?”
The Magistrate’s decision, “Information
dismissed,” fell on grateful ears.
While having a drink with me afterwards I
nqticed he congratulated me drily on the com-
petent manner in which I had handled the
matter.
EX-GENT.
The Ring.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
DOWN drifts a petal that the sun
Has wrinkled, like a woman's
When her brief budding time is dune
The satin-smooth of girlhood’s grace—
The green leaf’s shadow by your shoou
Is tesselated with the noon.
And soft as a moth’s flitting wing,
Grey in the grey of summer dusk,
Across your hand the branches fling
The shades of censers filled with musk;
Though when you look they must disclose f
Themselves but shadows of a rose !
To me you stretch that dimpled hand,
Its middle finger holds a ring,
A bloodstone in a golden band,
Red blooms in gardens green as Spring;
Or, in some pleasance grey with grief,
Spilled heart’s blood on an olive leaf.
I wonder why you wear that ring?
I wonder, yet scarce dare to aak,
So wide the doors of memory swing.
The pleasant noonday drops its mask,
The roses to a dirge sway slow.
Because a woman, long ago,
In a green garden much as this,
Beneath a trellis just as fair,
Paid out her life for one quick kiss
And one red blossom in her hair,
And where the soft brown tendrils cling,
Wore on her finger such a ring.
Stay where you are, O dimpled hands!
You cannot reach my heart’s shut door,
For by its latch another stands
r To guard its secret evermore.
You could not fill that place of gloom
Nor venture in that haunted room.
Down drifts a petal like a kiss ;
Half vexed, your eyes are turned from me ;
The noon dreams out its golden bliss,
Blue-aisled with summer’s witchery.
A nd to and fro leaf-shadows string
Above the mockery of a ring.
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORESET.
